Prospectus.
PROSPECTUS
— ol’
The Mnlgfoa Irrigation Company.
To he registered as a Limited Company, under the provisions of the Companies Act and the
Companies Act of 1888. ;
Capital: £500,000 in 500,000 Shares of £1 each.
375,000 Shares are now offered for public subscription, payable as follows :
ss. per Share on application,
ss. per Share on allotment;
the balance, if required, by calls of 2s. 6d. per Share, at intervals of not less than three months.
It is not anticipated that it will be necessary to call up tho balance.
' Provisional Committee:
GEORGE CHAFFEY, Esq. (Messrs. Chaffey Brothers), Mildura and Melbourne,
HENRY COPELAND, Esq., M.P., Newtown, Sydney.
J. R. CAREY, Esq. (Chairman Sydney Tramway and Omnibus Company)
Hon. GEO. H. COX, M.L.C., Warrigal Club, Sydney.
RICHARD R. TERRY, Esq., Ryde, Parramatta River.
ANDREW. S. LOW, Esq., Merrylands, Parramatta.

Dr. JOSEPH FOREMAN, Macquarie-street, Sydney.
J. K. CLEEVE, Esq., P.M., Penrith, G. W. R.

WILLIAM J. GREEN, Esq., Athenaeum Club, Sydney.
W. A. HUTCHINSON, Esq., Bond-street, Sydney.
ARTHUR W. STEPHEN, Esq. (Managing Director), Mulgoa and Sydney.
Dr. GEORGE MURRAY, Arrnidale, New England.
Tin; Directors shall be elected at the First Ordinary General Meeting, which will be held as soon as
convenient after the formation of the Company : in the meantime, the First Five Subscribers to the
Memorandum of Association shall be deemed for all purposes to be the Directors of the Company.

GEORGE CHAFFEY, Esq., C.E. (Messrs. Chaffey Brothers), Mildura and Melbourne.

133 PITT STREET, SYDNEY.
The main objects of the Company, briefly stated, are 1. To establish an Irrigation Colony at
Mulgoa, near Sydney, in the colony of New South Wales. 2. To purchase and acquire the rights and
privileges of the promoters, Messrs. George Chaffey and Henry Gorman, granted under the Act of
Parliament called the Mulgoa Irrigation Act. This Act was passed by the Parliament of New South
Wales in December, 1890, and enables and authorises Messrs. George Chaffey and Henry Gorman
“ to establish a system of irrigation and water supply within and adjacent to the police district of
Penrith, to acquire lands to establish irrigation works, to authorise the sale and supply of water for
irrigation and domestic use, and to construct, make, and lay dams, weirs, or floodgates upon or across
the Nepean and Warragamba Rivers, and for all other purposes which may be incidental thereto.”
And 3. To exercise the rights under the provisional contracts made by the promoters or on their behalf
with the owners of the lands proposed to be irrigated, and which are set forth hereunder,
The estates which in the first instance it is proposed to form into an Irrigation Colony (the first
in New South Wales), consisting of rich, fruit-growing, horticultural lands, suitable for the growth of
oranges, lemons, apricots, peaches, nectarines, vines, &c., in area about 20.900 acres in all, situated in
the County of Cumberland, 30 miles or thereabouts from Sydney, in tho parishes of Mulgoa, Chu-e-
-mont, Melville, and Rooty Hill, and extending to within a mile of the railway station of the pro-
sperous town of Penrith, and adjoimng St. Mary’s on the Great Western Line of Railway, form the
key to the whole country round Mulgoa. These can be irrigated from the Nepean and Warragamba
Rivers, and it is proposed to sell these lands in blocks of 5 and 10 acres and upwards, with water rights
attached. The actual cost of the land will be under £l2 per acre, and the time required for carrying
out the works so as to commence supplying water it is estimated will be within nine months. The
promoters confidently expect that these blocks, with the water rights, will realise a profit of from £25
to £5O per acre.
The genuineness of this enterprise and the practical skill of the Messrs. Chaffey (the head of which
firm, Mr. George Chaffey, C.E., will be the consulting engineer) are sufficient guarantees that this
great creative work, the first in New South Wales, will be carried out in a profitable way, and on a
scale not before attempted in this colony.
Messrs. Chaffey Brothers have successfully established irrigation colonies at Etiwanda and
Ontario, in California, where the lands improved by the growth of vineyards, orange and lemon
groves, &c., have been sold at from £250 to £3OO per acre.
Messrs. Chaffey Brothers have also successfully established, and are profitably developing on a
scale commensurate with the magnitude of the enterprise, the irrigation colonies of Mildura (in Vic-
toria, 500 miles from Melbourne) and Renmark (in South Australia, 250 miles from Adelaide).
The Vendors have agreed to transfer to the Company the valuable concessions, privileges, and
rights referred to, for the sum of £25,000, and 125,000 shares fully paid up ; they have, however, such
faith in the enterprise that they have agreed not to transfer 100,000 of the shares for a period of three
years from the incorporation of the Company.
The following contracts and agreements have been made, namely :
7th January, 1891. —Agreement, E. Standish Cox one part, and George Chaffey and Henry
Gorman other part.
7th January, 1891.—-Agreement, the Hon. George Henry Cox one part, and George Chaffey and
Henry Gorman other part.
10th January, 1891.—Agreement, Robert Marsden Fitzgerald, attorney of Elizabeth Rouse
Alston and William Evelyn Alston, one part, and George Chaffey and Henry Gorman
other part.
12th January, 1891.—Agreement Thomas Carnes Jamieson, William Charles Valentine Gibbes,
and Alfred George Gibbes, of one part, and George Chaffey and Henry Gorman other
part.
13th January, 1891. —Agreement, the Metropolitan Mutual Permanent Building and Invest-
ment Association, Limited, one part, and George Chaffey and Henry Gorman other part.
’ 14th January, 1891. —Agreement, Phillip Gidley King one part, and George Chaffey and Henry
Gorman other part.
20th January, 1891. —Agreement, Sir Daniel Cooper one part, and George Chaffey and Henry
Gorman, other part.
21st January, 1891. —Agreement, Arthur Winbourn Stephen one part, and George Chaffey and
Henry Gorman other part.
22nd January, 1891.—Agreement, Fitzwilliam Wentworth one part, and George Chaffey and
Henry Gorman other part.
28tb January, 1891.—Agreement, George Chaffey and Henry Gorman Ist part, William
Benjamin Chaffey, Elisha Clement de Garis, Robert William Hardie, and Arthurn Win-
bourne Stephen, 2nd part, and John Francis King (a trustee on behalf of the Company)
of the 3rd part.
The Memorandum and Articles of Association, and the contracts and agreements above men-
tioned, and the “ Mulgoa Irrigation Act ” above referred to, may be inspected at the offices of Messrs.
Dibbs and Gibson, Eldon Chamber's, 92 Pitt-street, Sydney.
A large number of shares have already been applied for, and the promoters have confidence in
stating that persons of moderate means, as well as large investors, will find, thin a lucrative investment.
The whole of the application money will be placed to the credit of the company, less the amount
to be paid to the vendors, and the usual charges for brokerage, advertising, printing fees, legal and
other expenses, &c., incurred in the formation of the company.
The Company will be registered as soon as 200,000 of the shares have been applied for.
Applications for shares should be made upon the accompanying form, and forwarded to the
brokers of the company, together with the amount payable on application. Where the number of
shares allotted is less than the number applied for, the surplus will be credited in reduction of the
amount payable on allotment.
Should no Allotment of shares be made, the amounts will be returned in full.
THE MULGOA IRRIGATION COMPANY.
To be registered as a Limited Company, under the Companies Act and the Companies Act of 1888.)

THE MULGOA IRRIGATION COMPANY.

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 21, 1891.
A Freetrade Manifesto.
The much-vaunted Freetrade Democratic
Association, which was to have shattered the
Protectionist policy of Victoria, and knocked
the fragments about, and thrown the small
dust of the old fiscal superstition into the
sea, still struggles painfully along and makes
very little impression. Now and then its
component parts meet in a dimly-lighted
hall, and make surprising assertions to each
other in the gloom, and read astonishing
statistics to each other off crumpled papers
which they haul out from among their
clothes ; and they also develope into mani-
festoes now and then, which precious docu-
ments the Association treasures up next to
its stomach. The following is a hunk from
the latest production perpetrated on behalf
of the society by one Gurry— a burning
and a shining light among the Freetrade
apostles :
Protection is all theory. What are the facts about its
influence upon wages ? The average wages in protected
Europe are 14s. per week. In Freetrade England the
average is 30s. Bd. This 30s. 8d in England goes as far as
555. a week here. Do our wages average 555. a week ?
Nothing like it. The paupers in England are lin 30
of the population. In protected France they are 1 in
18—nearly double. We have a duty of 20s. a pair on
axles for lorries, &c. The English price is 255. a pair, so
the duty is nearly 100 per cent. The men who make these
axles in Staffordshire earn £5 a week. 1 know this for a
fact. Yet we can’t make them at double the price. . .
The proof that Protection lowers wages is to be found in
the fact that every working man in this colony with a
family of four whose expenditure is £3 per week pays 12s.
per week in taxes on everything he buys. This is a reduc-
tion of 25 per cent in their wages.
The one drawback about this manifesto is
that the facts are wrong, and the figures are
infamous bosh, and the statements are howl-
ing ignorance, and the inferences are shriek-
ing insanity, and everything is degrading
stupidity except the commas. But it is such
a brilliant example of the sort of material
the average Foreign-trader travels on that
if is worth looking at.
# # •
The average wages in England, to begin
with, are not 30s. Bd. per week, but 20s. 6d.
The English artisan, it is true, is understood
to make 30s. Bd. per week, supposing he is
engaged all the year round—which fie cer-
tainly is not; but the nation never struck
such an all-round average since the world
began. And, as for the average wages in
Protected Europe, they are not known to
anybody, for the sufficient reason that at
least two countries have furnished no statis-
tics, and three or four more supply only the
most rudimentary guesses on the subject,
and, therefore, the Freetrade Democratic
Association might as well undertake to tell
what wages are paid in Saturn, or to give a
tip about horse-racing in the Polar-star.
But in one or two Protected countries the
average certainly is 14s. or thereabouts,
against 20s. 6d. in England, and at the same
time the entire cost of living, including food,
rent, clothes, drink, education, and taxes, is
Os. 9d. per head, as against Bs. 3d. in Free-
trade Britain ; and, therefore, the foreigner
might, if he spent only what is
theoretically or statistically necessary, save
in one year enough to keep him for 16
months, where the Englishman, on the high-
est estimate, could save enough to maintain
him for nine months only. These figures
are taken from Mulhall, the greatest living
authority on such subjects, and if the- Asso-
ciation knows more statistics than Mulhall
then it could give Mahomet points about the
Koran, and furnish Edison with information
on electric science, and supply Demosthenes
with a lift in the matter of Greek roots.
And as regards the pathetic query—“Do
our wages average 555. per week ?”—it is
only necessary to reply briefly that they
don’t, and that nobody ever said they did.
They average about 405,, and as any indi-
vidual can live as cheaply in Melbourne or
Sydney as in London, and more cheaply in
Auckland than any of the three, it is diffi-
cult to guess what the row is about. They
do not live as cheaply certainly, for the
regime of dry bread and pig-fat has not yet
been introduced in this country, but the
bread and the fat are both less expensive,
and a wooden cottage at Botany is as low-
priced as a home in a dismal slum at Hox-
ton, and rags cost about the same at both
ends of the earth, and dirt and broken
bottles and cheap boots and bad beer keep
on much the same level.
% # 0
The figures concerning pauperism, how-
ever, are a wilder, louder burst of menda-
city than all the rest of the manifesto put
together. The paupers of England are
correctly described as forming 1 in 30 of the
population ; the paupers of Paris are as 1 to
18 of that city’s inhabitants, but those of
France most assuredly are not, and the man
who professes to fit the average of one
crowded city to an entire country would
attempt to prove as readily—if it suited his
purpose —that because there was one murder
to the square mile in London on a certain
week, therefore the annual murders of
England would be arrived at by multiplying
the weeks and the square miles together—
result : three million homicides per annum.
The pauperism of the whole French nation
is about 1 in 26 of the entire population, a
result which is about 12 per cent, worse
than England. But in France the figures
represent the entire number who receive
even the smallest donation from any public
charity in the course of the year ; in
England they represent only the number of
paupers who are in receipt of relief on one
single day—generally the Ist of January.
And even these do not include “casual
poor,” who are numerous, and “ vagrants ”
who are past counting. When the
pauperism of England is estimated on the
same basis as that of France, it reaches the
ast.unding total of one in nine of the whole
English people ! and the Association which
can find an argument in favour of Freetrade
in these figures could also find thunder at
the bottom of the sea, and whales floating in
the atmosphere. The maintenance of the
innumerable paupers of England costs ss. Bd.
for every inhabitant ; those of France cost
Is. 6d. only, and the rest of Protected
Europe is run on an even cheaper basis.
* * *
The Freetrade citizen who makes axles at
£5 per week is hardly worth demolishing.
The middleman, who lives on axle-making,
certainly makes that amount, and the man
who does the work receives about 255. on a
steady average. He occupies exactly the
same position with regard to the prosperous
individual quoted by the Freetrade Associa-
tion as the discharged soldier with a wooden
leg and an extinguished eye who starves in
the workhouse does to the Duke of Cam-
bridge, and his position isn’t improving by
any means. The Freetrade litterateur who
howls in Melbourne “knows” his statements
“ for a fact,” and the present writer knows
his from personal experience, and because
he has been there, so the two are about
square. And concerning the Victorian
workman, who pays 12s. a week in taxes
out of an expenditure of £3, it would be a
gladsome thing if the Democratic Freetrade
Association would explain whom he pays it to.
For on that basis the entire Customs duties
of Victoria would amount to some nine
millions per annum, and as they only
amount to a little over two millions, there
is a ghastly discrepancy somewhere. And
as the Customs duties of Freetrade New
South Wales come to just about the same
amount, there is another awful discrepancy
in the argument, and as the Customs of
Freetrade England are the biggest in the
world, we arrive at the saddest discrepancy
of all, for Freetrade seems to be a trifle the
heavier tax of the two. Also, it would~be
pleasing to learn how 12s. comes to be 25
per cent, on £3, for this is also a heavy piece
of arithmetical shovelling ; and it would
make the world in general feel better if the
Association would show how the hypothe-
tical workman who doesn’t earn 555. a week
is to spend £3 anyh »w. ; and if, in addition,
it would expound the reason why all its
figures should have come out wrong without
one solitary exception, it would lift a heavy
load off the soul of many a tired calculator.
For a society that hasn’t even one solitary
fact belonging to it is a very weary thing
indeed.
* # #
Freetrade has made a good many bad
breaks at different times, but under the
guidance of the “ Democratic ” Association
it has not broken in the ordinary sense of
the term —it has burst, and exploded into
small dust. A society that is so full of
massive ignorance would solemnly argue
that the egg and the elephant were the same
sort of animal, because their names both
began with “e,” and, most likely, can’c find
its own feet without the assistance of a
diagram. It exists, apparently, by having
one man to collect the fiction and another
to distort the facts, and yet, surprising as its
information may be, its logic is a great deal
more amazing still. Here is a favourable
sample :
Neither can it be more than theory to say, without
giving instances and proofs, that when the duties were
imposed, workshops flourished. They could not have
flourished very much because they all want higher pro-
tection every year.
That about strikes the summit of human
derangement. It is a whole Himalaya of
idiocy and a Niagara of rushing drivel.
Protection must be bad because Victoria
wants more Protection every year, and
therefore we presume Freetrade would be
bad if the country wanted more of it every
year, and food is bad because the whole
human race wants it, and clothes and money
and boots are all objectionable for the same
reason. Any other alleged human being
would think that Victoria had found Pro-
tection rather a success else it wouldn’t
desire more of it, but, according to the Free-
trade Democratic Association, the fact that
Victoria wants Protection is the one over-
whelming circumstance which proves that it
doesn’t want Protection. Or, to express it
differently, the circumstance that the country
asks for Protection shows that it wants
Freetrade, and if it asked for Freetrade that
would also show that it wanted Freetrade,
and if it put a duty of 1800 per cent, upon
everything that would probably show that it
was absolutely howling for Freetrade.
Truly there are many different kinds of
logicians in this world, and some of them
have long ears, and go around on all-fours,
and argue chiefly with their hind legs.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Federal Conference.
The Federation Conference, from which such
great results are anticipated, meets next month,
and after that there will probably be a glorious
row. There will be about 30 delegates present,
and it is already sufficiently evident that 29 of
them will take one side in the discussion, and
Parkes will take the other, and after that lie
will spend the rest of his existence in telling
how 29 obstinate men wrecked the grandest
project ever heard of on earth. For out of the
vast resources of his inherent cussedness
Parkes has already resolved that the Federa-
tion must be built on the Canadian model, and
he has expressed this view over and over again
in letters and speeches ; and as no other pre-
sumably sane and responsible individual has a
desire to see the Canadian system reproduced in
Australia the old man’s foregone conclusion
must lead to trouble. The reason of his deter-
mination is easily understood. The scheme of
the Canadian Dominion is practically centralisa-
tion in its worst form. It extinguishes local
rights and local government, and its adoption
in Australia is simply the annexation to New
South Wales or to Victoria —whichever chances
to get uppermost—of South Australia, Queens-
land, West Australia, and Tasmania, and after
that it means either the annexation of Victoria
to New South Wales or of New South Wales
to Victoria, according as circumstances may
decide. It is very much the same system by
which England governs Ireland —the smaller
province has a due share of representation, but
as its government, down to the smallest detail,
is in the hands of the central legislature, it is
outvoted on every point, and cannot exercise
the slightest control over any single
matter relating to its own welfare. It
is a method which was invented in Canada
so that the English-speaking majority in one
colony might utterly wipe out the French-
speaking majority in another and deprive them
of any power to make their own laws or to
shape their own destinies; it was invented in
England so that Ireland and Scotland might
eacli have their share in a free Parliament, and
yet each should be as powerless to do anything
for itself as if it were a conquered country
governed by martial law ; and it is proposed
t hat it should be adopted in Australia because
New South Wales is the most populous state in
the group, and, consequently, Parkes believes
that it is possible to virtually annex all this
continent to New South Wales and rim the
show alone. And further, the old politician
suffers from the conviction that all New South
Wales is Parkes in the present, and, therefore,
all Australia will be Parkes in the future, and
he also labours under a vague impression that
almost all the habitable globe is Parkes in dis-
guise, and that he is the largest part of the
solar system. But the rest of the Australias have
had enough of centralisation, and the proposi-
■ tion that they should give themselves away
gratis for Parkes’ amusement has no attrac-
tions. The Federation they want is a union on
the plan of the United States, which gives to
the smallest member of the league the same
power over its own internal affairs as is
possessed by the largest, and not a combination
by which one or two provinces are to own Aus-
tralia, while the others cannot assert their right
to make even a drain or a railway-crossing on
their own responsibility, except by insurrection.
And as this latter is the only sort of Federation
that Parkes cares alamt, the dissonant voice is
likely to be heard at the conference complain-
ing in accents of anger and indignation that all
the regiment is out of step excepting only
Private Murphy —that individual being him-
self.
An Australian Interest.
Nothing but hard bungling on one side and
hard, energetic shooting on the other prevented
the establishment the other day of another
European republic, and the abolition of another
European king. For several years past the
Portuguese have suffered from an aching desire
to throw overboard their little shrivelled-up
potentate, with all his relatives, and his regalia,
and his other utensils of monarchy, and a few
days ago they just missed their chance by a
hair’s-breadth. The British Government lent
its moral influence to prop up the miserable
bankrupt swindle that reigns beside the Tagus,
because the Tory party loathes and abhors
Republicanism, and grovels to Monarchy no
matter how mean it may be, and for the
same reason that it habitually insults France
and abases itself before Germany ; but, despite
British influence, King Carlos’ crown will go,
almost to a certainty, at the next shot,
Portugal hasn’t found Monarchy a success.
Under its control the country has sunk into a
miserable little patch of sores and sin and
ignorance, and has acquired the biggest debt
per head in all Europe; and the army has
degenerated into a bug-bitten mob, armed with
rusty swords and burnt-out stove-pipes, and
the treasury is an 10 U stowed away in a dis-
used boiler, and the navy consists chiefly of one
old scow, which is being eaten away by worms
on a sand-bank, and there is nothing left to beg
or borrow or steal or confiscate from north to
south and from east to west in all the mouldered,
moth-devoured kingdom of the Braganzas.
That is to say, there is nothing but priests, and
nobody wants to confiscate them, for priests are
more common in Portugal than mud. It is
hardly possible to swing an umbrella loosely
in any part of the Portuguese territory
without afterwards finding the eye of some
holy father stuck on the end of it, and
the individual who assaults six inhabi-
tants in the dark is sure to be arrested
next morning for smashing four preachers, one
soldier, and a tax-gatherer. As for the people
in general, they work 14 hours a day and live
on nothing in particular a week, and the
amount they don’t know is enormous, for there
is not enough education in the country to teach
the average Portuguese to know his own feet
when he sees them. The Braganzas have
systematically spent money and smothered
education and boomed the Pope, and they have
also been celebrated in another way, for they
have bolted faster and further, whenever an
enemy was in sight, than any other batch of
monarchs in Europe. When Portugal is in a
tight place the private soldier—the individual
who is always kicked and never paid— is sent
to the front, and the officers shove him on from
behind, and the commander-in-chief escapes
into a tree, and the king dives down inoo a hole
and remains there till times improve. When
Spain and Portugal both get rid of their kings
the two countries will probably unite and form
,a great and progressive nation, but they have
been kept apart because neither of the kings
would give up his miserable job, and so they
have quarrelled and fought and bombarded
each other’s cities for the sake of two knock-
kneed monarchs with fallen-in stomachs, who
preferred to see their subjects quarrel and
minder each other rather than run the risk of a
coalition which would make one of them'un-
necessary. For the last 70 or 80 years Britain
lias undertaken to back up the Portuguese
monarch against all comers, and consequently
Australia, as part of the British Empire, has a
vested interest in the ignorant little spectre
that Portugal is so anxious to get rid of. And,
taking him all round, he is indeed a pleasant
object for any democratic community to have
an interest in.
Pro-trader Parkes the Free-tectionist.
Premier Parkes is, of course, absolutely loyal
to Freetrade. He is not, therefore, merely
trying to bulldoze the New South Wales Pro-
tectionist party by conditionally offering a con-
tract of 175,000 tons of steel rails to any
capitalists who will locally start the iron
industry; he is only expanding Freetrade
principles on wider curves and broadening him-
self down from precedent to precedent. Simi-
larly, the S.M. Herald, organ of the importers’
gang and Cos., is only stupefied by its own
smoke, and is not Parkes’ conscienceless hench-
man when it thus challenges ridicule (italics
ours): —
The policy of the New South Wales Government in
offering a large order on terms which entailed an increased
expenditure, though not touch an excess us would out-
weigh the collateral advantages of local production, was
not a policy of Protection.
Of course, it wasn’t. And an Imperial Governor,
at £7OOO a year and perks, is not a representa-
tive of Queen Victoria, but merely a “ citizen
of New South Wales.” And a man sent
to gaol for life is not imprisoned at all;
he merely voluntarily retires from publicity.
And the Sydney Morning Herald is not
a capitalistic paper, but labours wholly
and solely in the cause of the wage-earner.
What is the Herald giving us? The very
essence of Freetrade is “to buy in the cheapest
market,” and unless the Government of New
South Wales is prepared to submit its 175,000-
tons steel-rail contract to “ Dutch auction ”
open to all the world, without any conditions,
save as to quality, price, and time of delivery,
they have utterly departed from Freetrade
ethics and have adopted a Protectionist policy.
The Herald sees this all right enough, and
consequently it wriggles thus :
And no Freetrader who was not also a doctrinaire or a
pedant would cavil at it.
The Bulletin here merely remarks that no
Freetrader worthy of the name exists who is
not both a doctrinaire and a pedant.
The M‘Kinley Tariff.
The recent struggle over the M‘Kini,ky Tariff
is hailed by the Australian Foreign-traders as
the greatest Freetradc,triuinph since the Irish
Famine, rather than the logic of Cobden and the
eloquence of Bright, forced Peel to repeal the
Corn Laws. The last mail, however, brings
authentic information on this point. Goldwin
Smith was some years ago considered one of the
most prominent publicists in England. He
chose, however, to accept a professorship in an
American University, and afterwards drifted
into Canadian politics. Naturally he has since
been regarded as an authority on Transatlantic
affairs. Writing in the January number of
Macmillan’s Magazine on the M'Kinley
ft


Tariff, the Professor, though an enthusiastic
I reetrader, is careful to point out that the
recent Democratic victory was a declaration

The political world of Victoria—which com-
prises some two or three hundred souls, and a
few newspapers without souls—is more or less
agitated by the behaviour of Mr. £ ‘ Righteous ”
M rixon, one of the most wearisome members
of the late Gillies picnic-party. Wrixon, in
his former capacity of Attorney-General, was
elected (chiefly by himself) to act as a Victorian
delegate at the Federal Convention, but now
that he has reverted to his primeval state, and
possesses no capacity at all, the voice of the
country is understood to be calling upon this
uninteresting person to retire from the hurly-
burly and get lost amongst memories of the
past. In truth the political personality of
Wrixon, such as it was, died with the Coalition
Government. When he left off being “ chief
Law Officer of the Crown ” he became the hus-
band of Money Miller’s daughter—merely
that, and nothing more, his solitary achieve-
ment in Parliament having been a Puritanical
Bill for inciting people to suicide on the
Sabbath Day. Wrixon, in a word, goes to the
impending Convention as the ghost of a
deceased Attorney-General wearing the shoes
that properly belong to his successor, Shiels.
Not being aware that all the sawdust
dropped out of him some months ago, Wrixon
has been collecting Federation platitudes in
London with a view to making his hearers feel
more tired than usual when he proceeds to inter-
pose his spectral “spoke” at Sydney. This
seems particularly unkind on that gentleman’s
part. The Convention could put up with
Wrixon if he confined himself to opening the
proceedings with prayer, and relieving the
monotony with an occasional “ hear, hear !”
like a voice from the tomb. But it seems
almost certain that he intends to hang on to
this last chance of becoming a public nuisance
until the burden is no longer supportable, and
the chief reason for the proposal to exclude
reporters from next month’s cackle is an
earnest official desire that Wrixon may be
deposited outside on the mat with the strictest
Secrecy.
“ Augustus M.” writes us thus
Dear Bulletin,— A man like Carnegie, who is endea
vouring to do good, and in the manner that seems to him
the most advisable, instead of being commended for his
efforts, finds himself the object of much adverse criticism,
while Queen Victoria and other Royalist millionaires
hoard up their wealth and escape the Hood of opprobrium
against which that Republican has to contend. If, then,
this is to he the penalty for doing good, those men who
have acquired wealth by their brains would do well to
follow’ the selfish example of Queen Victoria, against
whom Sydney Daily Telegraph does not employ the
weapons of adverse criticism. It might be said that
Carnegie made his money by the sweat and toil of his
men. To some extent this is true, yet he is a benefactor,
nevertheless, for his commercial genius has found em-
ployment for thousands of men who might otherwise
have been workless. On the other hand, Victoria’s
millions (and she is far richer than Carnegie) are taken
from the industrious taxpayer, to whom she has never
given anything, and on whose shoulders she sits an
intolerable burden.
Viewed from the standpoint of present con-
ditions, Carnegie’s conduct is admirable ;
viewed from the standpoint of what should be,
it is something less than just. The unfortunate
thing is that the D.T., like many loose thinkers,
takes opportunity to administer praise or blame
from both points of vantage, as occasion suits.
It is a standing rule in the British National
Portrait Gallery that no portrait shall be
received unless the person it represents has
been dead not less than ten years. There is
the germ of a good idea in this regulation, and
if the Australians would only pass a law that
no statue shall be raised in any public place,
and no portrait hung in any public institution,
until the subject has been buried for some
period varying from a decade to a quarter of a
century, they would save a great deal of drivel
•and absurdity thereby. Pretty nearly every-
body can be a great man while he is alive, if lie
■can make it worth .while for a few newspapers
to boom him persistently, or can get the Orange
lodges or the Hibernian Society, or the
A.N.A., or all of them, to advertise him with
energy, but if his memory will not keep fresh
for 25 years after he is gone, then he is only an
empty delusion after all. By fixing such a limit
as this only a great man or a great fraud would
be handed down in enduring bronze to future
generations ; it is impossible to draw a line
which will exclude all the world’s dismal im-
postors, for many of them are very hard to
extinguish, but it is easy to sift out 90 per cent,
of the characters whose images are only cal-
culated to make the human race feel tired in
years to come. The world very seldom knows
its great men while they are alive. A century
ago it didn’t feel sure whether George Wash-
ington or George HI. was likely to last the
longer in history ; it imagined at one time that
>I A mes I. w r as n lore important than S ll akes r e a re,
and that Louis XV. amounted to a great deal,
and Voltaire to very little. And as it generally
rushed to put up a statue to the ass of the
moment, before it had time to take stock of his
ears, it happens that we fall over the image of
some forgotten fool at every corner, and crowd
<ip everywhere against the painted semblance
of some extinct king or some useless local
cheesemonger or glorified slaughterman, who is
allowed to insult the human intelligence merely
because his admirers wouldn’t wait to see if he
was worth preserving.
As to the attendance at a Queensberry glove-
fight of Dan Blank, New South Whiles
Minister of the Crown, and Mr. Money Fisher,
stipendiary magistrate, we may remark that as
such affairs are not illegal in the opinion of
the. local Court to which the question was
recently submitted, the attendance at a glove-
fight of a Minister and a magistrate is merely a
question of taste. The notoriety - hunting
Dan Blank has to be in evidence at every-
thing, and it does not follow that because Mr.
Money Fisher is a paid magistrate he should
be debarred from mixing himself up with a
popular sport. Though the law may have
decided in an opposite direction, there can be
no earthly doubt that the Goddard-0 hoy n ski
affair ivas a first-class prize-fight, and nothing
else ; and it has to be remembered that the
decision of the Court which upheld the legality
of Queknsrekry glove-fights was given by Mr.
Money Fisher himself ! That is where the
question of good taste comes in with regard to
Mr. Money Fisher, who surely cannot be an
absolutely impartial judge as to the morality or
immorality of an amusement which he con-
fessedly likes.
The trail of the flunkey and the toad-eater is
everywhere. About the most amusing thing
in this world is the lofty scorn with which
“General ” Booth declares he will never let any
“common person” be associated with him in
charge of the Salvation funds, but if he accepts
anybody at all as a colleague it will be, he says,
an earl, a duke, or the Prince of Wales. Poor
old bogus Christian and bogus democrat ! The
man who talks of God as if he went out and
drank with Him every night and they lurched
home together and were equally mixed up about
finding the keyhole, and who yarns as affably
about the Redeemer as if they lived at the same
hash-temple, and who seems to have been on
terms with all the prophets, and suffers from
an incurable tendency to allude to the patriarchs
as Abe and Ike and Mo from sheer familiarity,
snuffles abjectly in the presence of a duke as
any ordinary sinner. He is so intimate with
Providence that he is more than half inclined
to allude to it as “Old P.” for the sake of
brevity, and when he gets into Paradise he will
probably pinch Peter at the gate, but his
humble reverence for the Prince of Wales re-
mains unimpaired.
When the Foreign-trade statistician starts to
demonstrate the prosperity of England under
his favourite system he always points with
unspeakable satisfaction to the decline of
pauperism, and strikes a paralytic attitude like
Ajax defying the lightning when he knew
there wasn’t any of it about. How pauperism
does decline in England was demonstrated one
day a few weeks ago. The Mile-End Board of
Guardians, at a single sitting, was staggered by
a wholesale application to admit into the work-
house 200 families who—in that one district—
were out of work, penniless, starving, without
furniture, almost without clothes, and many of
them homeless. It seemed for a moment as if
pauperism was about to increase in England at
a most alarming rate, but the Board was equal
to the occasion ; the chairman explained that he
couldn’t admit them to the workhouse because
there was no room, and he couldn’t give them
outside relief for the law wouldn’t allow it, and
thus the prosperity of England was saved from
sudden annihilation. A pauper is understood
to be a person who lives on public charity, and
if the public won’t give any charity it is evi-
dent that pauperism must diminish. If there
were ten starving citizens in Mile-End, say 50
years ago, and they all received public assist-
ance, there was a certain well-defined amount
of pauperism, and if there are 1800 to-day and
only five of them receive assistance, then the
great evil of poverty has decreased 50 per cent.
In fact, if nobody cared a curse about anybody,
then there would be no pauperism at all, and
just here it occurs to us that hell must be a
prosperous place according to Freetrade logic,
for we are willing to bet the office harp and the
business-manager’s halo that nobody lends a
helping hand to anybody down there.
Some day or other the Australian Natives
Association may possibly realise that there is
something humorous and grotesque about its
existence, and when it begins to understand
itself it will crack up ignominiously. There
must be a dreadful scarcity of humour about
the born Australian, else the “ Natives ” would
have died very young. The man who attempted
to get up a French Natives Association in Paris
—thus admitting that they were only living on
sufferance in a foreign land—or a league of
British natives in Birmingham, would have
to escape in disguise in order to get away
from public ridicule ; but the Australian
solemnly gathers himself together, day after
day, to celebrate the astonishing fact that he
was born in his own country, and it never
occurs to him that almost everybody else is
born in his own country likewise, so that the
fact over which he whoops is more common
than grass, and a great deal less surprising than
damp water. The “ Native,” in fact, raises a
monument to commemorate the astounding cir-
cumstance that he is himself, instead of being
some other fellow, and that he was created
where lie was instead of being created in some
other place, and it doesn’t dawn on his compre-
hension that every other human being on all
the earth is in exactly the same situation, and
that everybody ought to whoop and celebrate
and commemorate and let off fireworks for
exactly the same reason. If the Australian
Native had been born in Europe, or if he was a
black Frenchman of Irish parents who came
from Germany and he had first seen the light
in Greece, there might be something sufficiently
surprising about it to be worth screaming over,
but the man who organises himself simply
because he is the son of his own parents and
was born in the country that he belongs to (and
that should belong to him even if it doesn’t
happen to do so) is exciting himself in a way
that is quite unnecessary.
Dear Bulletin, — You say the honorary magis-
trate system is rotten. Agreed. The stipen-
diary system, not properly supervised, is
equally bad. Some few years ago a N.S. Wales
Stipendiary Magistrate was holding Court
(liquored up, as usual). The case before him was
drunkenness, and, on the prisoner pleading “ I
s’pose 1 was drunk,” his Worship serenely pro-
ceeded to pass the sentence of death. Although
remonstrated with, he adhered to the sentence,
and ordered the prisoner to lie removed. When
a criminal case was to be tried, he generally
held Court in the open air—so that the people
might avail themselves of the treat, he said.
Sitting on his own verandah, he would pass
judgment, usually whilst in a state of inebria-
tion. Eventually the thing became so palpable
that steps were taken which resulted in his
removal to another town as 0.P.5., which
position he held till quite recently, when death
claimed him at the end of a prolonged drinking-
bout. ll. W.

Some weeks ago Justice Williams (Vic.) sen-
tenced a man to live years’ penal servitude and
two Hoggings for an assault upon a woman.
The evidence tendered was that of the woman
only. Enquiry made by the Minister for Jus-
tice into the character of the prosecutrix has
resulted in the hoggings being remitted and the
five years reduced to one. Now, the woman
was either a consenting party or she was not.
If she was, the prisoner is legally altogether
guiltless and should be set free, as was Buttner
on the day hxed for his execution at Sydney ;
if she was not, he is altogether guilty, and
should be kept in gaol for the term of his sen-
tence, for the complaint is not that the sentence
was excessive or unusual, but that the man was
not guilty. In the present case, the woman is
proved to have gone in a cab with the accused
to the house where the assault muxs said to have
been committed, and only made complaint when
challenged for being out late at night. The
man’s detention is a sop thrown to the Law (so
that it shall not appear an utter idiot) and to
Mrs. Grundy.
—_—
Professor Marshall Hall has induced Mel’
bourne University Council to endorse his sugges
tion that nobody can take the degree of
Bachelor of Music in Melbourne until he has
first passed an examination in German —an
exam, so rigid that a student must be able to
read German and translate it into English at
sight. Why not Italian and French as well ?
This broad idea might be still further widened.
Let Melbourne University refuse to grant
diplomas to medical men w'ho have not pre-
viously acquired the degree of Bachelor of
Trapeze at a licensed gymnasium. A legal
student again might be required to first graduate
Master of “Sh duffing ” under a certificated
Chinese cook before being granted a permit to
study for the Bar. Young men proposing to
qualify as architects might be compelled to pass
a rigid exam, in swimming. Similarly, intend-
ing civil engineers might be made to qualify
first in midwifery, and embryonic clergymen in
book-keeping by double (and every other church)
entry. In fact, the field is illimitable.
The Bulletin can understand the proposal to
erect a statue in Post-office Place, Sydney, to
the memory of that robust man, the late Chief
Justice Martin, though it fails to see any other
reason for christening the Place in question
“ Martin ’’-place (instead of Wentworth-place)
than that the name of Wentworth is already
sufficiently commemorated on the map of the
province. But a statue to “ Slippery-CiiARLiE ”
Cowper —bah ! Cowper was a most common-
place person, and the only reason for raising a
statue to him at the public expense can be that
it is desired to prevent his memory from slip-
ping into utter oblivion, like that of many
another small and hopelessly prosaic politician.
The rule should lie that the memory shall
advertise the statue, and not the statue adver-
tise the memory. We don’t think much of a
system wdiich practically enables a single in-
dividual to put his hand into the public
Treasury and pull out enough money to buy
brass in order that effigies of his political
associates and contemporaries may be adver-
tised to a world which was likely to forget that
they had ever lived. We have read and heard
a lot about the late Mr. Cowper, and yet we
only know one remarkable thing about him :
when pestered by a billet-hunter he would put
his skinny hand into his coat-tail pocket and
produce a bundle of papers. “ Look here, my
good man, I can give you nothing,” he would
say. “ See ! I have even got 50 applications
for the assistant-hangmanship.”

Dear Bulletin, — It is obvious that among
those institutions which have survived the pur-
poses for which they were created must now be
reckoned Trades-Unionism. Man being, how-
ever, in most cases, a mere slave to custom,
cherishes any effete legacy of the past, and
rarely exercises the slightest thought as to its
desirability, wherefore we find the world defer-
ring to grossly inapplicable laws. In many cases,
by the time that a long-desired reform is
actually accomplished it is only half useful and
the benefit is trivial owing to the difference of
surrounding circumstances in society at various
intervals. The accumulation of coppers by the
masses into what they term a defence fund is
too puerile to need comment. Now that Capital
has organised its forces, Trades-Unionism
perishes as 'an active power for benefiting its
votaries. Its sole nse will now be that it will
serve as a kind of club where all workers may
meet and discuss affairs relating to themselves.
The Ballot-box will in future be the weapon of
the proletarian, and now and again where
aggressive Capital drives too far a little healthy
anarchism will keep the balance from toppling
over. —Junor.
Melbourne Argus recently assumed the role
of a pitying critic towards “the deplorable
excesses and political instability of revolution-
ary South American States. Commercial specu-
lation is seriously affected, and the insecurity
of capital militates materially against the hap-
piness and industry of these foolish Repub-
licans.” We are not altogether so sure about
this. In an outsider who has never enjoyed the
intoxicating pleasure of repudiating a long and
burdensome foreign loan, originally incurred for
sinister purposes, an inability to appreciate its
exhilarating effects may be pardonable. Then
the sense of equality and brotherhood which
fluctuating individual successes and reverses
engender is eminently calculated to repress any
undue exhibitions of “ side ” or highhandedness
in the powers of the day. The knowledge that
to-morrow a party may break into your bed-
room, trot you out into the plaza, make a rifle-
target of your person, and appoint one of their
number to take over your billet with all emolu j
nients and privileges pertaining thereto, induces
a spirit of toleration and kinship with the rank-
and-file of society which is quite unknown in
more sedate constitutional countries. Then,
again, the splendid opportunity which a period!
cal riot affords for wiping out old grievances dl
oppressive debts more than compensates the
temporary inconvenience of remaining unem-
ployed while business operations are suspended,
further, the upshot of each revolution is that
some crying abuse is remedied, and although a
deal of noise is created and a few reckless
individuals go under, it is better that things
should take this course than that a nation
should have to wait for a couple of centuries
before an evil is constitutionally suppressed
rew guns or swords decay from rust in South
America, and it is some consolation to know
that the national funds are not wasted on use-
less toys. There are many other favourable
features in connection with this subject, but
the main principle underlying the lot is that
sometimes even the most violent convulsions
are infinitely preferable to mouldering stag-
nation,

The case of the clergyman lately brought
before the Melbourne bench charged with
stealing a novel from the Public Library and
discharged on account of his evident respecta-
bility, like that of several other genteel
defendants in similar case and similar
luck, evokes certain reflections. If it be
assumed as an incontrovertible axiom that a
professional man, although not in the best of
pecuniary circumstances, cannot be held actuated
by felonious intent in such a case, then the general
public is entitled to know which are the voca-
tions which will ensure immunity from prose-
cution in the event of their feeling inclined to
borrow other people's property for an indefinite
period. There can be little doubt that the
reverend one had no idea of stealing the giddy
Volume iu question, but where the decision
given in his case is open to serious objection is
that the bench apparently exonerated him from
blame solely because he was a professional man.
If this reasoning is to be accepted as a basis for
precedent, the lawyer, soldier, doctor, sailor or
civil-service employe must also claim exemption
from legal control; and as this is a democratic
country, not only will the heads of the various
professions demand this right, but also the
lowest members of these social departments.
The next hank-clerk who borrows a couple of
thousand out of the office safe will calmly assert
his non-culpability. Being a member of the
banking profession, how can anyone hold him
guilty of felonious intent so long as his people
refund his defalcations ?
Tkuth (London) has been ridiculing the names
bestowed by Administrator Macgregor on the
prominent physical features of New Guinea.
Truth is right, though Macgregor has an
apologist in Sydney in the person of another
unimaginative Scotchman who runs the local
Echo. For instance, Mac. has christened a
huge New Guinea mountain “Mount Foshery ”
—Fosbery happening to he the name of a
Sydney police-officer who chances to be a
pal of his. He discovers in a mysteri-
ous country a colossal mountain which
dates from the beginning of time—and he
straightway names it after an offensive police-
man who watches the Sydney publichouses to
see that tlie public don’t buy a drink on Sunday.
We are as sorry for Mount Foshery as we are
for the African Albert Edward Nyanza, the
mighty lake which, after it had defied for
scores of centuries the curiosity of man, hook-
canvasser Stanley profaned by making it the
godchild of a coarse, squat German man with a
big stomach and a bald head,
W. E. Smith, the wholesale manufactur-
ing stationer, of Bridge-street, Sydney, has ex-
tended operations as manufacturing stationer and
printer to Hunter-street, Newcastle.

Mr. Zichy Woinarski, who died the other
day in Melbourne, was one of the heroes of the
Hungarian war of independence in 1818, and
fought in eighteen battles during that much-
blundered outburst. Like Kossuth he was ap
ardent Republican. The Hungarians in these
days had a very wild idea of liberty. The poor
weak Austrian Emperor conceded everything
they asked for themselves, and then they rose in
frenzied insurrection to defend their inalienable
privilege of jumping on the Croats with both feet,
because they couldn’t understand that a Croat
wanted anything at all. Nine-tenths of their
army consisted of noblemen and their hangers-on,
who were lighting for their divine right of paying
no taxes, and owning all the land, and kicking
everybody who wasn’t a nobleman, and the other
tenth consisted of Republicans who would probably
have been hung afterwards by the bosses of the
nation for suggesting that a person who -wasn’t a
peer had a right to do anything but grovel on all
fours. They were led by two of the beet com-
manders on earth, but one of them, Georgei, was
an aristocrat who turned traitor ; and the other,
Bern, was a Republican who was looked down
upon by the Magyars because he was only a
beastly Pole, and consequently, whenever things
looked absolutely hojHiless, or wherever the
tattered insurgents were being borne down by
stupendous odds, they put Bern in front, and felt
bad because that objectionable democrat wasn’t
killed. Ami finally, when the great chance was
fooled away, 11 ay nan proceeded jp flog the
Magyar potentates and their wives and daughters
in a lavish way, because he didn’t respect a
Hungarian nobleman worth a cent, and had no
regard for any man of pedigree except himself.

848 Maoquarie-street North, Sydney, Feb. 18,1891.
Mv Dear Mooradinda, —
The Countess of Jersey—the real official representative
of Her Majesty (and, when you come to think of it, who
can represent a woman as well as a woman ?)—has reached
Australia at last, and, though she must glide into Govern-
ment House in as “ penny plain ” a manner as possible,
according to the statoot, still there will be a triumphal
arch at AlDury and a gaping throng at Redforn to assure
her — well, that sometimes the tail does wag the dog.
Lady Jersey seems to be in no hurry to reach Sydney, for
she will stay' at Melbourne Government House and will
come on just when the whim seizes her. At least, that is
her reported progress, which is probably set forth to main-
tain the dreary fiction that it doesn’t matter when a
governor’s wife arrives, though why she should come
full tilt from Adelaide per express isn’t explained. The
dutiful Earl will meet her and the four scions, and a short
visit will be paid to Sutton Forrest, just to enable Lord
Jersey to break some of the horrors of the situation to his
haughty Primrose dame. Captain Leigh—Lady Jersey’s
brother—will accompany the Countess from Melbourne.
It doesn’t seem as if Lord Jersey appreciates high-class
music. Though he went to the “Dick Whittington”
pantomime, which was starred with “Little Annie
Rooney,” et hoc genus omne, he did not patronise the
“ Golden Legend ” by the Philharmonicans, and now he
has refused to attend a classical concert given in the
big Town Hall by leading Sydney musicians. If Jersey
is going to play the invisible Tory he will have to begin on
the shingles of society.
It is an interesting sight to see how a certain adipose
professional, whose waist is a Sabbath-day’s journey from
hook to hook, takes care to engage a supe. who is larger
and fatter than herself, and whose billet is principally to
overshadow the leading lady'; the latter, of course, doesn’t
suffer in consequence. The actress’s bane is adipose
tissue—and yet the scrag who turns up a neck like a
duck’s hind leg, and whose bared arms suggest gas-pipes
at a hundred yards is enough to damp the spirits of the
entire audience. Still they all like to he slim ; as the
frockist says, “ You can add what you like, ma’am, but
you cannot take away.” Why don’t those Thespiennes
give up their oysters and porter?
The Earl of Jersey flits to Bowral on 21st inst. to open
the Bowral and Moss Vale Club—the roll-book of which
includes the names of several Sydney gentlemen who
abide in the Doctors’ Paradise half the year. There is a
hope that Lady Jersey may accompany her liege on thi 8
occasion.
It will soon become fashionable for men to go to church
if Lord Jersey maintains such a brisk attendance at the
sanctuaries as he is keeping up now. Once in the week
isn’t enough for the Earl, who went to the Cathedral in
great state on Ash Wednesday. But the real test is what
did his Excellency dine upon when he reached home?
And did his religious fervour extend to cold fish and pan-
cakes ?
“ Mrs. Yell and infant ” appear ominously in the list of
passengers by an outgoing English mail-boat, but wouldn’t
it have been more in accordance with the eternal fitness
if they had entered it “Mrs. and Infant Yell?” The same
boat has the honour of carrying a lady hilled as “ Miss
Blank Blank, nurse, and infant.” This is only one of the
penalties a good-natured girl pays when she undertakes
to chaperone her youngest niece aross the mill-pond.
There is a sound as of a long, loud shriek of female
indignation among the lady candidates for Inland China
honours, a parcel of whom we shipped off to Melbourne
the other day to be judged—it was understood—purely
on their theological merits by the Melbourne examiners.
Scarcely had they arrived at the wharf than two Anglican
clerics sailed up the gangway, and, without putting any-
body over their biblical jumps, so to speak, selected one
young lady and bore her off in triumph, to the amazement
of the rest. The examinations were a farce throughout,
and nobody was surprised when the favoured one came
out apostolically on top, with the rest of the would-be-
elect nowhere.
Here is an advertisement calculated to take away the
breath of the serious young lady, whose only earthly
ambition is to be annexed by a beauteous curate, with

Up to date, it has only been “Clergyman’s widow” who
has had to scratch for herself (since the common or garden
Rev. Samuel Smiffkins never leaves enough behind him to
keep his family in salt) but now it seems as if the support-
ing pow’ers of the third sex aren’t built to last a lifetime.
Let the anxious enquirer who is at present engaged on a
pair of woolwork braces for the new curate assimilate this
dismal fact before she draws another thread.
Smuggling is an expensive wickedness when you are
caught/ An unlucky Melbourne draper was recently
made to forfeit a bundle of Maltese lace, valued at ever
so much, with a further fine of three times the amount of
duty, not to speak of the “ professional” agony of seeing
a bundle of real lace confiscated by the Customs House
bogie-man. And only the other day a well-known ex-
rink-nianager was gone through at Wodonga and relieved
of four gold watches to the great amusement of the other
passengers.
But the boss smugglists are the stewardesses. One of
them has a “ system ” of bringing into Victoria a contra-
band cargo of feathers and lace every trip which she
disposes of to a loyal clientele, who wouldn’t give her
away for the world; and as she occasionally vaiF„
the monotony with cigars, the easy-going hus-
bands affect to laugh at the whole thing, you knew, and
get their smokes at sacrifice rates. Many and ingenious
are the ways in which she gets her smuggles ashore
One day she was ushered into a drawing-room, neatly
gloved and bonnetted, with not a vestige of parcel any-
where. “I hope you’ve brought me something nice,”
said the lady of the house, and then the unexpected
happened. Off came her bonnet, and some webby' lace-
handkerchiefs came out from between the lining and the
crown, and off came the Jaced-up boots, revealing a
rather flat instep after two soft parcels had been
taken out and unwound into yards of enchanting lace.
A cold-hungry time is setting in for wedding-guests,
and the visitor who has blossomed forth in a big cheque
and a piano is turned loose upon a “buffet ” covered with
sticky' “delicacies,” and sometimes not even that—the
refreshment consisting of the reception the happy pair
hold after the return from church, with, perhaps, a
dubious sandwich thrown in, since sit-down breakfasts
are considered bad form. Some people furnish the dreary
stand-up feed with a certain amount of toothsomeness,
but this is not an era when your hostess goes to any
greater expense than she need, and the leathern custard-
ball, the limp coffee, and the menacing cold “ chicken ”
are threatening the digestions of the chief mourners in
the scenes of blushes and old slippers. The only thing
that ought to go, but seems to have become a petrified
system, is the heavy, gritty, tomb-topped wedding-cake.
Is it becoming fashionable to display the bridegroom’s
presents along with those of the bride, or was the fact
that a recent list of wedding-gifts included two spirit-
stands and a silver cigarette-case merely an airy way of
saying that the lady ?
Doctor Carver’s “Wild America” Show, close by the
Zoo, in Moore Park, is one of the most thrilling and
realistic spectacles that I have ever sat out—from the
hazel-eyed Doctor himself, with his jubilee-red shirt and
wonderful shooting feats, down to the smallest papoose (a
society lady behind me on the grand-stand called it
“caboose”) in its beads and wampum. There was but
one lady Indian in camp—Mrs. Black Bear, wife of a swell
Sioux chief—and her mode of gettting up her really hand-
some face for the ring was reminiscent of pea-soup and
tomatoes : the whole visage was yellow-ochred, and each
cheek (even as a barbarian, woman knows just where to
lay on the brush) was coloured a deep, ballet-girl red.
After the performance the audience meandered amongst
the wigwams, which are painted by the Injuns with all
sorts of signs and symbols. One picture shows a degener-
ate pale-face flirting with a squaw in what looks like a
gin-bottle done in patchwork, while Nemesis, in the shape
of a redskin equivalent to the “hubby” of civilisation,
sneaks up at the end of a gun with which he destroys the
destroyer of his happiness. In this wasteful way does the
poor, ignorant savage get even with the other fellow.
Why' doesn’t some good kind missionary skip along and
enlighten him in the soothing, civilised doctrine of
damages ?
Local sassiety will not fall in love with the cowboys in
the Wild America show. They are small men, mostly,
and wear their back hair too degage for a nation that
bows down before the gaol-crop. Also, they haven’t
lovely moustaches to speak of, and they don’t swagger
worth a cent, neither do they cast bold glances into the
fine five-shilling seats, nor would their names on the
programme inspire the susceptible schoolgirl to cut out
and put them under her pillow. But they look more
romantic than the Australians in the show, who wear
boiled shirts (the genuine cowboy despises a man in a
boiled shirt), and one of them jarred the unities by dancing
round the ring in a hard felt hat! If any tender female
heart yearns for anything to worship, it will most probably
pass the cowboys and hurl the weight of its young-
affections at a massive Sioux chief with a red blanket
and a heart-breaking profile, who can’t speak a word of
English—but, of course, that wouldn’t signify.

“ minnit ” from a suburban alderman pray ing that the
“ biys and gals” attending the local public school be
taught the art of swimming—so as to give the sharks and
snags a*chance on half-holidays, I suppose. One wonders
what will next be added to the curriculum at the State
schools. But they never institute a class for embyro
domestic servants, nor are the girls taught to become
6 ona-Udc one-pound-a-week cooks.
And, apropos to public school “ fax,” one can’t help
smiling cynically at the Don Quixote of the N.S.W. educa-
tional system having his personal prattlers taught at
home by a visiting governess. What was sauce for the
gander would be poison to the goslings seemingly'.
A dreadful experience happened to a N.S.W. hospital
matron who was perambulating through a ward one day,
followed by a priceless wheezing pet pug, and who paused
at the bedside of a dying Chinese. “ Is there anything
you would like ?” she asked, impelled by the same motive
that pervades Law and Justice when it allows the con-
demned convict what he fancies for breakfast, and the de
mortuis Celestial faintly allowed that there was. “ Tel]
me and I will send it,” said the lady unwisely, and then
came the horrible whisper; “All li—eatee that litee dog
—you savee?”
Sydney is to have a National Juvenile Industrial Exhi-
bition at the Exhibition barn, P.A.P., commencing on
Easter Saturday and running till 11th of April ; and once
again the sobs of the disappointed competitors and the
indignant shrieks of the mothers of the same will waft
gently through the dailies. And the hated prize-winner,
who has spent £6 4s. Bd. on exhibit-carriage and has come
out at the other end with an evasively-worded certificate
and a copper medal, will want somebody with a soothing
manner to explain the attraction of the situation to him.
Jules Joubert (he doesn’t like to be called Joobert, by the
way) directs the outbreak, and the Countess of Jersey is
to be asked to set things going to a two-and-sixpenny
audience.
Quite the latest fad of the Apostles of Hygiene, who
see physical doom in a cucumber, and who regard bread
less than a week old as a sort of crusty felo-de-se—is the
aggressive onion, which is now worshipped as the only
specific for old age, false teeth, and every other form of
bodily decay. So now there will be something more than
her flat waist and her heelless square-toed boots to tell
the sanitary young woman by at sight. You will know it
is she by the way people will rush to windward when she
arises in the east.
The “ Separation ” waltz, by Charles Manby, of Towns-
ville, which reached me by a recent post, is the very
latest musical idea in North Queensland, and though it
does seem odd that the composer should introduce one or
two of De Beriot’s airs into what ought to be a purely
Australian inspiration, one forgives this freetrade in
harmony because the time is so good throughout. The
“ Separation ” waltz is one that would shine under the
heading of “ Buttercups and Daisies” in an instruction-
book, but it lacks the vigour the subject should have in*
spired in the bosom of a Tovvnsvillian.
If all “Jilts” dress as well as Fanny Enson does in the
name-part, at the Criterion, their mittened suitors would
console themselves by totting up the amount they save
annually in clothes-bills. Enson’s prettiest gowns are a
pale-pink brocade bodice and train, the jupe draped in
pink chiffon, the edges finished with gold braid; a
heliotrope velvet and chiffon race dress with tiny bonnet
on suite topped with heliotrope ; and a pale-pink bodice
and train designed in floral flowers, the jupe covered with
white chiffon ; sleeves of the chiffon and Medici loilar
lined with ruby velvet. Mrs. Brough, who made a dainty
Kitty Woodstock, was nicest in a sulphur-coloured brocade
bodice and train, the skirt bouffant and held down by
panels of the brocade, berthe of sulphur chiffon and
diamond ornaments ; a white voile with striped satin vest
and white hat and feathers ; and the simple ultramarine
blue silk walking gown with blue velvet beet, hat trimmed
with yellow asters, of the third act. There was a crowded
house at the second performance on 16th inst., including
a sort of ecoleastical theatre-party, the leading chaperone
of which wore her hands au naturel —but, of course, her
diamond rings were really' lovely'.

The marriage of Miss Ida Campbell, sixth daughter of
Hon. Alexander Campbell, M.L.C., with Mr. Reginald
Bloxsome, one time secretary to Lord Loftus, took place
at Mort’s Church, Woollahra on sth inst., in the
presence of a large wedding-party. The church was a
floral bower, a large bell being a feature of the decora-
tions, the prevailing tones of which were subdued. The
bride was simply attired in white embroidered muslin
with white sash, the skirt cut walking length;
dainty white hat trimmed with chiffon and heather
bouquet of souvenir and blush roses and leaves tied
with white ribbons ; the one maid (Miss Laura Campbell)
wearing doru guipure and heliotrope ribbons, hat
en suite. The reception and breakfast were held at
Rosemount, the residence of the bride’s parents, after
which Mr. and Mrs. Bloxsome left for Ranger’s Valley,
New England. The going-away dress was of fawn tweed
with ivory silk blouse and pink tie ; small sailor hat.

well-meaning coffee-palace) entertained Earl Jersey, a
handful of aides-de-camp, and Mr. Fuller, a guest at
Government House, at dinner on 10th inst. The orgies
were rather formal, and during the evening Lord Jersey
consented to become a member of the club.

Toby Barton, Q.C., has lately worn an
injured expression, suggestive of wounded amour-
propre. Robert Richardson, a cocky young man
who, like a shilling draper, has always got all his
intellectual goods on the counter, lately sent out
from Edinburgh to Sydney Echo a parody on
Longfellow’s “Excelsior” entitled “Up’ards,”
“by Robert Richardson and Edmund Barton,”
which appeared in the Sydney Punch of 1867.
No more unfunny and ill-constructed parody on a
fine piece of verse was ever contumeliously thrown
into an editorial dirt-box. Mr, Barton is only
alleged by Mr. Richardson, 8.A., to be respon-
sible for one verse of “ Up’ards,” which is lucky,
as if he were found guilty of a verse and a half of
such ghastly idiocy a hopeless blight would fall
upon his business as a lawyer. Mr. Richardson
(B. A.) says he feels very sore at heart that when
“Up’ards” was quoted by Parson Be van, in
Sydney, it was attributed by him to “an Ameri-
can author.” With a view of showing the sort of
humourless toramyrot affected by Richardson,
8.A., Parson Bevan, and the “ American author,”
let us give “ Up’ards ” in its entirety :
UP' ARDS.
(By Robert Richardson and Edmund Barton.) *
A cold dark night was a-comin’ down
As a young man pass’d through a backwoods town ;
In his fist he clutch’d a tatter’d flag,
An’ this yere motter was on the rag—
“ Up’ards!”
From under his old tile’s ragged rim
His dark eye flasht like a tallow glim ;
And like a penny whistle rung
The accents of his Yankee tongue—
“ Up’ards!”
Thrice through windows did he see
Three sevril families takin’ tea ;
He smelt the corncakes, yet never swerved,
But sadly to himself observ’d—
“ Up’ards !”
An old man holler’d, “ Young feller, tak oar’,
Thar’s a rotten old pine tree right up thar’ ;
You’re a dead gone coon if it falls on your head.”
The young man merely winkt and said—
“ Up’ards !”
A pretty little girl cried, “Stay, oh, stay,
And you mayjnarry me straight away ;
Adown his nose a tear did flow :
“No use, young woman, I’m flxt to go
Up’ards !”
About tea-time the follerin’ day
A pedlar chanct to pass that way,
When he suddenly thought he heard a coon
Remarkin’ to a pensiv’ toon—
“ Up’ards !”
His dog then rummag’d and snuffd-about,
And discover’d soon what, beyond all doubt,
Was that poor young man, as cold as ice,
Who carried that flag with that rum device—
“ Up’ards!”
Tuckt up in a drift to his chin he lay,
In a rayther uncomf’table kinder way ;
But stooping low the pedlar heard
A sigh and one myster’ous word
“ Up’ards !”
Well, the pedlar carried that young man down,
And he took him back to his friends in town ;
And “ don’t weep,” said he, in a kindly tone,
“ For I guess this young man’s not yet gone
Up’ards!”
Laugh, ye wretches, laugh !
* We hope this latter assertion isn’t a criminal libel.—
Ed. B.

Beautifully staged, the Monday night’s
performance of ‘ ‘ Arrah-na-Pogue, ” at Sydney Her
Majesty’s, was as long and tedious as the first
night of a Shakespearean production. Maggie
Moore, as Arrah (her brogue is simply beautiful),
is by turns witty, tender, pathetic, and fiery, all
with the utmost fidelity to nature, and her ren-
dering of the “Wearing of the Green,” with a
strong dramatic ending, was alone worth the
price of admission. George Rignold is all
right as the Post, but we don’t care much
for his Shaun. He interpolated an Irish word
or two when he’d time to think of it, and
they glared from the mass of English like the
widely-separated plums in boarding-school duff.
Here and there he was powerful, particularly
when he learnt that Beamish was the man sheltered
by his wife, but, throughout, he lacked the
devil-may-care spontaneity the character requires.
Harry Jewett is too large and too manly to depict
naturally the cowardly, cringing Michael Eeeney.
He did his best, but Nature would assert herself,
and his brogue was occasionally suggestive of the
“ Welsh fairy ’’ he handled so well in the “ Merry
Wives.” Arthur Greenaway, a perfect poem in
the dress of the period, is too subdued, lacks
the dash necessary for the MacCoul. Jack
Sweeney, bowld as a lion and purty as a
pratie - patch, played the O’Grady with the
air of a P.M.G. and a brogue that O’Connor
might envy. Edith Blande’s success as
Fanny Power was based on looks only, and her
“delicate taste of the brogue” was fitful and
tended chiefly in the direction of distorting vowel-
sounds. In smaller roles other folks were as bad
or as good as opportunity allowed, according to
their fitness for their parts, but the general impres-
sion was fluffy. A few nights’ run, however, will
rub off the corners and curtail the time of per-
formance by at least an hour. By the way, the
effect of the cabin-scene was much enhanced by
the engagement of John Coghlan, the Irish piper,
and the jig-dancing of Lucy Osborne.
i\'- * * *
“The Jilt” was greeted by a brim in ing-
over house at Sydney Criterion on Saturday night.
The plot is not remarkable for logic nor the situa-
tions for strength, and the racing allusions are
usually “a bit off;” nevertheless, the play will
stand comparison with either of its immediate
predecessors, and good acting may squeeze a fort-
night’s run oaf of it. The Mrs. Welter of Miss
Romer—a free and faithful impersonation of the
hard-headed, self-helping, straight-going York-
shirewoman, set off by a delightful dialect —is,
probably, the most ably-handled character. The
good-hearted and careless Geoff. Tudor is one of a
school that Dot Boucicault (he looks an ideal
jockey) has made peculiarly his own, and his
scenes with Patty Browne, the Phyllis, had all
the finish and dash that characterises their joint
“business.” Run on familiar lines, the Myles
O'Hara of George Titheradge has a lovely brogue
and is otherwise convincing. Arthur Lawrence’s
Daisy is a perfect study in make-up, gesture and
enunciation, and Cates’ Lord Marcus is an airy
villain of much acceptance. The Rev. Spooner of
Owen Harris, run on Thornton lines, is a feather
in the young actor’s cap, but he requires to be kept
down, here and there. Percy Lyndal and Fanny
Enson, as the Woodstocks, were, on the whole,
disappointing, and the Tudor of Trent Adams
was most brilliant when least in evidence.
“ Society,” usually accounted one of Robertson’s
weakest, is in rehearsal. By the way, the four
leading Sydney theatres are occupied by works
from Irish pens—Boucicault, Falconer, Wallace
and Fitzball.
** * *
At Sydney Garrick the Montague-Turner
Company opened on Saturday last with “ Mari-
tana,” when a music-loving houseful rushed the
theatre to welcome their old favourites, after an
operatic absence of seven years, and Treasurer
Smythe was reluctantly compelled to refuse
money. Annis Montague and Charles Turner
seem to have lost little of the freshness, purity
and vocal power that made them public idols then;
consequently, a repetition of former triumphs may
be expected. Natural and consonant acting
wedded to inspired singing kept the audience at
white heat, and encores and floral offerings fol-
lowed all the principal items. Lazarillo was well
sung by Cicely Staunton, who secured several
encores, but her acting was, in places, expression-
less. Warwick Gainor was Don Jose, and his
fine voice was at its best in “ Oh, ray courage now
regaining,” which made amends for the stiffness
he had previously exhibited. Save in respect of
mechanical singing, Fred. England cannot be
commended for his King of Spain, and need not
be yet awhile afraid of losing himself in his im-
personation. A. F. Magney, as the Marquis, and
Miss Whitmore as his Marchioness, were equal to
the demands made upon them, and the minor
parts were capably undertaken by Harry Craig,
W. P. Morrison, and others. A capital little
orchestra was ably conducted by Rivers All press
(the Turners’ old conductor), M. do Willirnoff
being leader. The opera was well mounted, taking
into consideration the lack of stage-room. “11
Trovatore ” will follow “ Maritana ” —perhaps on
Saturday night.

“Eileen Oge” was substituted at Syd-
ney Royal on Saturday night for “The Irish
Detective,” the curtain rising on a crowded
Olympus and a fair number of “classifieds.”
“ Eileen Oge,” an emotional melodrama by
Edmund Falconer, has been previously played in
Sydney, years ago, and is an Irish stew of
rampant landlordism and victimised tenantry,
revolving round a beauteous colleen who inspires
general adoration. The peasant’s marriage with
Eileen is stopped right at its first plunge by
means of a warrant for his arrest, based on forgery
and other villainy, followed by imprisonment and
exile. Various vicissitudes follow, including
atttempted seduction and murder, and the design
ing landlord is just about to get spliced to Eileen,
when his arrest occurs, and virtue is rewarded
while the audience proceeds to tumble out directly
daylight is apparent through the other end of the
tunnelled plot. The scenery, by George Gordon,
is particularly fine, (’.specially “the vision.”


Grattan can’t be equalled as an Irish im-
personator, and it goes without saying that his
Bnan I arrell is as natural as art can make it.
‘ Young Dick ” Stewart, in playing Patrick
(J Donnell, the persecuted peasant-lover, made
Bon l® weepingly-pathetic situations
decidedly comical. Robert Inman, as Harry
was a passable wicked landlord; and J.
P, West, as an avaricious Scotch bailiff, was as
forcible as most of the other characters were weak.
George Melville’s Father Mahony, Capt. de
Burgh s Mr. Moriarty, and A. G. Boulton’s John
Ihomas were indifferent performances. 0. J.
Berkeley’s brief appearance as Tim the Penman
was Constance Deorwyn, in
the title vole* was uneven in her emotional inter-
pretations : Maud Appleton was underplaced in
the part of Norah O’Donnell; and Alice Deorwyn
was a sprightly maid-of-all-work as Bridget
Maguire. ‘Shin Fane” will be staged on Satur-
day evening.
