** * *
Fernandez made his fourth ascension from
Bondi Aquarium, last Saturday, in presence of a
big crowd. The balloon was inflated inside 21
minutes, and the professor jumped, according to
some calculations, from an altitude of between
five and six thousand feet on to a fence. Return-
ing to the Aquarium full of barbs and covered
with glory, blood and sand, he received a purse of
sovereigns from the fairy paw of Alf. Wyburd,
and will be further benefited next Saturday. It
is reported that Alfred is about to resign. For
the sake of the sovereigns that sit behind the
seal-pond it is to be hoped that this is incorrect.
The wily Wyburd is the greatest hustler in the
Southern Hemisphere, and it would be mighty
hard to fill his place.
** * *
At Sydney School of Arts the company
has been further strengthened by the inclusion of
Harry Sullivan, an English comedian, and Tom
Thumb, said to be the largest dwarf extant.
Fanning and Tracey are still well to the fore, but
the Hugos have dropped out of the bill. The
management announce the expected arrival of a
consignment of American artistes by the in-
coming mail.
** * *
At the Toatro de Foli, Sydney, Harry
Rickards is doing payable business with a change
of programme all round. The season only lasts
another fortnight, then Rickards takes a rest in
the bosom of his family at Randwick, after three
years’ constant travelling and playing, ere pro-
ceeding to England, perhaps, through America,
to engage fresh talent.
** * *
“ The Miner’s Right,” as dramatised by
Messrs. Dampier and Garnet Walch for Mel-
bourne Alexandra, depends for success upon a
wild profusion of incidents and a liberal allow-
ance of Australian scenery and effects. The thin
thread of narrative which runs through four acts
is strong and visible to the naked eye, whilst a
short fifth act, or postscript, brings the curtain
down upon a striking bit of business and leaves
virtue in affluent circumstances at the bottom of a
gold-mine. Ralph Boldrewood, the author of the
novel, is not a brilliant dialogue-writer, neither
are Garnet Walch’s efforts in that direction likely
to bring him immortality, consequently the drama
shows a tendency to become tedious ’twixt events.
Although there are sufficient criminal offences in
this entertainment to support the heavy burden
of puerile, humourless talk, much of it should be
promptly expunged together with at least
half-a-dozen extraneous characters. Smart lines
alone can excuse the introduction of people
who have nothing to do with the case. Lacking
“snap” they had better withdraw themselves
entirely and allow the leading characters to get
along with their robberies and murders more
rapidly. The story itself has the all redeeming
merit of being strongest in the right place. It is
at the end of the third act that Dampier finds
himself talking to the corpse of Miss Lily, never
dreaming that the young woman’s happy despatch
has been accomplished during his brief absence
from her side. Presently the dear departed one,
rolling off the log where she lay, falls in a particu-
larly deadlike manner upon the sward, and Dam-
pier, with red gore upon his honest, horny hands,
suffers an inevitable arrest for murder. A subse-
quent trial scene, stage-managed on the lines of
that in “ Arrah-na-Pogue, ” is also cleverly worked
up to a strong climax. This murder-scene, and
the method of fixing the offence to the leading
villain, are cribbed from “My Partner,” facts
which will hardly excite criticism from Alexandra
audiences. More, indeed, might well be made of
Dampier’s discovery of the poor dead Lily. The
incident would bear to be written up, and longer
dwelt upon by the actor. Such acting oppor-
tunities as “The Miner’s Right” offers are well
enough looked after by the company for whom it
was arranged. The drama will run and prosper
in the land of its birth, being racy of the soil and
redolent of old days at the diggings.
** * *
<( At the Melbourne Princess’ the revived
“Yeomen of the Guard” ought to last for a
second week. Save in respect of orchestral un-
steadiness at some points and unnecessary vigour
at others, Saturday’s performance gained, on the
whole, by comparison with the show of two years
ago. Ryley could scarcely avoid being a more
bearable Sergeant Meryll than was Marnock, the
ponderous martyr to vibrata who lumped through
the part in the first instance. Miss Elsie Cameron,
as a fine contralto ami capable actress, enables
Dame Carruthers to take her proper position at
last. Formerly the Dame was either an inaudible
lady named Ameris, since dead, or a strong-voiced
supernumerary who tore her share of dialogue
to tatters with her teeth, like an amiable cannibal
discussing cold missionary. Howard Vernon and
Elton seem both to have put a finer polish upon
their original work, and Leumane gives far more
weight to his words than he used to, though, to
be sure, his rolling, nautical walk and mannerism
of singing, as though he were passing casual
remarks about the weather, show few signs of
abatement. Miss Ida Osborne, although rather
out of voice on Saturday, was the same old young
Phoebe, pleasing in a particularly pleasing
character, but still apt to lay too much emphasis
in too many places. Miss Flora Graupner, who
is acquiring the art of dressing her figure to the
best advantage, appears to find much favour as
the stroller Elsie, but more people than the
present writer are haunted by a suspicion that
Flora always converts a priraa-donna into
an elevated type of “singing-chambermaid.”
Another dose of “Gondoliers” follows “The
Yeoman,” this week or next.
** * *
Matters elsewhere stand as before.
“Skipped by the Light of the Moon” is not
another unparalleled success or overwhelming
triumph at the Opera House, but it draws fair
audiences and pleases them in an unconventional
way after they are drawn. The saddest feature
of this entertainment is its long, draggletail name,
which apparently satisfies the parties chiefly con-
cerned, otherwise they would have cut it down to
“Skipped by Moonlight,” or, better still, have
altered the thing to “On the Loose,” or “ Better
Halves,” “Bowled Out,” “Smash Up,” “The
Baby Chase,” “ Delirium Tremens,” or anything.
Life is too short for the busy world to think
seriously about a farce possessing as many names
as a German prince.
** m *
“ Doctor Bill,” in its fourth week, is still
blowing great guns at the Bijou, and appears to
fill the upper regions better than anything yet
produced in the new theatre. B. and B. produc-
tions, as a rule, are not eagerly rushed by the com-
mon herd, which likes to have its imagination
tickled by large green and yellow posters and
highly spiced advts. in the papers. “Doctor
Bill ” has some fine long posters outside and some
good broad jokes inside, whereby its all-round
popularity is accounted for. The upper-circle
religiously roars like a rhinoceros when Miss
Kemble seems to account for her neuralgia by the
fact that her husband is out so much at night.
And when Brough sententiously remarks that he
no longer does any of the things he did in his
bachelor days the gods drop big tears of laughter
unconfined upon the heads of the stalls. It is
announced, however, that the “Doctor Bill”
season must shortly terminate. Miss Achurch
and Co. will be coming along anon.
** * *
The Royal pant, is panting its last this
week, after enjoying a longer lease of popularity
than seemed possible at the outset. On Saturday
the Cartwright-Nethersole company make a first
appearance here in their Sydney success, “ The
Middleman.” The city having been overdone
with music and legs of late the new-comers are
sure of a good reception.
* * *
Programmes at the halls are about the
same as usual, with a little difference. The
Gaiety show has been winding up for several
weeks past, with a farcical sketch by Lance Len-
ton, who has now laid irreverent hands on “East
Lynne,” without considering that the sorrows of
dear Lady Isabel ought to make her sacred. He
has obviously missed a point, too—seeing that the
cruel jest had to be thrown off—in not persuading
Frank Clark to appear as poor little Willie. The
Waxworks has wisely taken from the Argus a
paragraph which was intended to be funny, and
is printing it as a serious puff. This device does
credit to Phil Stuart’s ingenuity, and should serve
as a wholesome warning to a journal which is
funny only in its most solemn moods. The
Oyclorama and other things are snorting away
satisfactorily.

The Bulletin’s announcement of the
coming event at Melbourne Royal was followed,
on Saturday last, by a manifesto from Mr. George
Coppin, and solemn paragraphs in the daily
journals, which latter always assume to let the
world into a secret after its disclosure in another
quarter. Nothing ever was, is, or can be until a
dreary old daily newspaper has said it.

Mrs. Adelaide Stoneham, actress, well-known in Aus-
tralia, died in England last Christmas Eve, aged 69
mother of Stoneham, long in Dampier’s company, at
Melbourne, and of Miss Adelaide Stoneham, who used to
excel as a souorette and burlesque actress. Mrs. Stone-
ham acted old women, the same line as Mrs. Phillips, Mrs.
Wooldridge, Mrs. Crosby, and Mrs. C. Jones, but, of
course, she was a heroine once. Her latest achievement
is to prove the virtues of the Theatrical Fund, into which
she had paid £9O, but was necessitated to draw over
£6OO.
Tne very successful comedy, “Jane,” at London
Comedy, seems to be abominably coarse. It is by
Lestocq, and Sydney Grundy writes scathingly about it iir
the latest Era, also expressing his absolute “hate” of
Clement Scott, who cracks it up. Ledger, of the Era
cordially joins in the disgust excited by the breadth of
the piece.
“Dramaticus” writes: “A bit about Jimmy Cathcart
may be appreciated. What a fine, fresh, young fellow he
was when he came out, in London, with Chas. Kean’s
gorgeous Shakesperean revivals, and orated before the
Queen, at Windsor Castle! The great treat of his life
was coming to Melbourne with the Keans, and seeing his
charming and beloved sister, Mrs. Heir. With Bob
Heir Cathcart struck up an enthusiastie brotherhood,
though at rival houses they acted exactly the same
line—the lagos, Bassanios, and so on. Heir had to
second Barry Sullivan, in trying to pull down the
Keans and Cathcart, but after the show the Heirs used to
whip round from the Royal, and meet Cathcart at the
old Haymarket stage-door, Little Collins-street, near
Paddy’s market—old times, Rocks ! old times, Jake !

On the first night of Jerome K. Jerome’s “New Lamps
for Old,” at Daly's (N.Y.), when the curtain went up on a
gentleman reading a book which he declared, horrifledly,
to be the wickedest book he’d ever road, a voice from the
gallery enquired—“ Say, what is it?”
Emma Abbott, the American singer, a native of Chicago
and a pupil of Marohesi’s, died at Salt Lake City, early in
January, aged 40, or thereabouts. In 76 she made her
debut at Convent Garden in “ La Figlia de Reggimento,”
but seceded from the company owing to her refusal to
play Violetta in “La Traviata,” on the score of its im-
morality, an objection which she overcame in after years.
Emma Abbott will be long rememembered for the way in
which she stood up in a Nashville church (Ingersoll and
Greeley were among her dearest friends) and demolished
the attack made by the inmate of the pulpit on the stage.
Her husband, Eugene Wetherell, now dead, was a
druggist.
The only time a singer ever really loses her jewels is
just before she files her schedule.

Felix Tanner, the late starving man, says, according to
a contemp., he is now 1 Jib. heavier than when he com-
menced to fast, but that” his flesh seems to be numbed
and doesn’t bleed when cut. Also that his head is giddy,
his feet and legs swollen. Further that: “ I can see
nothing before me but to go for another fast as soon as I
can feed myself up for it, and as sure as I do it will ruin
me for life. But what can Ido ? People won’t give me a
trial in the boat, and I cannot bring myself out without
cash, so it is all that is left for me.” The unhappy Felix
must starve in order to live.

Alma Murray, the well-known English actress, is sur*
prised that Bellew, the credited author of “Hero and
Leander,” did not proclaim his indebtedness to Grill-
parzer’s tragedy on the same subject. Miss Murray is
possessed of an adaptation of Grillparzer’s play, from the
pen of her husband, one Watson, hence the surprise.
Anyhow, the P.B. production in London will be fore-
stalled.
John L. Sullivan refuses to play the lover. He says : “ I
won’t make a fool of myself acting the love-sick swain.”
He also declares that he has no difficulty in memorising
his lines, but feels silly when practising gestures and
poses, and finds it almost impossible to control his voice,
having “ a tendency to bellow.” He further asserts that
“ I find it irksome to stand still on the stage. My legs
are forever attempting to take steps as one does when
facing an opponent in the ring, and I have to be on the
lookout continually to check this tendency on the part of
my understandings to back and fill. Not that lam con-
ceited enough to believe that I have the material in me
for the making of a Booth or a Salvini, but I want to
show the public that I have some brains besides a fair
share of muscle and a little more ability than the prize-
ring demands.” In the opinion of The Bulletin, John
has not now in him the makings of a Slavini, either.

baritone. _ Verdi has been looking for a subject for high-
class comic opera for twenty-five years. This is his third
Shakespearean subject.

“ ad.”;—“ There will be sixteen charming and shapely
young ladies on the stage arrayed in tights, which dis-

The leading London theatrical paper has a glowing
paragraph about Mr. M. Tier, “ the well-known Mel-
bourne entrepreneur,” having presented Miss Jessie
Phillips with a beautiful gold chain and diamond, ruby,
and emerald pendants, in recognition of her talents as a
serio-comic “on the eve of his return to Australia.”
Who’s this Tier ? We well enough know the Falstafflan
Leon Welby Tier, so long the ringmaster, and formerly a
smart clown of Burton’s circus, and we fancy he handled,
or engined, another kind of Burton, bitter but refreshing,
in Adelaide, but how on earth has he bloomed into the
rival of Williamson and Muzzy ? Perhaps he is bringing
out Irving, Bernhardt, Melba, or the Spotted Boy with
Two Heads.

Charles Warner, according to private advices, has no
intention of opening in Melbourne at Easter, as stated
by the Melbourne press. He tours America next year,
with Australia to follow, probably opening in Sydney or
Melbourne about July.
A number of Parisian playwrights are seriously pro-
posing that the dramatic critics of the leading newspapers
shall be elected for a period of five years by a council of
authors. In Australia it is the theatrical managers who
desire to perpetrate the press-notices. Sometimes they
do run in a column of ungrammatical slush.

“ Pull it down,” exclaim the architectural and theatri-
cal critics, apropos to Melbourne Theatre Royal failing to
get a new firm of tenants. The gallery is a beastly den,
and you can hardly see in the upper circle. The Royal
opened 9th November, 1872, the old house having been
burnt in the March before.
A young author was reading his piece before the
members of the Connklie Framjaise, when Got, the great
actor, fell sound asleep. The offended author complained
to the head of the Society that Got was grossly neglect-
ing his duties—instead of listening he was sleeping. It
was surely impossible for a sleeping member to give any
opinion at all. “Ah !” said the irrepressible G6t, with a
bland smile, “monsieur forgets that in a case like this
sleep w an opinion.”
Dilke says that one result of the late
Strike will be that the workmen will take a more
active interest in politics. How true that already
is in regard to Australia ! The Ballot-box is the
workman’s true weapon. First get One Man One
Vote, then lush the legislatures.
The other day a N.S.W. man who had
been referred to in print as Mr. “Judas” Some-
thing, promptly wrote to explain that his name
was “Judah” and not “Judas.” However, it
isn’t everyone who pays such deference to Gentile
prejudices, for up Scone (N.S.W.) way lately a
youngster was christened “Beelzebub.”
Out of the fifty-two counties of California
there is not one that does not produce grapes. The
largest vineyard in the world—that of Senator
Leland Stanford—is in Tehama County, and con-
sists of 3,825 acres, or about 3,000,000 vines!
California also has the smallest vineyard in the
world—the one grape-vine in Santa Barbara
County, which is seventy years old, has a diameter
of one foot twelve inches from the ground, and
whose branches cover an area of 12,000 feet. This
one vine produces from 10,000 to 12,000 pounds of
grapes, and the old Mexican woman who planted
it used to have her Indian peons make wine from
these grapes by treading them with their bare feet.
It is believed that there are planted not less than
150,000 acres of vines in the State and in the
adjoining States. The amount exported to the
Eastern States and foreign countries will not fall
far short of 10,000,000 gallons this year.

Verses We Have Read.
The Midnight Visitor.
Whose steps are those ? Who cornea so late ?
Let me pome in, the door unlock.
’Tis midnight now ; my lonely gate
I open to no stranger’s knock.
Who art thou ? Speak ? Men call me Fame ;
To immortality I lead.
Pass, idle phantom of a name.
Listen again, and now take heed ;
’Twas false. My names are Song, Love, Art;
My poet, now unbar the door.
Art's dead. Song cannot touch my heart,
My once love’s name I chant no more.
Open, then now for, see, I stand,
Riches my name, with endless gold,
Gold, and your wish in either hand,
Too late—my youth you still withhold.
Then, if it must be, since the door
Stands shut, my last true name to know,
Men call me Death. Delay no more;
I bring the cure of every woe.
The door flies wide. Ah ! guest so wan,
Forgive the poor place where I dwell;
An ice-cold hearth, a heart-sick man,
Stand here to welcome thee full well.
—From the French of Henki Murgkr.
The poem beneath, like the charming
“Bannerman of the Dandenong,” published by
The Bulletin some weeks ago, is quoted from
“A Time and Times,” a collection of poems by
Alice Werner, a young London poetess (where did
she get her Australian experiences?), who has
lately published another book of verses entitled
“ The King of the Silver City.”—
DAWN.
It comes—it looms up in the darkness —
Something—I hardly know
Of a word, or a name to name it —•
But I feel it must be so ;
For a time of choice is coming
For weal or for woe.
The pulses of a nation,
Beating in fever and pain ;
The fever of woe and want —
The fever of greed and gain—
And the stars are reeling in heaven —
And the great sea moans for her slain.
In the stillness of my life
I hear the tramp afar
Of the armies marching
Under the morning star,
To the Armageddon battle,
Where the eagles are.
The days lie dark before me-
I know not what shall be,
But at midnight or at day dawn,
When the call comes unto me,
I am ready to rise and follow —
To the death-agony.
O, my people, my brothers !
God grant me to be true—
Ever true to His highest truth ! —
No great thing can I do;
But, firm as a faithful heart may love,
Ever I stand by you.
Yours—and I see God’s angel
Coming along the sky
With the garments rolled in blood,
And the steadfast eye—
And some say her name is Love,
And some, Liberty.
She comes—who will know her coming?
Or be ready her step to greet
When she comes with the blood on her brow
And the dust about her feet ?
Who will bravely drink of the bitter
Without a hope of the sweet ?
Let us be true—heart-loyal—
Ready what time she calls !
Justice and truth are met
To cast down the age-built walls.
Happy shall be the victors that day,
And blessed he who falls !
If we reckon the population of the globe
at 1,400,000,000 of human beings, there would be
room for them all on the frozen surface of the lake
of Constance, Switzerland, and the crush would
not be so very great either, as there would be a
space of four square feet for each person. If the
ice were to break and the whole human race were
t r hen to sink into the watery grave, the level of the
lake would only be raised six inches.
The ordinary pay for making match-boxes
in London is a gross, though in one instance
the newly-formed Union has forced the price up to
Working from seven in the morning till ten
or eleven at night, and often all night on Fridays,
women can earn from ss. 6d, to 7s. a week,
of which 2s. 6d., at least, must go in lodging.
No wonder the match-making companies pay!
No wonder the big firms who employ these women
declare dividends of 22 and 25 per cent. And
what does it matter (asks a Radical paper) if, in
order to put on an extra 1 per cent., hundreds of
women are compelled to exist under conditions
which make life a mockery, pleasure a thing un-
known, and virtue slow suicide ? There is no law
of man against it.
A well-known Sydney artist tells a good
English story quite new to us. Many years ago a
meeting was held at Clapham, near London, to
take preliminary steps to erect a new Anglican
church. The first meeting was most enthusiastic,
and thousands of pounds were promised in the
room. Sir Gilbert Scott, the celebrated
architect, was written to and asked to supply a
sketch design for a £25,000 building. He did so,
and the sketch, an admirable one, was submitted
to a second meeting. By this time, however, the
enthusiasm had waned, and it was decided that
the architect should be requested to design a
somewhat less expensive building, which he con-
sented to do. Just then a great financial panic
intervened, and at a third meeting it was coh-
cluded to defer, for a time, the erection of the
church, and to ask Scott to make his fee as light
as possible under the circumstances. When he
sent in a bill for 200 guineas, the indignation of
the meeting, in which the bishop appeared to
concur, knew no bounds. “ What!” they
shrieked, “200 guineas for a couple of paltry
sketches !” The bishop was appointed to inter-
view Scott, which ho did, winding up his protest
with: “200 guineas for, perhaps, a day's work!
Why, I have amongst my curates a large number
of most estimable and intellectual men who have
to live, with their wives and families, on 100
guineas a year!" “My Lord,” said Scott, who
had patiently listened until the cleric had done,
“I am an architect—a bishop among architects.”

Tales of the Old Regime.
No 1.—Convict Jerrick’s Death-Mask.
(FOR THE RULLETIN.)
It is difficult to say at what pei’iods and in
what places the Old Regime reached perfection, so
near to that point of diabolism beyond which
devilishness could not further go, were New South
Wales at any time in the first forty years of the
century, Yandemonia at any time within the
first fifty, and Norfolk Island at any time within
the years 1788 to 1805, and 1826 to 1855. A tie of
imperial infamy linked the Administrators —
mark, the Administrators and not the subject-
peoples—of one epoch to those of another, and the
whole geographical area fanned by the miasmic
breath of the Penal System was encircled, so to
speak, by one unbroken principle of domination.
If, however, discrimination be insisted upon, we,
for ourselves, would be inclined to indicate
Port Arthur in V.D.L. in the “Early Thirties”
as the spot and the time when the Regime burst
into blood-red blossoms of unsurpassable beauty.
Beauty, that is, according to the notions of the
Colonial Authorities, and their masters, the
Eminent Statesmen of Downing-street.
At Port Arthur in the “ Early Thirties,” were,
for instance, first formulated those maxims of
penal science, by careful study of which—not to
mention less brilliant administrators—John Price
was enabled to render devoted service to his
Country. At Port Arthur in the “ Early Thirties ”
cruelty first became scientific. At other places
and at prior dates, the Regime, while never
deviating from its primary principle of sovereignty
—that the convict wai not human - had proceeded
after the Rule of Thumb. Hence it occasionally
erred ; now and then an official lapsed into the
grievous mistake —mistake ? let us not palter with
words, but say the sin ! —of supposing that a trans-
port retained the heart and brain of a man with
his outward semblance. At Port Arthur in the
“Early Thirties,” any such tendency to error was
corrected by local regulation. Cruelty was for
the first time scientifically organised with due re-
gard to a convict’s physical and mental character-
istics. Not that every convict was the subject of
this methodical treatment. There were some who
were cravens; others who were sneaks; others,
again, so thoroughly depraved that they would
barter their daughters’ honour for a fig of tobacco,
and a brother’s liberty for a glass of rum.
These were the exceptions—technically, the “ex-
empt.” The rule touched only the best, the most
man-like of the prisoners. An “exempt” could
always escape its operation by filling one of the
frequently-occurring vacancies on the staff of the
convict constabulary.
Now, this rule, stated briefly, was that a
prisoner, if a “ special ” transport—that is, of
superior education and training—was systematic-
ally put to the work likely to be farthest removed
from his instincts and aptitudes when free; if an
ordinary convict, to work xn excess of his strength
and capacity. And this is a rather important
part of the rule —punishment was entailed in the
event of failure to perform the allotted task. For
the first failure, a task and a-half was ordered
plus a flogging; for a second failure, a doubled
task and a doubled flogging ; for a third failure, a
trebled task and a trebled flogging; for a fourth
failui’e, a quadrupled task and a quadrupled
flogging. After the fourth failure, what was left
of the transport was almost invariably in hospital,
or reposing on “Dead Island,” or on its way to
earn a fee for Brother Dougherty, the Hobart
Town hangman. Beautifully exact in operation
(as became its scientific basis), the. reader will not
be surprised to learn that the rule seldom failed to
drive a prisoner utterly and completely to the
devil. This much by way of introduction.
* # #
The before-referred-to invention of the
Regime never received a happier illustration of
its utility than when it broke the refractory and
stubborn spirit, of Convict Benjamin derricks,
aged 23 years, per ship Marquess Wellesley.
There was this singular circumstance connected
with the case of Benjamin derricks—he was a
native of Sydney, notwithstanding that he was a
transport from England for forgery.
At least, the circumstance if not singular was
peculiar enough to draw the particular attention
of Doctor and Commandant Rossell to the trans-
port in question.
A cargo, newly landed from the Government
schooner, was being called over in that mighty
potentate’s presence. The cargo, some forty, were
nearly all Marquess Wellesley people, the ship
having swung to her moorings in the Derwent
just in time to allow of the transfer to the schooner,
ere the latter vessel was despatched to Tasman’s
Peninsula, of the more desperate portion of her
consignment. It was a mere chance that had
brought Dr. Rossell to the mustering-yard at this
juncture. Usually he made the acquaintance of
new comers the day after their arrival, when it
was his practice to personally allot their tasks
under the Local Regulation. This day, however,
his after-lunch stroll led him to the yard, instead
of along the Point Puer Road. In after years,
when enjoying in his comfortable villa in Camp-
bell Town, the pension which a Constitutional
Government continued to him after the demise of
the Old Regime, he used to wonder what impish
impulse had sent him to the yard that day. But
then, when the Doctor indulged in this specula-
tion, he was doting and pious, and very much
afraid of the Hereafter. At the period of the
muster, he was alert and intelligent, fearful of
nobody save Sir George Arthur and a certain
woman in Hobart Town, and in place of being
pious, was devoted to the Regime.
* * *
“Isaacs, Abraham, 50, London, Middlesex
Sessions, 14 years, ship’s number 73, colonial
number to be assigned, Marquess Wellesley,”
•ried the schooner captain from his list or mani-
fest.
“Right,” responded the Mustering-Warder,
who by that monosyllable gave an interim receipt
for the body and appurtenances—sundry chains
and so forth —of Abraham . Isaacs, a Hebrew
“ fence ” from Ratclitfe Highway. And the
Mustering-Warder motioned Mr. "Isaacs to pass
over from the “ cargo side ” to the “ Settlement
side” of the yard. “Isaacs, per Marquess
Wellesley, your number’s 2-5-9-3.”
The Jew passed over, cringing as he clanked,
and muttering, “2-5-9-3,” “2-S-9-3,” as he went.
Only a forty-eight-hour-old resident of Van-
demonia was Mr. Isaacs, but already he under-
stood that to forget his “colonial number ” was
to invite an introduction to the scourger.
Here the doctor entered the yard.
“The Com’dant,” exclaimed a Sub-overseei.
“ Prisoners—attention ! The Com’dant!” re-
peated the Mustering-Warder. “ Cap !” And
the cargo side “capped,” as did most of the
Settlement side—not all. For the officials’ backs
were towards Settlement side, and one or two
hardy spirits, the eye of Law and Order not being
upon them, asserted their independence by keeping
their hands stationary. “It is shocking to
learn,” wrote Lieut.-Colonel Mundy, “that some
convicts, from the moment of their landing, set
themselves in defiance to the regulations.” Fore-
doomed to a bad end were such men.
“Proceed, Mr. Brewer,”ordered the Comman-
dant. “This the schooner lot—Marquess Wel-
lesley ?”
“Yes, sir,” replied the Mustering-Warder, again
touching his peak. “A bad lot, sir, I b’leeve.”

“ Some mistake there,” he said. “Not Sydney,
surely? Interrogate the prisoner, Mr. Brewer.”
“ derricks, forward !”
A tall, lithe form, the virgin down marking
with a faint reddish line the pallid cheeks, shuffled
to the front of the cargo rank.
“ derricks, where were you born ?”
“ Sydney, Botany Bay,” was the answer.
“You he, pris’ner!” said the Commandant.
“Take off your cap when you speak to an
officer, pris’ner! ” said the warder, speaking
simultaneously with his superior.
“ I don’t lie, and I won’t take my cap off again.
I’ve capped three times already.” The prisoner
flushed, it might be with shame, it might be with
passion, as he made this daring speech.
“ Ho, ho! Here’s a young bantam-cock wants us
to trim off his spurs,” laughed the Commandant.
“You don’t lie, you won't take your cap off. But,
please the pigs, I say you do lie, and you shall
take your cap off. Now, cap ! ”
There was a nervous trembling of the convict’s
hands as he clutched a trouser-leg in each.
Beyond this, Dr. and Commandant Rossell re-
ceived no answer.
“Has he heard the Regulation ordering respect
to officers of the Establishment ? ’’
“ Yes, sir, ’twas read at last ship muster in the
Derwent, " replied the schooner skipper.
“By an officer of the Establishment, or ships
surgeon ? ”
“Muster-master Strode read it, sir.”
The evidence was sufficient to satisfy even that
rigidly-conscientious functionary, Commandant
Rossell.
“Give him twenty-five, Brewer, as soon as
you’ve cleared the schooner. We can’t begin too
early to discipline a precocious youngster like this.
Now, Brewer, ask him again where he was born.”
derricks flung back his head as an answer to
Warder Brewer’s question.
“Won’t speak, eh?” said the Commandant.
“ Got a copy of King’s Regulations handy,
Brewer ? ”
A copy of that ingenious compilation which
might not inappropriately have served as by-laws
for a Satanic council, was handy.
“ Read him, No. 40, Brewer.”
The command was obeyed. No. 40 ran—
“ Any such transported offender who shall not, on in-
terrogation hy a person in duly constituted authority as
to particulars of his identity, promptly and truly make
answer to each and all of the questions put to such
offender by such duly authorised person, shall he awarded
such punishment as in the discretion of such authorised
person shall be considered to meet the case.”
“ There, Benjamin derricks, do you hear that ?
But I don’t wish to be harsh.” (The Doctor be-
came humorously tender in tone,) “Come, be
advised, young man. You can’t fight the System.
Answer, truly and promptly, where you were
born. You know you can’t have been born in
Sydney as you’re transported from home.”
derricks began to break. He “capped,” and
said :
“ ’Fore God, sir, I was born in Sydney. At
least I was brought up there, and my mother lived
in the house where she said I was born in.”
“ Heavens !” said Dr. and Commandant Rossell
with something like a sigh, “ what a liar !” He
sighed again and went on: “I’m sorry, young
man, but the Regulations must be obeyed. Give
him another fifty, Brewer. It’s impossible he can
have been born in Sydney. He’s made a good
beginning. But, go on !”
“Where shall I say his native place is, sir?”
asked the Mustering-Warder. It was necessary,
you see, that the records should be exactly kept.
The only books that the System did not trouble
about keeping accurately were the “Punish-
ment,” “ Stores issued to Officers,” and “Officers’
Labour.” Life was too short to keep the first
properly; it would be insulting to the officers’
integrity, and injurious to their pockets, to keep
the second and third.
“Oh, say London, or anywhere; it doesn’t
matter. Now, go on—you’re losing time.”
“Did I give you your colonial number, pris’ner ?”
asked Brewer.
Young derricks’ face had taken a ghastly pale-
ness. It was white when he landed ; it wore now
the hue of a bleached shroud. Already, one would
think, he was getting admirably broken in. Never-
theless he remained mute.
The Commandant lost his temper.
“ What’s your number, young 'un ? ” he roared.
‘ By God, sir, we’ll find a way to your tongue.
String him, Brewer, string him at once and give
him the first ‘dozen.’ String him, I say ! ”
In those adyta penetralia of \ice, the hold and
the “waist ” of the Marquess Wellesley, derrick*
had taken frequent lessons in convicts’ slang. Ha
was, therefore, no stranger to the meaning of tha
term so viciously repeated by the Commandant.
With his flesh creeping, nerves twitching in anti-
cipation of the lash, he stammered out: —
“ Don’t flog me, sir, and I’ll speak. He didn’t
give me a number, sir.”
“Brewer!” Dr. Rossell turned to his subor-
dinate. _ Though the heavens should fall, he would
do justice. He would not punish a transport for
refusing to give his number, if no number had
been assigned him. But
“ I gave him his number, sir—No. 2594,” replied
Mr. Brewer.
“Of course you did —I remember now, I heard
you myself. Oh, you young reprobate ! ”
It pains us to have to throw an imputation of
inaccuracy on any gentleman of the Old Regime;
but we know Dr. Rossell was in error here. The
number he had heard was Abraham Isaacs’:
“No. 2593.” There was no one, however, to tell
the good Commandant so; and, in a few minutes,
the eloquence of the lash and the music of a young
man’s agony filled the muster-yard. Not all of Con-
vict derricks went over to the Settlement side that
day. Some of him clung to the cat’s tails, and
some of him strewed the ground at the foot of
the triangles, For the (logger was in good form,
and the floggee was rather plump on the back,
and tender flesh rent easily.
V # % #
Thus was Convict derricks introduced to
the Regime. It was his happy fortune that the
acquaintance so begun did not last long.
Being, as we have hinted, a tender youth, it, of
course, took him some days to get over the first
scourging. Consequently, it was not deemed
expedient to give him his second dose of “fifty”
till the end of his second week, he having filled
in part of the interval by residence in hospital,
and the remainder by quarry-work.
Following out the ingenious application of the
Local Regulation, by which a convict was put to
the work for which he was least fitted, No. 2594,
who had been taken from a college study to a.
Newgate cell, was ordered to stone-hewing. He
was “ tasked ” to turn out, daily, a cubic measure-
ment of quarried stuff which experienced and
inured prisoners would have found it difficult to
furnish in the time. It was, then, not surprising
that Convict derricks’ folio in the “Register of
Task-work ” presented the following appearance
at the end of his first fortnight:—
[No. 2594.
Folio 1231.—Prisoner; Jebricks, Benjamin ; ship Mar
(juess Wellesley. Received from Hospital, 21/3/3;
- Transferred to Hospital, 1/4/3.
This transcription can only be understood
by a reference to the Punishment Book—the
“P.8.”0f the marginal notes.
On March 21, an entry in this still-extant volume
of delightful reading (as the fine old British gen-
tleman of the Regime period would consider it)
states that Benjamin derricks, No. 2594, Marquess
Wellesley, was presented by the Quarry Overseer
for neglecting to perform his stipulated task. In
consequence of its being his first quarry offence,
mercy and justice were linked together by the
sentence: “Reprimanded; half-rations for three
days ; task work to be doubled.” The ordinary
penalty would have been 25 lashes and a task-and-
a-half.
On the 22nd, by a gigantic effort, Benjamin
derricks, and so forth, doubtless encouraged
thereto by the sustaining force of a half-ration,
and peculiarly stimulated by the persuasive
geniality of a reprimand, managed, his gashed
back notwithstanding, to perform the feat of
extracting from the quarry-bed an aggregate mass
of stone measuring 04 cubic feet. Nevertheless,
he was again reported for punishment. He was
deficient by 152 cubic feet of his task.
On the 23rd, either the gashes on his back were
telling, or else the influence of a half-ration was
waning, or the reprimand lost something of its
persuasiveness, for only a paltry 27 feet repre-
sented Convict derricks’ exertions for the day.
By some remissness, his task, which should have
been 343 feet at the least, was allowed to stand at
216 feet, so that the deficiency on his day’s work
was only 189 feet, and not, as it should have been,
316 feet. But it didn't matter; he would have
been ordered no more than 100 lashes had he been
short 316 feet instead of 189 feet. He was due
for the 100, anyhow. So the punishment entry
was made accordingly.
The 24th was a Saturday—a half-time day.
The men had to bathe, shave, and wash their
clothing in the afternoon. Consequently, the task
imposed on our youthful friend was only the mere
trifle of 125 cubic feet or so. He should have been
getting accustomed to the sustaining power of the

Sunday was a day to be kept “holy” by the
System, and as was appropriate to an officer of the
Regime, Dr. and Commandant Rossell was a
devout upholder of the Ten Commandments—for
the transport. With respect to himself, his
practice was erratic, particularly as to the
Seventh. But we have nothing to do with that
weakness of his in this story —save that we must
make a slight retrospective allusion. The Day,
we say, was kept holy. No unnecessary work was
done.
The Day was kept holy. Only Missionary
Mantin, the hospital doctor, the warders, and
the scourgers worked. The functions of these
classes were necessary on the Sabbath —particularly
thoseof the last-named. For, being a day of rest for
the mass of convicts, punishment in lashes, so far
as regarded breaches of task duty, having been
allowed to accumulate during the week, could bo
“ paid off” without any loss of working time.
It was Convict Jerricks’ first Sunday, and it
was made peculiarly holy to him.
First of all, there was the remanet of 50 from
the day of his landing.
Then there was Thursday’s contribution—so.
Then there was Friday’s allowance of 100, and
Saturday’s solatium of another 100. .
In all, there was a credit balance of 300 lashes,
or 2/00 cuts, iu his favour. The System, having
sanctified the day by a reading by Missionary
M inton of the official prayers (those noble Quakers,
James Backhouse and George Washington
Walker, had not yet visited the settlement, and
the Regime had not been yet convinced there was
any particular necessity for a stronger staff of
c erics) and having honoured it by the reading of
Regulations, proceeded to pay its debts to Convict
Jerricks among others.
Convict Jerricks, when he landed, was a tall,
well-formed, upstanding fellow. Convict Jerricks,
when the Regime, six days later, intimated it was
ready to pay him what it owed him, was a
shrunken, mis-shapen thing. His spirit had broken
and with it his frame. His boyish sulkiness, and
his youthful bravado, had given place to the
cowering terror of the man, as the Regime liked
him to be. We cannot truly say the change was
for the better.
Dr. and Commandant Rossell was present to sec
that Convict Jerricks was duly paid.
# # *
On the Sunday afternoon, No. 2594 was
“ temporarily in hospital.” On Monday, March
26,, at 5.30 a.ln., as the muster of the Quarry-
gang was being made, the Overseer was informed
that Convict Jerricks was still “ temporarily in
hospital.” “Temporarily?” he asked. “Tem-
porarily only,” said the Mustering-Warder. And,
thereupon, was Convict derricks assigned his
normal task of 125 cubic feet of hewage.
On Tuesday, Wednesday, Thursday, Friday,
Saturday mornings, the same little farce was gone
through. No. 2594 remained “ temporarily iu
hospital,” and each day he was allotted his daily
task.
On Sunday morning, as the Commandant took
his customary stroll through the long, low, dark
room which formed the hospital ward, his atten-
tion was directed to No. 2594’s bed.
“ Who this—2s94 ? Who’s he ?”
“Jerricks, sir. In, after punishment,” the
attendant-surgeon made reply. .
“ Oh, I recollect! We had to break him in.
And he passed on.
He bad got to the next bed, when the hospital
doctor spoke again. . . .
“ Number tweuty-five-uinety-four, sir, is in tem-
porarily only. Better be transferred, sir—lie’s
dying. Case of utter collapse.”
“ Certainly, in that case ! ” was the humane
order. “ Transfer him.”
On next Monday morning, at 5.30, Convict
Benjamin Jerricks, No. 2954, was marked off the
“Quarry Muster Book.” “Transferred to
Hospital.” No task was allotted to him that
day.
As the quarry-gang shuffled to their work, they
had to mss the pier-head. As they did so, a
boat shot out from the landing-steps at the end.
An unshrouded coffin extended its uncanny length
[ore and aft.
“ Know whose cheated the Croaker * ?” queried
No. 17,824 fronl his mate, No, 326.
“Did hear —Jerricks.”
“ Oh, the young’un.”
And otherwise unheeded the boat sped on its
voyage to Dead Man’s Isle. A shark followed in
its wake. . ■
At eleven o’clock on the Sunday night the hos-
pital wardsman had taken down No, 2594’s bed-
card, and had obtained the Surgeon’s signature to
11 requisition for “one coffin, pine, No. 2594 on
lid.”
* * *
There were some conundrums propounded
oy the System and the Regime to which no solu-
tion could ever be obtained. One of them was—
“ Supposing Convict Jerricks, instead of taking
what must have been a pleasure trip to Dead
Island, had resumed quarry-duty, would the
Administration have subjected him to the penal-
ties which were incurred by his neglect to perform
the task-work allotted to him whilst in hospital?”
It is conjectured that the men who demanded
an impossible amount of work from a transport,
and who punished him for not performing the im-
possibility by doubling the already impossible
task, would, probably, have had no compunctions
as to holding No. 2694’s back responsible for
No. 2594’s laches. But as there is no evidence on the
point we will give the Regime the benefit of. the
doubt.
# * *
It was 011 Monday, April 2, that No.
2594’s corpse was interred on “Dead Man’s.”
By one of those coincidences, which, as mani-
fested in the spotless career of Dr. Primrose,
Oliver Goldsmith took so much trouble to explain,
but which are, obviously enough, nothing more
than the ironic pleasantries of the Occult Powers
as they jest with men’s lives, there arrived at Port
Arthur by the schooner on Tuesday, Aprils, Con-
vict Jerrick’s mother.
# # #
Her and his story—is soon told.
A naive, pretty country girl of 20, she was trans-
ported in 1808 for robbery in a dwelling house. A
bit of her mistress’ cast-off finery inflamed her
vanity and her cupidity, and—within a month
she had gone through the gruesome sequence of
gaol, assize-court, the condemned cell, and the re-
ceiving-hulk on commutation of sentence. Prom
the receiving-hulk she was removed to the JEolus,
convict ship, Adio master, Dr. Jonathan Rossell
medical superintendent. On the voyage, the
young surgeon’s deference for the Seventh Com-
mandment and all its implications became some-
what weakened; the houris in the hold were
many; their charms were as various as numerous;
what wonder the youthful and susceptible
“ Croaker” tripped ?
The long voyage over, Katty Higgins, with some
other prisoners as frail, was removed to the
maternity ward of Parramatta Factory, from
whence, in due course, she was wedded
to a young man, Benjamin Jerricks, who
had just been granted (on his freedom) a two-
hundred acre block on the Hawkesbury. Leaving
the pledge of the young surgeon’s affection behind
her, Katty went forth to conquer fortune, and

Benjamin aided her efforts by dying within a
couple of years of his marriage.
The block multiplied into two, three, four.
Katty was on good terms, very good terms, with
prominent Government officials. She was their
agent in surreptitious “ rum ” transactions, and
made money. She was offered marriage by some
of the highest in the land, but, bless you, she was
now “an honest woman,” and “didn’t want no
more truck with husbands.” The boy was
released from the Infant Asylum, new-named,
the dead Benjamin officiating as sponsor, and
sent “ home” to be educated. A private school, a
public school, a university career, a few months
about town-and Benjamin, junior, thought he’d
anticipate a maternal remittance by imitating the
signature of his mother’s agent. The agent repu-
diated the signature before knowing w .0 was
responsible for it, and Benjamin Jerricks, after the
customary routine, merged into “ No. 2594.” -
Her son on the highway, as she thought, to
“becoming a gentleman,” Mrs. Jerricks, the
wealthy widow, devoted her charms and her
energies to becoming wealthier. A magnate of
Sydney, she was ambitious to become the same in
Hobart Town. Making frequent trips in one or
other of her own vessels, to the “old town,” she
yet chiefly resided by the Derwent. Another
object was that she had the let us call
it the ambition, to marry Dr. Jonathan
Rossell, who had become a leading man of
the Regime. She did not love him ; there was
no old-time romance to inspire her scheme : but
she had some vague notion that her mai’riage with
him would console her son when he came out from

her the history of his paternity. It was the one
dream of her life to be able to meet that demand
by a presentation of her husband, Dr. Rossell:
“ Here, son, is your father ! ” Poor fool! Rossell
had left a young wife in England, who obstinately
refused to die. He would borrow Mrs. derricks’
money, but marry her —he could not. So she led
him a pretty dance. But she had never told him
that his and her son was living ; and as for him,
you could not expect a man to be solicitous as to
the result of one of many liaisons.
* * *
On the Tuesday, then, Widow derricks
presented herself at the Commandant’s Cottage
and demanded her son.
“ I want my son, Rossell. He came down with
the last schooner lot,” she said.
“ Your son ! ” he gasped, “ what about your
son. Didn’t know you had a son ! ”
“ Nor care, I suppose,” she went on. “ But I
want him, and will have him, or else I’ll ruin you
tc-morrow. Give him to me, cook yonr registers
as you have cooked them before, with less reason
—what’s one pris’ner more or less ? ”
“ I don’t know what you’re talking about!
What do you mean ? ”
Dr. and Commandant Rossell, being one of the
best type of officials of the Old Regime, seldom
troubled himself as to whether he was telling the
truth or not. But on this occasion, he certainly
spoke the truth. Convict derricks was simply a
convict to him. He had never connected No.
2594—n0t even when the transport, had declared
he was born in Sydney—with the wealthy Mrs.
derricks. And, even now, when the woman
claimed her son, he did not think of the young
man. How could a high official with a thousand
or two wretches on his hands, remember all their
names ? Why, “ derricks ” might be an alias.
She told him, then, that the Marquess Wel-
lesley had brought out her son. She was at
Richmond when the ship arrived in the Derwent,
and though her agent had written her full par-
ticulars of the young man’s trial and conviction
by the same vessel, she did not get the letter till
ten days after he had arrived at Port Arthur.
The convict had known next to nothing of his
mother's station—only that she was “a rich lady”
in Sydney, and he had resolved, for her sake, to
keep his knowledge of her address to himself.
Not till she had ended her narrative, and im-
plored Rossell to give up her boy, did the Com-
mandant recollect the young convict whom he had
seen, a couple of days previous, dying in hospital.
Of course, he had not been informed of the death.
That was a mere nothing that need not be re-
reported till the usual weekly hospital “ state and
report” was laid before him on the following
Saturday.
When he recalled the dying man’s name, he
could no longer plead ignorance of his identity.
“ He’s in hospital,” he said, “ suffering—oh,
from fever ! I’ll give you an order to see him and
send a man with you—l’d go with you myself, but
I’ll have to see about squaring up the books.
I s’pose I’ll have to let you have him, but, of
course, you’ll keep him out of sight in Hobart
Town, and get him away to Sydney as soon as
possible. ”
Then he penned this :
H.M. Penal Establishment.
Port Arthur, 3rd April, 183—.
derricks, The officer in charge will permit
Marquess bearer to see prisoner named in
Wellesley. the margin.
(Sd.) Jonathan Rossku,,
Commandant.
At the hospital, she presented the order.
The prisoner-wardsinan looked at it—and at
her, curiously.
“derricks, ma’am? Know his number ?”
“ His ship’s number was 76. I don’t know hia
colonial number.”
He turned to a book, and said “ No. 2594. Ah!
He went to the Island yesterday.”
The widow had never been at Port Arthur
before. She did not know what the Island was,
and said so.
“The Island, ma’am, is the place where they
get better.”
“Then” —the mother’s heart leapt up within
the vulgarly-decked bodice —“he’sgetting better?”
“Yes, ma’am, he is quite convalescent. Better
take the order, ma’am, to the pier—(he pencilled
“2594 ” on the note) —and you’ll find a boat there.
They’ll row you across if you show ’em the order.”
# # #
Unsuspecting the grim fact which lay
beneath the man’s words, and not perceiving the
irony in his phrase, the woman went forth to the
pier. She told the coxswain of the boat that she
wanted to go to the Island, and showed him the
Commandant’s order. Ho could not read, but
took the truth of her statement for granted, and,
for answer, motioned her to a seat, he and his
crew wondering what was the errand of their pas-
senger.
She was still unsuspicious when the boat ran up
to the beach. “Shall we wait, mem?” the cox-
swain asked, and in response she threw him a gold
coin. Gold coins were current even at the Settle-
ment.
She mounted the pine-bordered path, and then,
for the first time, she feared. She saw a monu-
ment, two or three tombstones, some scores of
nameless but numbered sticks protruding from
the ends of mounds ! Why, ’twas a graveyard,
surely ? She derided herself, however, for her
fears. He was not dead—the System had some
curious vagaries—why should it not place a con-
valescent ward in the proximity of a beautifully-
situated cemetery ? The neighbourhood might
conduce to a speedier recovery. So the mother,
and not the woman, reasoned.
# * *
The grave-diggers—she took them for the
warders guarding the convalescents —looked at
the order—and at one another.
The elder moved forward, it might be ten paces
or so.
“ This way, mem,” he said. But he did not look
at her.
He stopped- at a half-filled and unmarked grave.
“This is No. 2594, mem ; buried yesterday.”
“ Dead ! ” she shrieked. They heard her down
at the boat.
“They’re mostly dead when we buries ’em,” the
convict-sexton answered sententiously.
# * *
It might have been ten minutes, it might
have been ten hours, before she came to herself
again. She was only brought back to conscious-
ness when a sexton threw his next day’s ration of
water in her face.
“The order says, mem, that yer to see him.
D’yer wan’ to ?”
“ Yes,” she said, with a sudden decision.
She saw him, as he lay in his shell, ghastly,
shirtless and shroudless, and with the ends of
great wheals (which ran over his shoulders)
exscoriating his chest. She lifted him up to kiss
him—and her hands felt the ridges of raised flesh
on his back.
“Be you his mother, mem?” It was the elder
sexton who spoke-
“ Yes,” she moaned.
“Wud yer like a death-mask, a clay pioter?
I ’udn’t do it ’cept for a mother. Anybody else
’ud split.”
“Yes, yes ; can you make one? Here’s money,”
she said, “ here’s money ! ” She freed one hand
to release her purse.
The man put it aside. “’Cos yer a mother,
an’ not fer money,” he said.
* * *
He sent his mate to the tiny cove to tell
the coxswain to come back for “the lady ” in a
couple of hours, and to bring a bucket of water
back. And while his fellow went on his message,
he himself dug some clay from a newly-sunk and
unoccupied grave.
“ I’ve done this afore,” he said, “for a mother. ’
He went into his hut, and when he returned, he
held in his hands a long piece of tin and a small
bottle of oil.
He was bending the former into an oval to
enclose the face, when the woman stayed his
hands.
“ Not his face,” she muttered, “ but his back.”
“ 1 don’ know as I can. I ain’t enough tin to
make a border.”
“ His shoulders ’ll do, his shoulders—there’s
enough for that.”
There was. And, while the younger man
puddled the clay, the elder fitted the tin frame to
the dead convict’s shoulders, and anointed them
with the oil, so that the clay should not adhere to
them. , ~
In an hour, exposed to the sun, the puddled
clay which had been poured into the frame, dried,
and the men lifted the cast carefully.
“ Give it to me!” said the woman. And she
took it tenderly, as though she were handling
a new-born babe.
“Oil it again, mem, an’ fill it with plaster of
Paris, an’ yer’ll ’av a death-mask complete in the
plaster,” said the elder man, as she left him, to
retrace her steps to the cove.
“ It’ll do as it is ! ” she answered.
# * *
Wrapped in the covering of her shawl,
she carried the thing to the Commandant’s cottage,
in both hands.
Dr. and Commandant Rossell received her.
“I have fixed up his release,” he said, “but
keep him quiet.”
“ Yes,” she responded calmly, “he’ll be quiet
enough now he’s released.”
She unfolded her shawl.
' 1 ‘ Do you know what this is, Doctor ? ”
“Clay?”
“ Yes, clay, and something else. It is the
' death-mask of Benjamin derricks, ray son.”
5 “A death-mask !” The honoured officer of the
Regime was really horrified. “ Is —he dead? ’
“Yes, my son is dead. This is his death-mask,
taken from his dead shoulders. Can’t you guess
why I’ve brought this to show you ?”
“N—o!”
“ Because he was your son as well as mine!”

Society
It will take all Lady Jersey’s tact to
arrange this matter of Lady . In London
distinguished men of all sorts were received in
her drawing-rooms, but they did not insist on
bringing their wives. How Her Ex. ought to act
in this “difficult case” is the most discussed
question in Sydney society. In England Lady
•Jersey could please herself; at Government
House, Sydney, she represents Queen Victoria,
whose Court, so Tennyson sings, “was pure.”
However, Tennyson is a poet, and must be allowed
a big margin.
* * *
The authorities of the P. and O.’s steamer
Valeria report that on last voyage there was no
amusement-committee formed on board the ship,
and that what fun took place was purely spon-
taneous. “This on a modern mail steamer is
rather a novelty ; tourists usually lay themselves
out for a complete breakaway from the strained
restraint of society forms,” remarks the shipping
report. The reason is subjoined :
At Brindisi on January 4 a large addition was made to
the number of passengers, among whom were included
Lord Wenlock (.the new Governor of Madras), Lady
Wenlock, Hon. Captain Lawley and Hon. Captain
Baring (aides-de-camp), and Lord Douglas Compton. The
new commander-in-chief of the China Station, Admiral
Sir F. Richards, also joined at Brindisi, the members of
his staff having come round from London in the ship.
Colonel Sir Fleetwood Edwards, assistant-secretary and
groom-in-waiting to Her Majesty the Queen, with Lady
Edwards, also embarked at Brindisi, on their way to
make a tour through India during the cool season.
You mustn’t kick up your heels in the presence
of a Governor of Madras, a groom-in-waiting to
her Majesty the Queen, and other such oppressive
personages, who have never in all their lives lived
in a free country like Australia. From travelling
with a cargo of the Anglo-Indian aristocracy,
good Lord deliver us, poor Australian nobodies !
# # #
A London paper informs us that “Captain
de Molyneux, late A.D.C. to Lord Hopetoun, has
to rejoin his regiment,” which reminds us that the
wegiment, you know, is a wemarkably useful
thing for fellah to fall back on, bai Jove! when
fellah gets in doose of mess. Captain Willoughby
come< out as “suite” to Lady Hopetoun, when
that firry young woman accompanies Lady Kin-
tore back co their bony—no, bonnie —earls in
April. One feels anxious to know which of the
Willoughby families this new Captain belongs to.
There are the something-or-other Willoughbys of
Somewhere, and the very ancient tribe of .Wil-
loughbys de Broke. No doubt this is one of the
de Brokes. Beerthh st was a broker, by the way.
« * *
A Northern paper says that The Bulle-
tin’s “ virulent and ungentlemanly attacks ” on
Gov. Carington in rc the Mount Rennie case
“ were never taken notice of by that nobleman.”
The fact that The Bulletin was the fly in Gov.
Carington’s ointment is amply evidenced by the
fact that the departed nobleman has devoted page
after page in his confidential despatches to the
Secretary of State for the Colonies to specifically
replying to The Bulletin’s indictment. The
despatches in question will, it is hoped, shortly see
light in the Commons.
# * *
The Bulletin thinks the proposal to
erect statues to defunct politicians in a populous
business thoroughfare a blunder. If Wentworth
and Martin have earned statues let them be
erected in suitable places : Wentworth’s, say, in
front of Sydney University ; Martin’s, in proxi-
mity to the new Law Courts. It may be interest-
ing to know when the statue craze will end, and
“who pays the piper?” In front of the new
Sydney Lands Office niches are left for 12 statues,
presumably 12 apostles of the province, beginning
with those of Sir Joseph Banks and Dr. Ludwig
Leichhardt. The artists are now at work on
statues of Burke and Wills, but we would like to
know which niche is destined for the “Judas
Iscariot of N.S.W. political life ?”
# # *
Whether a lady may visit an artist in his
studio alone has now been settled in the affirma-
tive by the Princess Louise. She went unattended
to the studio of the late Sir Edward Boehm, and
while there, strangely enough, the sculptor died.
Why should there have been any mystery about
the visit of the Princess Louise ? asks Reynolds.
A number of accounts, all varying, have appeared
with reference to the matter, but there seems to
have been no doubt that the Princess was with
Sir Edward Boehm when he died. There was no
inquest!
# * *
The late 11. C. Dean Fitzpatrick, of Mel-
bourne, was astounded one morning by a well-
known parishioner, a fairly wealthy man, waiting
upon him to procure a special marriage-license.
“Why, Mr. M ,” exclaimed the old priest,
“your wife is only a month dead!” “But,
Dean, isn’t she as dead as ever she’ll be, and. then,
there’s the childer want looking after ! ”
# # *
Never put money into your mouth. A
well-dressed girl sat in a crowded Newtown (Syd-
ney) ’bus, next to a malodorous Chinaman. The
conductor asked for fares. The Chinee placidly
pulled a “thrippenny ” out of his right ear. The
maiden drew a sixpence from between her teeth.
The conductor mechanically handed her back as
change the Chinkey’s threepenny-piece. The
sweet and innocent girl, from sheer force of habit,
placed it between her pearly teeth, until such
time as she could adjust her glove and dive for her
purse.
# * #
The lady who was so much given to per-
sonating a member of the family of a well-known
N.S.W. District Court Judge is at large again.
* * *
N.S.W. Minister of Lands Brunker is
about to visit the rabbit-infested district. If he
goes to Silverton he can kill the “ bunnies ” with
his umbrella from the Silverton tram. The last
time the present writer came that way the guard
killed three with stones while travelling 12 miles
an hour.
* # #
In Melbourne they are raking up all
about Sieber, the Hungarian. Eight years ago
Judge Williams, then green and raw under the
full-bottom, gave him 20 years for shooting at a
bar-girl, and wept, did the Judge, with a foolish
reference to the unhinging effects of the works of
Kotzebue.
# # *
“Andrew Menzies” complains in th e
daily press that 250 men have been employed for
the whole of two Sundays on certain Sydney rail-
way works, “ within sight of two churches.”
Works of necessity, no doubt. Mr. Menzies adds;
PLAYING WITH FIRE.
LORD OF THE MANOR: “See here, my friend, [?] set fire to that; the grass is very
dry and you might burn up the whole place ! ”
AMATEUR FIRE-FIEND: “What are you talking about? I’m lighting it with safety
matches!”
—“P.S. —I see that one of the men so employed
was killed last Sunday.” The lightning of an
offended God, no doubt! What are the odds
about Mr. Menzies being a clergyman?
# # *
This story is most probably a lie, but that
doesn’t matter much. Most amusing things in
this world are lies—it is only plain and solemn
statements such as sermons and gas-bills and
corns and lost dogs that are absolutely reliable.
A female physician has started business some-
where, but her door-plate doesn’t go into details
about her sex, and thus the casual visitor is apt
to be taken in. In fact, he is taken in frequently.
The last caller at the house was an itinerant mer-
chant. and when a tall, gaunt female in goggles
opened the door, he stated that he had called
round to offer the highest current price for the
doctor’s left-off trousers, and would also take his
old shirt at market rates. And though it is over a
week since that visit, he doesn’t know yet why
the lady drove the door up against his head, and
left him lying at the foot of the steps, a dis-
hevelled heap of unhinged intelligence and bashed
hat and broken merchandise.
# # #
On “Edinburgh’s” second visit to Aus-
tralia he gave Sarah S the cold shoulder, she,
in revenge, forming an alliance with the Duke’s
Equerry, Elliot Y orke. It was the common talk
of the day that Madame Celeste, the actress, who
visited Melbourne at the same time as the Duke,
had under, her elderly wing a jealously-guarded
“fairy ” who was an especial favourite of H.R.H.
The Duke was a constant visitor at the villa on
Carlton Gardens where Celeste lived, and he
made a special trip to Hobson’s Bay to see her off
for “ home ” by the mail-steamer a few days before
he left Australia.
#* * •
A member of the Prison Gates Brigade, at
Melbourne, has been arrested in the act, it is
averred, of burgling the female captain’s resi-
dence. As he had a prayer-book on him when
searched at the lock-up The Bulletin declines to
believe a word of the accusation.
# * *
A poll will be taken to determine whether
Sandhurst (Vic.) shall continue in that name or
revert to its old diggings cognomen of Bendigo.
The Bulletin backs “Bendigo,” which is the
original and unique name of a goldfield whose
memory will be immortal. Sandhurst is the name
of an English military-college to which no old
associations bind us.
* * *
Martin Place, in front of Sydney G.P.0.,
will be the new St. Martin’s Le Grand.
* # *
It is whispered in the mart that cheques
of the N.S.W. Roads Department have been
“ referred to drawer ” by another “unpatriotic”
bank.
* * *
A Bathurst (N.S.W.) jury the other day
gave in the following verdict to Judge Docker:
“ Not guilty, your Honor; we consider the
prisoner has been sufficiently punished already.”
Another —though a minor—illustration of the
unwillingness of jurymen to hand over a prisoner
to the tender mercies of a judge.
# # *
The excuse of the engraver who, to the
order of a trading firm, cleverly forged a number
of beer-labels, &0., and who was afterwards asked
by an employe of the same firm to forge a £l-
note plate, that “he never suspected roguery was
afoot until he saw a reward of £250 offered,'
reminds us of the clergyman who never hears a
“call’’from the Lord until he discovers that it
means monetary increase.
# # *
Sassiety in Wellington (N.Z.) has had its
nerves jarred by a story to the effect that the
female cook employed by one of the very best
families asked her mistress for a loan of the
morning paper, as her brother was now a member
of Parliament, and she wanted to read his maiden
speech.
# * *
Two vicious women, neighbours, appeared
before Police-Magistrate Lukin (Q.) for vile and
abusive language. Quoth Lukin: “It is useless
to fine you. You must be separated by distance.
I will not send you to gaol this time if you go into
the yard and toss up to see who’s got to shift,"
The well-known Jack Macnamara, formerly
“champion footballer of N.S. Wales,” and who
is now champion something or other in Queens-
land, offers the local Government for £25,000 the
secret of a consumption specific. Jack had better
invent a lymph to cure people of telling lies. It
is badly wanted in the North.
# # #
When Governor Hopetoun paid his first
visit to Bendigo, one loyal ass hung up, as his
share of the decorations, a card bearing the word
“ Welcome,” and a wreath which was constructed
by tying roses on to a rope. But unluckily the
roses blew away, and when the Governor appeared
the first thing that met his eye was a hangman’s
noose and a gladsome inscription which seemed to
invite him to put his head inside and be choked.
* * #
A local fashion writer makes one or two
suggestions “ to obviate spoiling a nice skirt by
crushing past other folk to reach one’s seat ” at
the theatre. The one obvious remedy is to take
the skirt off, and crowd past in a corset and a
petticoat, and then put the outer garment on
again when the seat is reached, but the writer
never even thought of this. There is a dreadful
lack of inventive power about the average female.
# # #
One of these things which man would like
to know, but which woman won’t explain, is how
she fastens on her hat. First of all she sticks the
article on her head, where the great mass of hair
is that she bought at the shop where they were
selling things below cost on account of the estab-
lishment having been struck by lightning,
filling the remaining vacancies up with
the bag her hat came home in. Then
she tilts it fiNward and drives it down over her
eyes, where it hangs on about as securely as a
man standing on the side of a wall. Next she
gets out a pin, one foot long, and drives it right
through the hat and through her head, and brings
it out the other side as if she rather enjoyed it
than otherwise. Then she smiles and goes away.
* * *
The statement which lately went the
round of the press that “the Princesses Maud
and Victoria of Wales have stamped their little
feet and made the startling announcement that
they intend never to marry ” is now in process of
correction. The real fact is that the two ladies in
question made a horrid crash with their extremi-
ties, for they each take No. 7 boots, and when
either of them puts her foot down it sounds like
a quartz-crushing plant which is doing its best to
produce a big dividend. This is not uttered,
however, in any disrespectful sense, for The Bul-
letin has a sincere respect for the maiden with
copious feet, and it recognises the fact that the
girl who is plentiful about the trotters and abund-
ant in the matter of boots does far more to
gladden a fellow’s heart in the thorny ways of
life than the female whose fairy footfall is as soft
as the tread of a kitten walking upon air. There
is comfort, too, in her large, ponderous hand, and
when trouble is all around and the atmosphere is
full of gloom her proprietor can trust her with his
sorrows, for there are brains and sympathy in her
wild, red head, despite the fact that her hair won’t
he down unless an iron stove or a fender is hung
on the end of every separate bristle. But the
truth is mighty and must prevail, for we have
seen the two ladies lots of times
and they have not got small feet, whatever may be
said to the contrary. If the British fashion-papers
state that the Princesses deposited their pedal
extremities resolutely upon the earth’s crust The
Bulletin is willing to accept the assertion —
otherwise wo must pass for a euchre.
# # *
“Professor Moestig, of Vienna, alleges
that he has discovered a certain cure for cancer,
but no details of the remedy have as yet been
made public.”— [Cable.)
The first sermon preached in the new
Anglican cathedral at Melbourne was delivered
by a Catholic, and once more the Terewth has got
left. One day when the building was near com-
pletion a holy father dropped in promiscuous like,
and addressed a short but impassioned discourse
to an audience of a dozen carpenters, several
polishers, a man with a pot of varnish, and an
artist in paving-stones. Then he walked away
with a look of unutterable contentment on his big
shorn countenance, for he had got ahead of the
beastly heretic, and bowled the schismatic out.
* * *
Mr. Lamrock, of Carcoar (N.S.W.), who
was lifted into the local corporation unopposed,
blankly told the ratepayers of that town that “ he
didn’t thank them for making him an alderman,
as he did not consider it an honor to be one.” The
climax of consistency would have been to kick
them all round.
* * *
A Campbelltown (N.S.W.) magnate, who
had endured bachelorhood for nearly half a
century, was married last week, and in the ex-
uberance of the blissful enjoyment of his honey-
moon at a certain seaside resort wired to a friend
as follows “ Arrived safely last night, never
was so happy in my life. Advise all young men
to follow my example.” After this matrimony
should boom at Campbelltown.
# # #
Sydney City Council has lately proclaimed
economy to the world. Thus does it practise the
same : First of all it proclaims a street; then it
paves it thoroughly; then digs it up to make
sewers; repairs it; digs it up afresh to lay water-
A Song.
Our lip we can’t help curlin’
At the medical profesh :
Sure, there’s Doctor Quack, of Berlin,
Always finding something fresh
To prevent mankind from croaking,
And to load himself with fame.
This is not a theme for joking,
But —we get there just the same.
Sure, the world is full of asses
Who will swallow any stuff
From a long-haired crank in glasses,
If he “ puts on jam ” enough.
And the Liar of the Cable
Lends assistance in the game,
But despite each learned fable
We must get there just the same.
Why, a Frenchman brewed elixir
For renewing youth in age
(Rone so impudently tricks yer
As the scientific sage).
But the stuff lie drew from rabbits
For that fizzle was to blame—
O ! it didn’t change his habits,
And he’ll get there just the same.
Here’s a man has struck a plan, sirs—
So the daily papers say—
To prevent the growth of cancers, -
And we only hope he may ;
All the things they’ll soon be healing
To which one can put a name—
Yet we’re haunted by a feeling
That we’ll get them just the same.
O confound all foreign “ masters ”
With a secret to disclose !
We believe in mustard-plasters,
And put tallow on our nose.
Let the savants of Vienna
Spin their narrative s so lame—
If we stick to salts and senna
We can get there just the same.

# * *
A remarkable instance of reformation on
the part of a character from whose antecedents,
and considering the company he kept, little go. cl
could have been expected, has been reported
recently from Maoriland. The individual referred
to, notwithstanding his previous career of noto-
riety, is now a respected employe of the Mosgiel
woollen factory, drawing his wages regularly
every Saturday, and living contentedly on his
humble, but honestly-eome-by screw, very much
to the surprise of some of his former associates,
who still carry on in their old way. To show the
great change that has come over the man who is
now earning his bread as a factory-hand, it is
only necessary to explain that lie was for several
years previously well-known to the police and
others as a member of Parliament.
# * *
A diminutive female enjoyed the pleasure
of sitting in a Melbourne Court last week, whilst
a witness stated that her (the d.f.’s) father told
him “ he had a dwarf daughter, and wanted to
get a husband for her.” The witness supplied
this long-felt want right off. Meeting a six-foot
young man of his acquaintance, he said, “Jones,
I’ve got a good thing on for you. A wife and
plenty of money.” Whereupon Jones jumped at
the good tiling, and because he carried out his
promise of “getting every note the old man
had,” somebody is worrying the happy pair with
a caveat. The course of true love never runs
smooth.
# * *
Two dozen English baronets, more than
three dozen knights, with scarcer kinds of noble-
men in due proportion, died during the year
ending December 31, since when the best blue
blood of the realm ha< been drying up like creeks
in drought-time. “Never within the memory of
this generation ” (wrote a gushing flunkey in the
World) “have so many parsons of note passed
away in the course of a twelvemonth.” A list of
the parsons scarcely disclosed a single name of
any note outside the columns of society papers.
Simply so many holders of titles had passed the
things on to their next descendants, or carted
them away to the cold grave.
That Austrian warsiup came on a voyage
o' discovery, with instructions to car y back the
Emperor’s wandering son, it possible. Has any-
body seen an archduke? —
“Inexpressibly sad” is the comment of
one of our religious papers on Bradlaugh’s abso-
lutely silent burial. This phrase seems to come
from the heart of the writer, and not to be the
mere commonplace of a paid reverend literary
mullah. The force of custom will also make
many laymen think it sad—for there are few of
us who could bury our kinsman or our mate with-
out a shred of ceremony. Nevertheless, many
thousands think a silent burial in the case of a
strong man like the great iconoclast magnificently
dignified, a solemn reverence for the majesty of
Death, infinitely more religious than the frigid
mummery of words repeated by the eight-aiid-
sixpenny cemetery-chaplain in a hurry to get
away to his tea. Yet one likes the French system
of an oration at the grave.
# * *
A Melbourne paper says that Broken Hill,
N.S.AY., being a place where no one lives from
choice, is practically destitute of leisured classes.
We have read of another place which will be, if
not already, full of leisured classes, but they don't
stay there from choice.
* # *
Dear Bulletin, —Fifteen years ago Mr.
Blank having tried many occupations and failed
in all repaired to (here may be inserted the name
of almost any moderately-sized town in Victoria)
and opened a boarding-school. As educational
establishments w re then scanty he soon got a fair
number of pupils and established a pretty large
connection. He is a harmless old noodle as fitted
to form a boy’s character as to-—translate Latin at
sight. But you must not imagine that Mr.
Blank’s seminary has not got a scholastic reputa-
tion. If I had his prospectus at hand I could tell
you how many of his boys have passed the matri-
culation examination. For this exam, is taken
as the criterion of the work of our pro-
fessional “ crammer.” Of his 30 boarders
ten are being prepared for matriculation ;
these ten are forced to work day and night
wrecking whatever brain-power they may have—
f >r Mr. Blank prides himself on the celerity with
which he “crams” his pupils. The remainder
are left to the tender mercies and meagre scholar-
ship of some junior master, who, having just
passed through the cramming ordeal, and being
without an idea of his own, and utterly inex-
perienced in teaching, is, according to Mr. Blank’s
idea, well fitted for the position lie holds. But
the ten candidates for matriculation are the main-
stay of the school —they are the prize-boys of the
establishment. On the annual speech-day their
wondrous deeds of scholarship will be recounted.
The local rag will compliment the town on pos-
sessing so ripe a scholar and so successful a
teacher as Mr. Blank. The fame of the estab-
ment increases - fresh pupils are enrolled, all
striving to matriculate. Poor but foolish parents
deny themselves of necessaries almost, in order
to send their sons to Mr. Blank’s seminary and
make “gentlemen” of them. Fanners’sons arc
transformed into needy clerks and embryo em-
bezzlers, ready to go clown on their knees and
kiss the feet of anyone who will give them a
pound a week. The army of bank-clerks and
other supercilious gentlemen is increased, the
unemployed become more numerous—and Mr.
Blank makes a fortune.—Yours, Nickleby.
A GOOD RIDER.
STATION-HAND (to Jackeroo): “Why, you ride like a gentleman from home.
JACKEROO (highly flattered): “Indeed, how— particularly ? ”
STATION-HAND: “With a leg each side of the horse.”
A persecuted Mount Gambler (LA.) law-
yer has just migrated to Adelaide. A Mount
Gam bier girl bore a child and elected Bluebag as
its father. Bluebag denied the allegation, and
defied the allegatoress. The girl-mother had no
corroborative testimony, and the case was dis-
missed. But public opinion somehow* went mad
over the verdict. There was held, on the Satur-
day night following, in Mount Gambier, a
“ Working Men’s Carnival.” First came a borno-
aloft effigy of Bluebag with a baby in his arms ;
next, a brass band ; and last, a procession of 4CCO
howling, hooting, horse-playing sons of Belial.
At an improvised gallows the effigy was hanged,
danced round, and yelled at, and finally burnt.
Then a big subscription was taken up for the girl.
That is why Mr. Bluebag lias left for Adelaide.
