A fashionable London marriage, just
mailed out, recals a well-known man of the past,
in Melbourne, Capt. Anderson, first husband of
“ Money ” Miller’s daughter, Charlotte, now and
for a long time, Mrs. (ex- Attorney-General)
Wrixon. The young lady married, to a wealthy
young squire, is Anderson’s daughter. He was a
queer fellow, and they say drove a stage-coach at
last, after showing himself air excellent amateur
actor iu Sir William Don’s company ; he was very
chummy with that Bart, and a good deal like
him. Anderson, they say, graduated as * Go-
vernors something.
# # #
A “Guelph Exhibition,” consisting of
“pictures and objects of interest connected with
the Royal Family,” is to be held shortly in Eng-
land. Queen Charlotte will be shown a shade less
short and snuffy than she was in real life, and the
forehead and chin of George 111. will slope back not
nuite so obliqu-ly as they did when he was alive.
No mention will be made, either, of the harem of
giantesses maintained by George L, whose mis-
tresses were all ladies of immense size and as ugly
as the menagerie of gorgons that howl round the
public-houses of Hades; and any little sketch of
the same monarch’s legitimate wife, during her 20
years’ imprisonment in Zell, will be judiciously
omitted. Also, George IV.’s partner in life will
not be delineated in the act of being thrown out
by her husband’s flunkeys on the eve of his corona-
tion—in fact, the exhibition will consist of the
tilings that are not there, rather than of those
that are, otherwise British loyalty will receive a
lot of damage. P.S. —We hope that this will not
lead to our being struck off the roll of J.P.’s.
# # *
Dear Bulletin, —I see you have a refer-
ence to the D. H. Deniehy memorial-stone. It
may interest you and others to learn that despite
all the blather and slobber we have had over this
matter, the monumental masons who contracted
to erect the stone have never been paid a penny
for it and have consequently become involved in
difficulties. Neither lias their assignee ever suc-
ceeded in getting a penny out of the guaran-
tors, and consequently the unfortunate wage-
earners have never been paid for working the
stone, &c. The amount of the contract was £4OO.
—A.A.K.
# #
The Wesleyan Advocate says that Gov.
Jersey “made a most favourable impression on the
clergy at his first levee.” Considering that in the
long run the individuals who appear to captivate
the clergy generally assume false beards ere en-
deavouring to skip to Honolulu in the lazarette of
a coal-ship, The Bulletin feels called upon to
offer its sincere condolences to his lordship on the
present trying occasion.
Y # *
A hideous outcome of the eternal Chinese
plague lias revea’ed itself at Cooktowu. ihere,
on the North Shore, a black gin, mottled with
innumerable sores, lies dying by inches of leprosy,
which she contracted from Chinese lepers formerly
quarantined there, and with wh rn she consorted.
Even this horror of black humanity is, howeve v ,
sought after and consoled by salacious niggers,
who cohabit with her, and afterwards perform
various menial offices for the whites of the town-
ship.
# * *
A huge Southern drapery concern which
is gradually developing into an immoral monopoly
has a staff of managers whose quality distinctly
averages on the side of the tyrannical. One of
the magnates connected with the concern has
opened a girl’s private letter, addressed to her in
care of the firm, and read out the contents in her
presence before a crowd of grinning parasites.

An English church paper denounces the
want of propriety made evident by a clergyman
who arrays himself in full canonicals and then
rides to church on a bicycle.
# * *
Paris L'lllustkation has just dealt
phrenology a blow in the epigastrium. It gives
about 200 hat-measurements of distinguished
politicians, soldiers, artists, actors, doctors, law-
yers, and what not in diagrams, proving that it is
impossible, from the shape of the head, to afford
any diagnosis of character or ability.

A North Queensland paper assures a
querist that “it is not true that the P.M. is an
habitual drunkard.” We’re sorry to hear it, and
deeply sympathise with the local boozers.
# * *
Mrs. Kenealy, lady doctor (no relation,
apparently, to the Wagga fat man’s legal
friend), writes a striking paper, in last National
Review, on "Woman’s Physique.” Logically
carrying out Darwinism, she irrefragably proves
that the high intellectual culture of women
deteriorates their offspring, giving actual examples
from her practice. Energy bestowed on one part
of the frame is taken from another; brains v. the
pelvicular, nothing more natural, as we see in the
vice versa action of the English “ aristocracy,” and
the weedy way in which they are training down.

One of the most valuable blocks of un-
occupied land in Sydney city, having big frontages
to Castlereagh-street and Elizabeth street, near
Hunter-street, and the greatest eyesore in the
neighbourhood, is owned by the wealthy absentee,
“ Bob ” Lowe —Viscount Sherbrooke.
# # #
C. C. Kingston, Q.C., the Adelaide
lawyer, occupied over 11 hours addressing the
Court for the defence in Morrison’s “ Aldine
History ” case. About £20,000 was indirectly in-
volved in the verdict, and the case is stated to
have been the longest on record. Kingston was
retained by a syndicate of subscribers who refused
to receive the book..
# # #
An Adelaide man started as a parson,
and for a time the business panned out well.
Then he took to temperance-lecturing, but
changed that for brokering on the Adelaide
Corner and made £50,000 during the boom. He
put half this into a suburban brewery and
“ specked ”on the Corner with the rest. Finally
he dropped the whole lot, and is now meditating
over his misfortunes at a salary of 30s. a week.

The Case of Mrs. Moeller.
GREAT EXCITEMENT IN SYDNEY.
In these days of talking dolls, telegraphs, ocean
cables, telephones, microphones and other electric
inventions, when the occurrences in one part of
the world are telegraphed to the farthest point
thei’efrom in one day, and when a traveller makes
a journey right round the globe in 03 days, it
takes something out of the common to startle a
community, but the late experiences of Mrs.
Laure Mueller are enough to startle the dead.
Mrs. Mteller, who resides at Newtown N.S.W., is
a native of Germany and came to this country
some years ago. She had suffered from Liver and
Kidney complaints for years, and about three
mouths previous to the present time was com-
pletely prostrated and unable to work ; dropsy of
the ankles and lower limbs having set in. We
diagnosed the case ourselves and can truthfully
assert that it was a cas ■ of advanced dropsy due
to relaxation and enfeebleraent of the membranes
of the Liver and Kidneys, which permitted the
waste fluids to exude through them into the
muscular tissue. We advised the use of Clements
Tonic and it was taken for some weeks and has
resulted in a complete cure. Mrs. Moeller herself
thus describes her ease :—“ Stanley Laundry,
Georgina-stieet, Newtown, N.S.W. I can with
confidence bear witness to the wonderful efficacy
of Clements Tonic in curing Liver and Kidney
disease. I have long been a victim to indigestion
(caused by liver complaint) and an affection of the
kidneys. I suffered terribly from heidaches,
pains in the back and side, with obstinate o:n-
-stipation. No one except myself knows what my
sufferings have been. I was always tired and
languid, with no ability or inclination to perform
my daily work. My tongue was furred in a
morning, and a sour taste in the mouth, and fre-
quent vomiting ; one day I would be pretty well
and the next very distressed or completely pro-
strated with a very confirmed languor. This went
on until I was so ill I was completely done up
and quite unable to move. My ankles being
swollen as thick as my thighs, I consulted
physicians all of whom pronounced the case one
of Dropsy, they variously ascribed the cause to be
heart, liver, or Bright’s disease.- The kidneys
were undoubtedly diseased, because' the swollen
ankles when pressed with the finger would leave
a dent in the place for days, and it was easy to
press the end of the finger an inch deep, when the
hole would not fill up for four days. I had heard
of the wonderful cures effected by Clements Tonic
and commenced its use. I took three bottles and
the swelling in the ankles commenced gradually
to subside, I continued the medicine and am now
completely cured ; my ankles are their usual size,
I have no aches nor pains and can do a good day’s
work with pleasure, my tired aching feelings have
all disappeared, and I am confident I have been
rescued from an untimely grave by the wonderful
virtues Clements Tonic. My address is at the
head of this letter, and I will reply to any com-
munications if a stamped envelope is sent with
inquiry, as I consider it my duty to publish a
record of my wonderful cure. Yours very grate-
fully, Laupe Mocller.
Mrs. Moeller’s case is only a prototype of many
others in this country. She obtained a cure by
persistent treatment, and this is the only way
chronic disease can be cured, a half-hearted treat-
ment is never satisfactory, you must continue the
remedy for a considerable time if you want to cure
long standing cases, the tendency after a certain
age is always towards decay, therefore if debility
lias been eating its way into the system for
months or years, it is very unreasonable to except
one or two bottles to restore perfect health, all
bad cases requiring protracted treatment.

M.L.A. (N.S.W.), for £lOOO damages in respect
of a certain libel contained in an article pub-
lished by Sydney Truth and said to have been
written by Schey. As it would be an unpardon-

Minister Drunker (N.S.W.) has approved
of the forfeiture by the Urana Land Board of
a selection taken up by a Miss M‘Connell, on
the ground of alleged dummyism, because her
father had provided her with the funds to pay the
fees. Miss M‘Connell is “ sweet seventeen,” and
her father would be considered by most people to
be her natural backer. Her selection would pro-
bably have been her dot for the man who selected
her. Is the inference to be drawn that, had Miss
M‘Coimell borrowed the money from a strange
man, the Urana Land Board would not have
forfeited the -selection? Or, should she have'
become a usurer’s client ?
The trimmest of trimming newspapers is
the Melbourne Age. One day last month we
were favoured with a leader in support of Parkes’
action in dismissing the Bourke J.P. for reflecting
on the character of the Prince of Wales. Last
week we had an unexceptionable article on Aus-
tralian Nationalism, apropos of the Carington
lecture. Nothing delights the Age more, than to
fire off a leader that would astonish old Dr. Lang,
if he were here, after indulging in weeks of
twaddle about royalty, loyalty and the colonial
possessions of Britain. David Syme is making
ready to get down on the pleasantest (monetary)
side of the political fence.

A humble, plain, ordinary picnic, that
had no points which could distinguish it from any
other picnic, came off lately in a back-blocks town,
and the local paper published three columns of
burning frenzy about the event. A small sample
follows :
“ The party resolved themselves into family and
friendly groups in picturesque confusion about the
grounds, on the green carpet of which the unfortunate
carriers of the many ‘ apoplectic’ hampers (with undis-
guised gladness) quickly deposited their burdens, under
whose weight they had groaned more severely than they
have hitherto probably done under that incubus on the
human conscience, the vicarious accountability for origi-
nal sin. The explanation may not he difficult of search ;
for whereas the former was an undoubted corporeal tangi-
bility, the latter, to many minds, presents itself as a mere
theological abstraction. But whatever may be the true
philosophy of this hotly-disputed and seemingly insolvable
crux in polemics ; or whatever of real scientific value in
the forcible deductions drawn by Macaulay from the con-
sideration of cognate theses in his masterly essay on
Milton—a literary gem of most exquisite splendour,
which every Briton should highly prize - the fact remains
that limits of space and the occasion alike forbid the
elaboration of such matters in this article.”
And yet all he wants to say is that Smith humped
the grab.
Henniker Heaton lately related to the
Royal Colonial Institute the old, old chestnut
about the semi converted blacks who jibbed on
Christianity and its missionaries with : “You gib
it no more rum ! No more rum, no more bally
hallelujah !” Whereat the podgeys of the insti-
tute guffawed loudly, as if the superior races of
whites never showed the same sort of gratitude
and flunkeyism for favours to come. Think hard
over the subject and you will find that the white
man’s motto, through every grade, is : “ Plenty
pray, if you gib it ’bacca.” The Uriah Heeps of
society and other varieties of artful young men
are not slow at discovering that now-a-days if
they want to acquire and keep a situation it is
advisable for them to join the Y.M.C.A., or the
Evangelistic choir, or some other religious coterie
that works as do all the godly rings, on mutual
scratch-me-back principles. It is the very same
slimy spirit that impels Burglar Bill and the other
cracksmen to join the Salvation Army, as causes
Adolphus de Pitsuout to worship at the tedious
socialities of Government House. A premium
has always to be paid for prospective benefits
either a cash bonus or a servile hypocrisy .that
only lasts till “ you no gib ic ’bacca.”
If asked to drink, call for Wolfe's Schnapps.
The Watts River (Vic.) is to have its name
changed to Maroondah on Peb. 18th, when that
charming trout stream will be solemnly dedicated
to the service of the Melbourne Water Supply.
The original Watts has no solid claim to immor-
tality. He neither discovered nor improved the
little shining river. All he did was to fall into it
and get drovyned, long,Tong ago.
When Disraeli first appealed to an English
constituency at Wycombe Tie addressed the free
and independent electors from the doorway of the
local pub., and by his side was . a wooden lion
painted red, with a flaring tail and an eye like the
rising sun. It was a rowdy gathering, and
Disraeli nearly collapsed in, trying to make
himself heard : hut at last, finding it alp to no
purpose, begirded up his loins for one vociferous
whoop, and, grasping the lion by the scalp, he
screamed forth : “Thus, in time to come, will I
also grasp the British lion.” Disraeli is dead now,
and the old lion lies doubled up in a stable on
Baron Carington’s estate ; with his hide washed
off through rain and frost, and his legs broken,
and his tail reduced to a mere skewer.
Advfc. in the Parisian papers :
All persons of the female sex, between the ages of six-
teen and forty, who will bring a long hair from their head
to the Baron Arnold de Fortville (who is well-known) will
receive five francs on condition that the said hair he from
the head of the person who brings it.
This was no take in, for the Baron, a great
original, wished to .form a collection of women’s
hair wherewith to make himself a bed-pillow.
He had already amassed a considerable amount,
when, on account of being greatly ridiculed, he
was obliged to leave Paris. The Baron went from
Paris to London, Rome, St. Petersburg, Vienna,
Madrid, Lisbon, Stockholm, gathering up Italian,
Russian, German, English hair, &e., to keep
company with the French hair. After travelling
some time the Baron returned to Paris with his
precious pillow filled with the spoils of so many
lovely women, and certainly more than 100,000
had contributed to satisfy this strange whim, and
at present the Baron sleeps and dreams every
night with his famous pillow.
The “loyal” editor, who fills his paper
with joyous knowledge about the shirts and hair
and hash of the aristocracy, is a weariness and a
desolation. lie suffers from an incurable con-
vict:on that the solar system revolves around the
pimple on a monarch’s spine, and that the axis of
the earth passes through his big, coarse feet, and
that his sore ear is the principal decoration of
Paradise ; and when some superfluous potentate,
or his son, or his eleven hundredth uncle is
graciously pleased to pick his back tooth with a
carving-knife, he telegraphs the information all
over the earth. Also he cables about the oint-
ment that was put on Brown’s legs, and publishes
an appeal to the nation to bear up and meet mis-
fortune with Christian heroism when Battenberg’s
dog’s brother has a cold ; and if the royal dust-
man loses his deranged aunt he states that an in-
scrutable Providence has been pleased to inflict a
heavy sorrow upon the nation. The following,
from Vanity Pair, is a decent sample of this most
awful sort of slush :
I notice that my contemporaries say nothing of the fact
that Prince George did really dine with the Prince of
Wales on the night of the first levee. Prince George
arrived at Marlborough House from Portsmouth at
7 o’clock on that evening. Yet when Vanity Pair said
beforehand that Prince George would dine with his father
on this occasion Vanity Fair was violently contradicted in
the usual fashion.
And yet there was no "wild crash and row among
the stars, neither did the sun tear its hair, or the
planetary system break its neck as it bolted in
affright, nor did the volcano and the thunderbolt
and the great sea-serpent burst forth. In fact,
nobody cared whether George raged like a de-
vouring pestilence among his father’s hash, or
whether he ate a pie under a tree, or consumed a
carrot on top of the great pyramid, or assimilated
stolen hen down a coal-mine. Nobody cared,
either, if he arrived from Portsmouth at 7 o’clock,
or if he got back from Gehenna in a hansom cab
at a-quarter past two, and had to sit on the door-
step till daybreak, or if he emerged from a hearse
exactly at noon. Neither does it interest anybody
whether Vanity Fair foresaw that he was going
to feed with his ancestor, and even if that paper
had learned in advance that he would wash him-
self before dinner, or that he would lose his shirt-
button down his back, the sun would probably
rise next morning in the same miserable, old, con-
ventional way. The paper that worries over
things like these ought to buy a rattle and some
toffee, and then get washed by its mother and put
to bed.
“Becoming respectable ” means abandon-
ing your own vices and taking up those of your
friends.
If your liver troubles you take Wolfe’s Schnapps.
WHAT EXPERIENCE TEACHES
IS BEST.
There is something to consider in the daily ex
penses for the household necessities, which in th
aggregate of the year amount to a good deal
The ethics of buying and selling appear to be foi
the vendor to get all he can, and the purchaser to
see that he don’t get too much. But what is too
much ? It is almost a profound question, and
presents the strange paradox that the desert in
many things is often the cheapest. Certainly this
is so in food, when health is involved, and in
remedies which restore health after if has been
impaired. It is certainly so in clothing; for a
cheap suit that will hardly last one is dearer than
the one which will last two seasons, the difference
in price being reasonable. So in shoes and the
like. Recurring to the items of food .and health,
undoubtedly the most important, it is found that
villainous adulteration is what renders cheaper
much that is sold, and men are known to
have spent thousands to be cured of disease, have
suffered years of agony, and have trifled away
their substance on worthless remedies. That
which is testified to by thousands as being an
absolute cure, and permanently efficacious, is
cheap at any price in comparison v-ith such as
have no virtue, and which prolong suffering. A
case in point is the following: “New Bloom-
field, Pa., April 20,18SG. The Charles A. Vogeler
Co., Baltimore, Md. Gentlemen, —Eor more
than thirty years I had been afflicted with rheu-
matism so severe Iliad to use morphine to secure
rest at night. Spent hundreds of dollars with
physicians and for remedies without benefit.
Five years ago I tried St. Jacobs Oil, and it
effected an entire and permanent cure. I have
not been troubled with it since. Cold or damp
weather does not affect me at all. I desire to
give it my unqualified endorsement. — J. E. Bon-
sall, clerk of the several Courts of Perry County,
Pa.” The point here is not so much what Mr.
Bonsall paid for the great remedy of pain, for
the price is a mere bagatelle, but that he was
permanently cured after 30 years’ suffering. Of
course the poor must count the cost in everything ;
but they should reckon on the sound basis that
that which is bad is worthless, and that which is
the best and will cure, and stay cured, is cheap at
any price.
SERIOUS FLOOD IN SOUTH AUS-
TRALIA.
DISASTROUS LOSSES.
By recent advices from South Australia we are
informed that, under date of 13th October, Mr.
H. 0. Merrett, of North Creek, Warrina, writes
as follows to Messrs A. W. Dobbie and Co., of
Adelaide, which is sure to prove of interest to a
large number of our readers ; “ Dear Sir, —I am
writing to tell yon about a Water bury Watch that
I had in use for about twelve months. I lost it in
the water on Easter Monday last when there was
a very big flood here, and it was found again on
the 1st of October, and three inches deep in the
ground. When I got it I wound it up and it has
been going ever since, and keening splendid time,
so I thought that I would let you know, as it may
be something in favour of the watches. There are
any amount of people about here that know this
for a fact. If you would like to see the watch let
e know, and I will send it down to you.”
AT A TRIFLING COST
you can obtain a Water bury Watch, which rarely
gets out of order, and never const!tues, itself a
permanent tax on the owner. In the event of
injury befalling it, by taking or sending it to the
Company’s Repair Depot, 56, Margaret-street, it
can be efficiently repaired at a nominal cost,
ranging according to the extent of damage, from
6d to 2s 6d, but never exceeding the latter amount
unless by mutual consent.
The genuine Waterbury may be obtained from
any respectable storekeeper or dealer. Price,
series “E” (the old favourite), 13s. 6d. ; series
“J” or “L” (the new marvels), with enamelled
dials, 22s. 6d. each.

We notice with pleasure that the Equitable
Mortgage, Discount, and Deposit Bank, of 4
Moore-street, Sydney, has recently been incor-
porated, and is now opened for business. This
bank has been formed for the purpose of assisting
those who may at any time be in pecuniary diffi-
culties and wish to avoid paying the excessive in-
terest which is too often charged. The bank has
secured the able services of Mr. Henry A. Birk-
myre, which should be a guarantee that clients will
be fairly dealt with. In these days of extortion, the
above institution should about fill a long-felt
want.*

POLITICAL POINTS
Sir Thos. MTlwraith is a maker of epi-
grams. One of his latest is—“ The flourishing
colonial industry of borrowing money.”
;The Prince of Wales’ appeal, to Austra-
lians “ not to forget the land from which they
sprang ” reminds one of how gallantly the Wettin
family stick to their native Yarmany.
The Indian Government has got an idea
that Australia objects to “ Indian natives of bad
character ” coming to Australia. All black
Indians are of bad character, so far as Australia
is concerned.
The new W.A. loan (of which £900,000
only is to be devoted to railway purposes) will
double the indebtedness of that province—
making the public debt £2,700,000, or about £6O
per head of the present population.
Manifest signs of a N. S. W. general elec-
tion are now visible. The Government, not the
Railway Commissioners, has called for tenders for
175,000 tons of steel rails, to be made in N.S.W.
out of N.S.W. minerals and with fuel raised in
N.S.W.
Attorney Levien, M.L.A., says that he
“ would not be seen in a dog-kennel with some of
the men that honest old Parkes has been associated
with.” The Bulletin has yet to meet the man
so dishonest that no lawyer will have him as a
client.
“Parkes, the political Colossus,” says a
N.S.W. Foreign-trade paper. Yes, says The
Bulletin, the Colossus of Rhodes-and Bridges.
The old humbug who has kept office by strang-
ling the Local Government movement for twenty
years.
The cableman says that Dillon and O’Brien,
who have surrendered to undergo their six months’
gaol, will be released long ere the full time has
expired. Likely enough, as Salisbury will use
every possible means to cripple Parnell and divide
the Irish.
The Irish Parliamentary party having en-
couraged Parnell to defy Nonconformist cant after
the divorce exposure, now find that he is prepared
to defy bishops, priests and patriots paid and un-
paid. Parnell has hold of the money-bags and
will not unloose his grasp.
Sir Hercules Robinson in a letter to
Parkes:—“As in Canada, the United States,
and even in these [British] islands, there must be
in Australia a uniform tariff against the outside
world, and unrestricted trade internally and
coastwise throughout the whole Federal domi-
nion.”
Melbourne Age seems to be doubtful
whether war is not “ a divinely-appointed means
of working out natural selection, by showing
which party can kill the other off,” and seeks to
apply such a conclusion to the industrial war now
raging between squatters and shearers. Firestick
philosophy ?
In the democratic constituency of Colling-
wood (Vie.), 75 persons—shareholders in a
brewery limited liability co.—claimed to be
entitled to electors’ rights by virtue of their scrip.
Although opposed by the registrar acting under
instructions from the Chief Secretary’s office, all
the claims were allowed !
Young Nick Willis, M.L.A. (N.S.W.)
charges the prophet Egeson with having driven
hundreds of thousands of pounds of capital out
of the country by predicting drought and disaster.
The Prophet, in reply, is understood to allege that
Willis made a lot of capitalists clear out by in-
citing the crowd to take up arms.
One of the demands that Townsville
Separationists made to Premier Griffith was
that the far Northern colony should possess the
full control of all its alien labour. So the black
question—which belongs to United Australia and
not merely to North Queensland —is not so dead
as its secret friends pretend it to be ?
J. B. Patterson, late autocrat of the Vic-
torian Customs, a gas bag with the “cheek” to
say that “we want thinkers and not talkers”—
was to be entertained at a public banquet at New-
castle recently, said the cable. It would be in-
teresting to know who paid for Patterson's little
feed, and for the cablegram appertaining thereto.
The revenue of West Australia for the
last three years has shown steady improvement:
1888, £357,003; 1889, £375,329; 1890, £414,313.
During last year a lump sum of £27,000 was paid
in by the Hampton Plains syndicate, a London
co. who annexed 216,000 acres at 2s. 6d. per acre.
The cash was paid, and the syndicate have the
deeds!
“ Mr. A. J. Balfour, Chief Secretary for
Ireland, has,” it is cabled, “received the chgree
of Doctor of Laws from the Dublin University.
He met with a remarkable ovation.” So does the
Czar when he goes to Poland; so does the Kaiser
when he visits Metz. So, we might add, does
Burdett Smith whenever he honours Smithtown
with his presence.
Erastua Wiman writes powerfully in"last
North American Review, on the Canada ques-
tion —points out that a frontier of 4000 miles is
hampered and both nations annoyed by the
tariffs. When Sir John Macdonald passes off the
scene, all will be squared up. Canada’s trade
with the U.S., both in imports and exports,
greatly exceeds that with England.
Messrs. Young and Vivian, M.P.’s for the
Manning and Hastings, announce to the electors
of Taree that “Protection is dead.”
Two ghosts crawled from their graveyard nook,
And on the living world did look,
When, lo ! they heard a tread.
Beneath whose might the land fair shook—
One spectre said to his brother spook,
“That there live thing is dead.”
Queensland separationists are whooping
over the analysis of export returns. During
1890, Rockhampton exported goods to the value
of £2,057,319 —principally Western wool, Mount
Alorgan gold, and Lake’s Creek meat; Towns-
ville, £1,214,419, almost entirely wool and gold ;
and Brisbane (least of the three), £1,145,218. As
Brisbane outbulks in population Rockhampton
and Townsville combined, the significance of the
analysis is heightened.
An Orange (N.S.W.) press contributor
loudly declares that “ Protection is dead to the
world.” What world? Take out of the world
Protectionist Germany, Russia, France, Italy,
United States,- Queensland, New Zealand, Vic-
toria, South Australia, China and a few other
small places, and what remain ? Why, Hingland
and Orange (N.S.W.), which latter empire
manages to return a Freetrader to Parliament
by a majority of several votes.
Christian consistency ! The Church of
England Diocesan Synod, Melbourne, which de-
voted weeks upon weeks to synodical wrangling
about sectarian matters, has decided that the time
is not opportune for agitating the question of
religions instruction in State schools. The
Bulletin agrees with the latter conclusion, and
further believes that the time never will be oppor-
tune; but, from a church point of view, the in-
consistency is there all the same.
Secretary Houghton, in his annual report
to the N.S.W. Trades and Labour Council, sum-
marises the lessons of the strike : —“ It has taught
us to count well our chances before engaging in
such another conflict; to remove instanter the
dire scourge of sectionalism which has too long
existed among the unions in this country—nay, in
this city ; to marshall our forces for the ballot-box,
both parliamentary and municipal, for we are
devoid of friends in either senate; to amass a good
fighting fund, and not depend upon public sym-
pathy ; to avoid higher entrance-fees, so that the
unions will be open to all.”
Lord Jersey professes to be profoundly
struck by the surprisingly tall stature of N.S.W.
prominent public men—Parkes, Darley, Abbott,
Dibbs, &c. Which reminds us that Aristotle laid
down the rule that “all tall men are fools,” and
Bacon, in his 17th apophthegm, observes : “ My
Lord St. Albans said that Nature did never put
her precious jewels into a garret four stories high,
and therefore that exceeding tall men had ever
very empty heads.” Alessrs. Crick, Alelville, and
many other “pony ” public men are pleased to
agree with My Lord St. Albans.
It is significant that all the Australian
newspapers which have consistently supported
Irish Home Rule from really democratic motives,
are unswerving in their allegiance to Parnell ;
while the money-policy journals which, when the
Redmonds came here ridiculed the Irish cause,
and afterwards slewed round out of politic defer-
ence to growing public opinion, now jump upon
Parnell under the delusion that he is a crushed
man. All tha Irish-Australasian papers stick
to Parnell, some openly and others by discreet
abstinence of adverse comment.
The China Mail says :—“ It must be re-
■ membered that the Australians hate the Lascars.”
No, we don’t. We love them—at a distance. As
interlopers amongst local white labour, we don’t
want them at all. It is no question of love or
hate, but merely one of protecting the right of our
whites to live up to a free man’s standard. The
Lascar is a fine fellow —in his native lair. As
an alleged navigator, he’s a slummocker, a
fraud, a coward, and a willing sacrifice to the
boss sweaters. As a landsman immigrant, he is
an eyesore and a human cockroach.
The reason for Secretary Pulsford’s re-
tirement from the N.S.W. ' “ Ereetrade and
Liberal ” Association may be summed up in one
word—Cash. In 1888 the society was formed,
and a wealthy few guaranteed secretarial and
other salaries and expenses for three years.
Pulsford started with £5OO a year, and was
assisted by a clerk and office-boy, costing
another £3OO a year. The three years guaranteed
have elapsed, and the guarantors will not renew
their responsibilities. So Pulsford retired from
what the daily press called his “ honorary ”
billet, and the F. and L. A., which has loafed all
along on Mayor Burdekin, who practically gave it
free board and lodging, is bankrupt.
Dear Bulletin, —I am distraught from the
effects of reading a S.M. Herald editorial on the
American fiscal policy. You can’t “ have ’ these
Ereetrade acrobats. If a country like England
is Ereetrade, they trot out its imports, and say
that an excess of imports over exports is a sure
sign of achieved and increasing prosperity. If a
country like America is Protectionist, they trot
out the exports, and ask—“ How is it that the ex-
ports are so much less than the imports ? This
means blue ruin.” You see, they can’t be beaten
at flinging back-somersaults. Witli England,
surplus imports means millennium; with America,
excess of imports means sheol. I will now adjourn
to drink confusion to fiscal conjurors.—Yours, &c.,
The Dipsomaniac.
Sir Hercules Robinson to Sir Henry
Parkes: “Please remember I am now an idle
man, or comparatively an idle one, and if I can be
of any service to yon here, either personally or
in regard to p iblic matters, you can at all times
command me.”
It is the same old style and plan
“Yes, I’m your crony ;
‘ Remember, I’m an idle man ’ —
App’int Maloney!
You know whose writing this you scan?
Your brother? That’s he.
‘ Remember, I'm an idle man ’ —
App’int your Patsy 1”
We wonder whether Sir Hercules would take the
N.S.W. Agent-Generalship?
Personal Items.
Ex-Bank Manager Bennett’s shortage is
now totted up to £30,000.
Earl Jersey opens his addresses in Exeter
Hall style : “Dear friends.”
Henry George is suffering slightly from
aphasia, or paralytic loss of voice.
The estate of Bendigo millionaire Watson
looks as if it would be a fat oyster for the lawyers.
Sydney Evening News is moving for a
new trial of its libel-action against the Austra-
lian Star.
It is understood that a certain Sydney
statesman will (0.D.V.) attend the Federation
Conference.
Mrs. Wyndham, widow of the famous
N.S.W. wine-grower of Dalwood, Branxton, died
last week of heart-disease.
A newspaper named Truth lias gone bung
after a brief life in Adelaide. It was regarded as
too much of an innovation.
Reynolds’ Newspaper is firmly under the
impression that Lord Carington has, for the last
five years, been Governor of Victoria.
A Cooktown editor describes his hated
local rival in big type as “a prime sniveller,
driveller, liar, and uneducated blackguard.”
Captain Rossi in his latest letter to Arch-
deacon Pownall suggests that it would be unbe-
coming if the oooulus episcopi should squint.
The largest land-owner in the world is
Russia’s Czar, whose private landed estate com-
prises an area nearly equal to the whole of France.
Those who wonder why Lord Carington
so much affected Sir Henry Parkes forget that
“the Baron ” was a difehing Corinthian in his
day.
Bill Nye says Battenberg bolted away
from home because ma-in-law used to employ him
to rub her Imperial back when she had the
lumbago.
G. D. Clark, Sydney’s boss prohibitionist,
has just lectured on “ the comic side of the alcohol
question.” The ginger- beer question, you see,
has no comic side.
Dr. Mueller’s strychnia-injection treat-
ment has up to date proved itself a snakebite
specific, no life having been lost in cases where it
has been applied.
The leader of the Adelaide bar is Mr. J-
H. Symon, Q.C., a ginger-thatched, heavily-put-
together “Australian Deutscher.” His is the
thunderous style of oratory.
The Prince of Wales is still “looking
after” little Lord Dudley, and graciously request-
ing that that pigeon shall be asked to join the
Royal party at country houses and so forth.
Lady Kintore, who, after all, is coming
back to South Australia, will arrive in Adelaide
just in time to meet her husband on his
triumphant return from his transcontinental
picnic.
The Chilian fighting centres much around
Iquique, the Nitrate Port, where trade is stopped.
This is the grand fount of the riches of Col.
North, the London Nitrate King, who may be
put in a hole by the war.
Chief Justice Way (S.A.) generally lias
the misfortune to take murder-cases in South
Australia. Judge Boucaut has never sentenced a
man to death since his occupancy of the Bench ;
neither has Judge Bundey.
B. D. Morehead, ex-Premier of Queens-
land, and his family have arrived safely in Eng-
land. At the Brisbane end of the cable wait
bulls’-ears listening for the first echoes of the
expected Alount Alorgan re-boom.
The Bathurst Advocate says that Mr.
Charles Lyne has written a life of Sir Henry
.Parkes, which will presently be published in Lon-
don. Mr. Lyne, formerly "of the S.M. Herald
staff, was appointed by Premier Parkes to the
secretaryship of the Public Works Commission at
£7OO a year.
Joshua Ives, Mus. Bac., who occupies the
Chair of Music at Adelaide University, has been
so successful an operator on the Stock Exchange
that there was recently some talk of reducing his
annual salary of £6OO. Josh averted the calamity
by subscribing to his own “ Chair.”
Mr. Landseer, a S. Australian M.P., once
said of his legislative colleagues “It is well
known that most members use their free railway-
passes in order to run over to Alelbourne and
plunge into its wickedness. You needn’t laugh.
I know, because I have been there myself.”
Millionaire Tyson employs four men per-
manently at his Felton station to look after
30.000 siieep; three men at Alount Russell for
30.000 more sheep ; and three men at Wyobie for
39.000 sheep. Here are areas of land capable of
sustaining in comfort 10,000 peasant families
given over to one hungry millionaire and 10 bush-
exiled employes.

Robert Louis Stevenson says that “the
last eater of long-pig (human flesh) in Nukahiva,”
whom he met in Anaho, possesses a “face rugged,
astute, formidable, and with a certain similarity
to Air. Gladstone’s, only for the brownness of the
skin,” &c. We can imagine the lay-reader of
Hawarden paraprasing the old preacher’s confes-
sion— “But for the grace of God, there goes the
cannibal chief, William Ewart Gladstone.”
General Sherman has died, aged 70. His
claim to immortality is the march “from Atalanta
to the sea,” at Savannah. He claimed the idea of
it, too, but Grant insisted that it was part of his
plan as Generalissimo, a point never cleared up,
but probably Grant was right. However, Uncle
Billy Sherman was the man to plunge with an
army of 60,000 into the enemy’s country, cut off
all his communications, and work on to success.
Captain Acland-Hood, aide-de-camp
and brother-in-law to Hopetoun, who talks of
standing for one of the English counties at
next general election, is an especially small-
headed and small-featured young man, whose
clothes are a lot better than his tailor. He is the
sort of candidate who gets off one speech by rote,
and has to begin again at the beginning every
time bis flow of eloquence is interrupted by a
rotten egg.
The best-preserved Melbonrnite is barris-
ter J. J. Walsh, husband of John Pasco Eawkner’s
widow. He has hardly changed since 30 years
ago, when he was a moving spirit in the Land
Convention, with Wilson Gray, Ebenezer Syrne,
Dr. Owens, Don, Crews, and Alderman Keeley.
Keeley was good on bulls, but Ambrose Kyte
made the best when he referred in Parliament to
a rumour as a Brutus Fulcrum (Brutum Eulmen).
One Curtain went, if possible, one better in a
Town Hall meeting with “ Mr. Chairman, why
these dissensions ? Are we not working for the
same Gaol ? (Goal.)
When Sir George Grey was Governor of
New Zealand, during his first term of that office,
he lent the Government of the day £lO,OOO to buy
native land for settlement in Hawkes Bay. The
Governor intended the land to be settled in
moderate-sized farms, but the Provincial authori-
ties, who got hold of the land after purchase,
simply divided it among themselves and rela
tions (the clique generally known as the Twelve
Apostles, Judas included), land worth at that time
£2 an acre being disposed of in large blocks for ss.
and even less. Afterwards, when Sir George had
removed to Cape Colony, the Government of N.Z.
repaid the loan without interest, and deducted
£7O charged by the banks for exchange !
Lady Florence Dixie on the O’Shea
situation: “I am not aware that at present
women are slaves and that man’s law has laid
down the rule that a woman may have no friend
outside her husband. It is a law that would
vanish with woman’s education and power of
freedom. The conclusion I have arrived at after
reading the trial is, that Mrs. O’Shea was for-
saken by her husband, and made friends with Mr.
Parnell. Treated by her husband as a mere beast
of burden, Air. Parnell treated her as a friend. I
recognise no law but the true law of justice. One
man appreciated her, the other treated her only
as the law regards her—as his slave. If Mr.
Parnell has sinned, let the greater number of our
politicians and statesmen retire from public life,
for they are worse than he.”
William M’Kinley, law-reporter and genial
Bohemian, died shortly after midnight on Satur-
day last. M‘Kinley was admitted to the Bar in
1867, and for a quarter of a century was the chief
law-ieporter for the Argus. His knowledge of
“case” law was wide, and his memory one of the
world’s wonders. It was his habit to take scanty
shorthand notes on scrappy tags of paper—any
irregularly-shaped “ tear-off ’ that came handy—
or on his shirt-cuffs, until “ M'Kinley’s notes”
passed into proverbial currency among the ink-
slinging “cult.” M'Kinley’s law reports were,
moreover, the unchallengeable bible of the Courts
when in search of Victorian precedents. A more
kindly-disposed and unselfish man never drew
breath on this earth,'and he was a universal
favourite at the Y oriole Club, of which he was a
member. For over 18 months his many friends
had watched with anxiety his visibly ebbing
vitality.
If the Sydney Town and Country's por
trait of Airs. O Shea is to be relied upon, then
Parnell should have got clear of his divorce-suit
by setting up a plea of incurable, rampant, howl-
ing insanity. According to that paper, the Irish
leader threw away his reputation and broke up
his party for the sake of two smudges, a big ink-
blot, a horse-bead, and a badly-disguised bag of
hay—in fact, the Journal’s siren is the sort of
object that any man might reasonably break his
neck getting away from—but when we are asked
to believe that Parnell hung round on cold,
damp nights for the sake of a smile from the
blackened spectre that bursts out on Alfred Ben-
nett’s pictured page, it is too much. If a man did
that sort of thing once he would straightway give
all his money to the Imperial Institute, and then
hang himself on the lady’s door-handle, so that
his remains might surprise her when she came out
next morning. It is the only wav he could get
level.
Friends of Henry George declare that his
recent withdrawal from the Standard is really the
most striking bit of self-sacrifice that has marked
his career. His managing lieutenants, it is said,
had become satisfied that the radical —the work-
ingmen—element ®f the party had withdrawn
from it for good, having lost confidence in Mr.
George and his methods, while on the other hand
it was difficult, with his former reputation as a
Socialistic and revolutionary agitator still cling-
ing to him, to make headway among the “respect-
able ” and well-to do. After a long struggle, as
the story goes, necessitating considerable finesse,
the lieutenants and the inner council of moneyed
men convinced Air. George that lie must retire,
at least for a time. The question of the retention
of one of his sons as a member of the staff, which
brought him to the verge of irritation with the
new proprietor, was at last accounts left unsettled.
This, at least, is the run of talk reported from the
Manhattan Single-tax Club, where there has been
prolonged emotional-conference over Mr. George’s
surrender of his paper, and where it is confidently
held there is still a field open for him in Australia.
The sister of the German Emperor is to
marry the Crown Prince of Italy, and as an
essential preliminary she is to abjure the Pro-
testant faith and become a Catholic. What in
thunder is the good of being that kind of a Pro-
testant, and still more, what is the good of being
that kind of a Catholic?
An election of aldermen took place last
week at Balgowlah—a small town situated some-
where in the vast wilderness where the population
consists chiefly of the oldest inhabitant and the
pound-keeper. When it was over the man at the
top of the poll returned thanks in an eloquent
speech, in which he stated that —“Some people
affect to despise borough councillors, but X can
assure them that they know no more of the glory
of the position than the mole knows of sunshine,
or the serpent of the eagle’s triumphant flight
through the upper air.” Who says that the Aus-
tralian isn’t a man of oratory.

The T. and 0. Journal publishes a de-
pressing narrative about a bullock-driver who has
got into trouble out in the back-blocks. He had
got down to argue with one of his animals and
lay the matter before it in a new light, when the
brute stuck its horn through his lower jaw and
his tongue and part of his head, and then gave a
lift and hung him up by the teeth till they began
to give way beneath the strain. Just when he
was about to fall off, however, and leave his face
suspended on the old heifer’s horn and go on his
way rejoicing without any head or ear or counten-
ance, his mates observed him and lifted him
down. He is now in the hospital, and “tending
to recovery.” It is always the ambition of this
paper to encot rage local industry, and therefore
we are prepared to believe in the horn and the
teeth, subject fo the oidinary trade discount, if
the Journal will knock off the lower jaw and the
hospital. Or, if this arrangement doesn’t suit,
we are willing to place implicit faith in the
hospital and the bullock, provided anybody else
will takeover the bullock-driver and the rest of
the accident; or if tl e Journal will tone down
the assailant into a i lain goat we will take the
lot, even if we have to believe with the assistance
of a steam crane.

Mr. J, W. R. Clarke, the well-known
bookseller of Sydney Markets, issues a circular
complaining of the frequency of “ compositions ”
in the book-trade, where people who undersell
their square and solvent neighbours calmly offer
to their creditors ss. to 10s. in the £. He urges
the wholesale houses to look to the matter.
In their new agreements with “general
labourers” the squatters propose to retain a
week’s wages and 50 per cent, on all further earn-
ings, as a coercive preventive against any labourer
desiring to leave.

They are tearing up the old cemetery of
Marcel, in Paris (now playground of a boys’school)
searching for the bones of Mirabeau. He was
buried in the Pantheon (the first of a legion of
great ones) with much pomp in ’9l. Two years
afterwards papers were discovered showing that
this people’s idol had been bribed by Louis XVI.
and they threw his dishonored bones into a leaden
coffin at Saint Marcel. Now they don’t know
who is who. No leaden coffin can be discovered,
and they’re as puzzled as Dan O’Connor was over
Deniehy’s remains.

The Half-Duck.
TOLD BY MYSELF.
It was my first introduction both to bush life and
to Australia, and my experience of a day during
the earlier part of my career still makes me feel
hot when I think of it. I had an only brother in
New South Wales, and I came out to join him
after we had been parted for some years. We
both had been left a little money ; he had invested
his in a cattle-station, and was doing well when I
landed in Sydney.
Let me premise that I am a woman, and was
then young and, I believe, good-looking, and very,
very verdant. My brother was a bachelor, and
almost immediately after my arrival I went up
country with him to his station and was installed
as housekeeper.
“Jenny, dear,” he said to me after a day or
two had passed, “ I shall be very busy for a few
days, and I want you to remember that out here
we aren’t tightly tied down by strict rules of
etiquette as in England. Possibly somebody will
call while I am out on the run ; if so, mind and
treat them hospitably, ask them to stop to dinner,
tell them I shall be back before evening, and
make them welcome.”
I duly promised.
“Remember,” he said, “you must not judge
men who come here by their dress. Some of them
may be pretty rough in their style, so you must
exercise your feminine tact a little, ” And so Ned
and I parted for the day.
It was very pleasant weather, and the novelty
of my surroundings had not worn off, so the
morning passed quickly.
About eleven o’clock I had a visitor. I was in
the somewhat rude garden, gathering a few
flowers for decorative purposes, when an elderly
man, carrying a roll of blanket over his shoulder,
as a soldier carries his great coat, came up to the
gate and looked over. Two of the dogs com-
menced to bark furiously as though they scented a
natural enemy.
“Beg pardon, Miss,” said the stranger man,'
but is the boss in ?”
“-No, he’s out on the run.”
“ Oh ! I suppose it will be all right for a bit of
tucker ?”
Though the wayfarer spoke in a language literally
unintelligible to me, I at once concluded that he
Bought hospitality. My brother’s instructions
flashed acrois ray mind,, and I immediately con-
cluded that this was one of the rough specimens
he had spoken of.
“ Come in,” I said, as graciously as possible,
and sit down.”
The old follow looked slightly bewildered, but
he opened the gate and followed me. He put his
rolled blanket down by the steps, and, at my in-
vitation, took a seat in one of the cane verandah-
chairs.
He was a remarkably uncouth and dirty speci-
men, and seemed very uncomfortable into the
bargain. I ventured on one or two remarks as to
the weather, to which he only replied, “ Yes,
Mis-.”
Was it too early to offer him any refreshment, I
wondered ? for I was anxious to fulfil my
brother’s wishes to the letter. I therefore went to
the cupboard to get some whisky. On my way I
met Sophy, a girl whom Ned had engaged for my
especial benefit. She was a nat. ve of the district,
and it struck me that she could tell me who my
queer visitor was. “ Just look out in the verandah,
will you, Sophy,” I said, “and tell me if you
know the old gentleman sitting there.”
Sophy put down her broom and went out to in-
vestigate. Presently she returned with a look on
her face impossible to describe. She was a good-
natured girl and desirous of not openly laughing
at me, but the situation was evidently too much
for her. “Old gentleman! Miss Perrier! why
he’s an old sundowner gammoning to look for
work. And you were going to give him Mr.
Perrier’s three-star whisky? Gracious!” And she
had her laugh out.
“ How can I get rid of him, Sophy ? ” I said
quite humbly.
“ My word, I’ll soon clear him out,” she re-
plied, and darting out on the verandah in less
than a minute I saw my late visitor on his way to
the men’s quarters.
Just then a horseman came in sight, and riding
up to the place. “ Who is that, Sophy?” I asked,
feeling rather nervous after my mistake.
“ It looks like young Thompson from the next
station,” returned my handmaiden after due in-
spection.
The new-comer soon put in an appearance on
the yerapdah. He wag a .tall, gawky yputh,
greatly sunburnt, and with very big hands. He
had on a cabbage-tree hat, worn very much at the
back of his head, which hat he, on seeing me, at
once pounced upon and took off; but unfortunately
he had the chin-strap between his incisors at the
time, and forgetting to open his mouth much
extra confusion and embarrassment was experi-
enced by him.
However, I felt on a sure footing this time and
rattled off a "volley of small talk that left the
youth nothing, to dp but opep his mouth in silent,
admiration. I found him as taciturn as the old’
man.
Luncheon-time drew near and I asked my guest
to take possession of my brother’s room to make
his toilette. To tell the truth, the conversation
was getting too one-sided altogether. Sophy put
the lunch on the table and duly rang the' bell, but
Mr. Thompson did not appear. For fully ten
minutes after the gong went great sounds of
scrubbing, splashing, and brushing proceeded
from the insnfficiently-divided'compartment.
After two more rings Mr. Thompson came out.
He had groomed himself to an alarming extent.
A bottle of gun-oil, kept by Ned in his room, had
been pressed into the service, and Mr. Thompson’s
hair was plastered down and combed smooth with
an elaboration that I certainly had not displayed
on my own coiffure.
A roast duck was on the table, and, feeling
quite unequal to the task of carving it, I asked
Mr. Thompson to take the head of the table.
The energetic way in which that silent young
bushman seized on the carving-knife I shall never
forget. In an instant, with one vigorous slash,
the duck lay in two halves on the di&h, and the
next moment one half was in a plate before me.
Now, I had been accustomed to trifle with the
wing of a fowl, and partake perhaps of a slice or
two of the breast, but to be expected to publicly
devour half a duck was rather a. strain upon my
good nature. However, my confrere attacked his
half and I followed the example, presuming that
it was the correct thing to do, and made it appear,
by judicious picking, that I was quite accustomed
to such mid-day repasts.
Presently Sophy brought in the pudding, which
was placed before me. Prompted by the spirit of
mischief, I cut it into two equal parts, and sent
one-half down to Mr. Thompson.
He took it with a look of intense gratitude, and
we finished the meal in the most sociable way.
My guest would not wait for my brother’s
return, and after hearing me talk for some
time prepared to take his departure. We shook
hands very cordially, and he started down the
garden, out of the gate, and I saw him mount and
depart, casting a backward glance as be did so.
Half-an-hour passed rapidly, in laughing over my
late experience; suddenly, to my surprise, Sophy
called out: “There’s Mr. Thompson back again.
What’s he forgotten?”
Sure enough there he was, coming, in a hesitat-
ing, half-hearted way, up the garden.
“ Miss Perrier,” he said, with his eyes examin-
ing the verandah floor, “I suppose you know
(which I didn’t) that the governor is very well off
—one of the warmest men about here ? Why, our
run is nearly alj freehold. When he dies I shall
have it all, but don’t you think I count on that.
He’s a good old boy, who gives me all the
money I want, and I hope he’ll live to a hundred.
But what he wants is to see me spliced. Now ”
(here he became the colour of red-hot beetroot) —
“do you think you will have me ? I’ve seen soirie
nice girls in my time, but never one like you, and
I’d make you a real good husband.”
Was this not a most embarrassing question to
put to an unprotected newchum? I had to
stammer a little before I could reply. Then I
said ; “Really, Mr. Thompson, I suppose I must
consider your offer very flattering, on such a short
acquaintance ; but I don’t think I can say ‘ yes ’
so suddenly. Yon seem to have a very good
nature, and inclined to share everything very
fairly—you gave me half of the duck at dinner.”
(“And you gave me half the pudding,” he mur-
mured) “but I think we had better wait until we
know each other a little better.”
He looked up and down the path, as though
expecting somebody to come to bis assistance,
then shook me again by the hand, and said,
“ Well, you know where to find me, I’m there
always;” and departed.
That was my first offer.

ing solicitor, blurted out, with much virtuous
asperity : “ I wouldn’t come here to tell a lie for
so small an amount.”

Dummyisrri is rife in the Bourke (N.S.W.)
district, the 10,000-aere chances being largely
applied for by boundary-riders, stockmen, bush
labourers, &c., who are bribed by squatters to
block bona-fide selectors by pretendedly taking tip
the available areas. The amount of perjury this
homestead dummying involves would whitewash
Ananias by force of contrast,
A well-known Sydney speculator, who has
just bankrupted, lately found himself, from sheer
force of habit, attending a land-auction. He
hadn’t a copper to jingle against a tombstone.
Eleven acres of ground were put up but nobody
bid. The penniless speculator gave the auctioneer
a start with £4 10s. an acre, whereat the
auctioneer laughed derisively, as the land was
worth at least five or six times as much. This
aroused competition, and the bidding mounted up
to £l9 an acre, where it stuck. Mr. Stonebroke
thereupon offered an advance of half-a-crown.
The auctioneer at first declined to accept so
small an advance, but ultimately knocked down
the area to the sou-less man. The conditions of
sale were—2s per cent, cash; balance, on com-
pletion of deeds. The total price accepted was
£2lO 7s. Cd., and the auctioneer said a deposit of
£5O would do. Stonebroke coolly wrote out a
cheque for £5O, although he hadn’t a cent, at his
credit in any bank. As the bank-doors were
swung open at 10 o’clock next morning, he entered
and interviewed the manager, asking that his
cheque for fifty might be honoured. “Do you
mean to tell me,” asked the astonished banker,
that you have issued a cheque for £5O, and expect
“me to honour it, although the bank is already a
loser in consequence of your insolvency?”
“ That’s exactly the position,” calmly replied the
Busted One—“but I’ve got a good thing on.
After I cracked up last time I paid ray creditors
in full the first rise I made, and I mean to do it
again when I can.” Ultimately the bank-
manager, with whom our friend had had
big transactions in days gone by, let him
have the £5O, saying that if it were not
repaid he (the manager) would have to
suffer the loss personally. Stonebroke imme-
diately set a full gang of unemployed to work to
clear the land at once at £3 an acre. In a few
days, he received an application from the vendors
for the balance of the purchase money, and pro-
mised to pay up in full within a fortnight. The
vendors didn’t object, as they already held £5O.
He hurried on the clearing, and as soon as it was
finished, called on a moneyed acquaintance, led
him out to the property by the ear, and said—“ I
want you to lend me £5OO on this splendid piece
of land.” Which his friend did. Well—“ there
you are !”

A Lesson from the Bee.
Deak Bulletin,— Your article on “ How the
Honey is Shared ” reminds one that the bee ha s
long been cited as an example of the benefits of
monarchism and selfish accumulation. It is now
the turn of the Socialist to draw illustrations
from the economy of this industrious insect.
First, let me knock the Royalty sham endways
with a hard brick of fact. The so-called “queen
bee is no monarch at all—inasmuch as she neither
grabs the taxes, nor declares war, nor rears a
gluttonous, unwholesome - looking “Royal”
family at the cost of the State, nor signs death-
warrants; she is not even a Defender of the
Faith, and has no hereditary rights or
privileges whatever. She is reared from
an egg that would, under ordinary cir-
cumstances have produced a common, plain
worker, but being provided with special food and
a roomy cell she has been enabled to develope—
entirely at the will of the community—into a egg-
layer. She is simply a mother, and directly she
becomes unfitted for her functions she is merci-
lessly slain and replaced by a young and vigorous
bee. But if she were a monarch and possessed
of all the grim powers and absurd privileges
of the by-the-grace-of-God article, the fact that
her life is twenty times the duration of the
ordinary bee would enable her to assume the posi-
tion with more show of reason than our Kaisers
and Victorias possess. If these had a lifetime
proportionate in length and could show that they
had made good use of it, respect and reverence
would naturally follow ; but the possibility
of the average monarch developing any in-
telligence or usefulness whatever even in
a thousand years is so unlikely that the
parallel may safely be dropped. The phase of
bee-life that the Socialist may most profitably
study is that relating to the drones. Strictly
speaking, one drone only is required for the pro-
pagation of the race, but the bees like a big
margin and raise a few hundreds to make a sure
thing of it. They build drone-cells, however,
for two distinct purposes : first' for the breeding
of these drones, and second for storing honey
under certain circumstances; and here is Just
where a ponderous moral lesson crops up. When
nectar is very abundant the thoughtless insects,
pushed for time and too greedy to pass by what
they do not require, run up drone-cells
for storage purposes as being more quickly
constructed than worker - cells ; but when
these in turn are emptied the queen
lays eggs in them —which, of course, develope
into drones. Thus, the very foundations of the
State, laid in greed, become a permanent source
of trouble; the ingredients that, rightly ad-
ministered, would produce workers, yield breeding-
places for privileged idlers. And although when
stores run short and starvation is imminent the
drones are thrown out, still the relief is only
temporary; the drone-cells are there, and fresh
drones are reared ; and the placid air with which
these loafers eat up the food that the workers are
slaving to obtain is keenly suggestive of what is
glibly styled “the relation of Capital to Labour.”
It is, moreover, a somewhat depressing fact that
though the workers periodically make a clean
sweep of the drones, the conditions for producing
a recurrence of the evil are allowed to remain.
Certainly the bee-keeper, perceiving the state of
affairs, may effectually remedy matters by
removing the drone-cells and substituting
worker - comb; but this is, akin to a
special Providence —and the connection just here
becomes vague. Philosophy says that man is
far superior to the animals in that he has the
choice to rise or fall; but when we can see new
States arising where man is free to build for
workers only, but ends in making provision for
an unlimited supply of idlers, it would seem that
mankind, on the whole, regards falling as the
easier mode of motion.
THE CROW-EATER.
Pall Mall Gazette speaks of “ Hobart,
Victoria.” •
Maoris call the N.Z. Parliament the
“ Korerorium.” Properly pronounced, it sounds
like the escape from a blast-furnace.
Kaiser Wilhelm, in ordering two soldiers
who had been kept out of promotion because they
were Hebrews, to be decorated with stripes, re-
marked— “ I know neither Jews nor Christians in
my army; I know only soldiers.”
Mrs. John O’Keefe, who still flourishes at
Newtown, near Albury, is a lady who has done
her level best to render assisted immigration to
Australia unnecessary. Born at Cork, Ireland,
in 1818, Mrs. O’Keefe arrived in N.S. Wales in
’4l, and is the mother of 17 children, 12 of
whom are still living; grandmother of 139, and
great-grandmother of 42.
The celebrated G.B.D. brand of pipes not
only maintain their high position, but are advanc-
ing with giant strides in public favour. The
manufactory is the largest in the world, and its
sales are at least three times that of any other.
No higher testimony to the superiority of G.B.D.
goods can be mentioned than that they have
obtained the highest award at all Exhibitions, a
record without precedent. Messrs. Julius Gold-
schmidt and Co., are the wholesale agents, 231
Clarence-street, Sydney.
A former Town Clerk of Bathurst, on
retiring from his municipal billet, said to a certain
alderman (who publicly told the yarn); “John,
old man, I have robbed the Council of £260, but 1
defy anyone to show how it was done or to find
me out.” Bathurst has been improving since. Its
Corporation finds itself debited with an overdraft
of over £12,000, whereas it has been placidly
jogging along under the impression that the bank
limit was only £lO,OOO. No ledger has been kept
since October, 1889. Aldermen exonerate and
hold immaculate the present popular Town Clerk,
who has “ kept all the books as he found them
when he entered office.”
A witty rascal, one Duval, of Baltimore
(U.S.A.), has duped many U.S. senators. Pro-
curing somehow a stock of official baptismal
forms, he sent each senator a letter (with bap-
tismal certificate attached as proof) to the effect
that in reverential admiration of the said senator
he had named his son after him, and fervently
hoped the baby would, when grown to manhood,
strive to become as illustrious in the service of the
nation as his namesake. Flattered and proud,
most of the senators sent some present to Duval’s
address, which, by the way, was a drinking
saloon where he called for letters, and the swindler
raked in gold and silver mugs and dollar-bills
galore. Duval is a discharged post-office employe
and the polynomial baby a pure myth.
“The Briton’s Land.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the December number of the Journal
of the Royal Colonial Institute a vociferously
patriotic lawyer cf Melbourne named Dennistoun
Wood rises to ask in a deep, hollow tone about the
exact location of “ The Briton’s land.” He starts
by throwing the following statement off his chest:
Should German, Russ, or Frank demand what country is
the Briton’s land,
As no vain-glorious boast would I to that inquiry thus
reply:
Looking over the reply it appears that the Briton
owns pretty nearly all the earth, and that those
few unconsidered patches which he hasn’t got are
not worth counting. In fact, Mr. Wood’s
geography is built on the same pattern as that of
Jules Verne’s little nigger, who started his lesson
thus : “France, belonging to the English, capital
Calais, where the Governor, Lord Napoleon,
resides; Spain, belonging to the English, capita*
I Gibraltar.” However, this is a mere detail. The
really important point is that the bard from
Yarra’s dead-oat-strewn river has quite forgotten
to mention how the Briton got so much land. The
brief explanation which follows may enlighten
him on this matter ;
The Briton’s Land.
Should any grovelling wretch demand
What country is the Briton’s land,
And any brute whose soul is dead
Cast low reflections at his head,
We answer that he always got
All he could get—he took the lot;
He jumped the land, he boned the gold,
He snavelled all that he could hold,
And from the Poles to far Cathay
He faked things in a Christian way ;
And every article of worth
He sneaked from every tribe on earth,
And everything on earth he took
Save one —he never took his hook ;
And every place that came to hand
That was the Briton’s promised land.
Where’er the nigger asked for rum
There did his cherished Scripture hum,
And where brass gods were in demand
That was the Briton’s favoured land,
And 10 per cent, reduction he
Allowed upon a quantity;
And where the black inhabitants
Were very badly off for pants,
He came— and cleaned the pagan out,
And put his country up the spout;
Where’er men wanted guns on trust,
He sold them guns that always bust,
And by way .of security
He grabbed the earth, and stole the sea;
And when he found the tribes at strife,
He bore to them the Bread of Life.
And to each heathen he did preach,
And sold a rusted axe to each.
And when they caved each other in,
And each man stole the other’s gin,
And cut each other’s uncle up,
And used his skull for a drinking-cup,
And the tribes grew thin on the blood-stained
strand—
That, too, became the Briton's land.
He spread the Gospel near and far
(He was not too particular,
But he always took the Word along—
On this one point he came out strong),
He lifted up the pagan’s eyes
To mansions that are in the skies,
And then he knocked him on the head—
That heathen aforementioned ;j
Or shot him on some dusty plain,
Or hove him in the rolling main,
Or smashed him in a general way,
Then over his remains did pray
That, at the resurrection, he
Might live again by the Jasper sea,
And wander round amid the flowers
That blossom in Valhalla’s bowers,
And sing an everlasting song
The groves of Paradise among.
Or sometimes he put dynamite
Beneath that “brudder ” in the night,
And scattered him like grains of sand,
And his fields became the Briton’s land.
But now and then it chanced he fell
Upon some warlike infidel —
Some unregenerate son of gore—
Some mail-clad fiend on a lonely shore,
And to him there came a sadder fate
For that fiend became a syndicate,
And the Briton taught him how to be
A Loan and Mortgage'Company ;
And in his fastnesses and hills
He fooled around with notes and bills,
And the infidel was floated then
In shares paid up to one-pound-ten,
And eighteenpenny calls fell due
Unfailingly, each week or two,
Till the Buddhist’s clothes went up the spout,
And the bailiff sold the Moslem out,
And the Parsee saw his rose-strewn vale
Foreclosed beneath a bill of sale,
And the Brahmin looked from his mortgaged
towers
At the execution within his bowers,
And a lien was on the Sacred Cow,
And they all were broke—-they knew not how ;
Then they fell like leaves on the arid strand,
And their plains became the Briton’s land.
And thus it was the Briton spread
On many an isle far sundered ;
And still the pagan wants to know
Where the lands of his swindled fathers go,
And how they swapped for a rusted gun
The fairest vales beneath the sun,
And why he pays an endless tax
Because his uncle got an axe;
And how, in a general sort of way,
Things are as they seem to be to-day,
And wherefore the oily preachers tell
That the nigger who steals makes tracks for hell;
And, in short, he fails to understand
That the earth should be all the Briton’s land.

The constant danger that menaces the
lightning express coaches of Cobb and Co. in the
Western districts of Queensland is their being
overtaken on the track and run into by slow*
going bullock-teams. To avoid this it is recom-
mended that the coaches should wear signal-lights
behind.

What folly it would be to cut grass with a pair of scissors! Yet peo-
ple do equally silly things every day. Modern progress has grown up
from the hooked sickle to the swinging scythe and thence to the lawn
mower. So don’t use scissors!
But do you use SAPOLIO ? If you don’t you are as much behind the
age as if you cut grass with a dinner knife. Once there were no soaps.
Then one soap served all purposes. Now the sensible folks use one soap
in the toilet, another in the tub, one soap in the stables, and SAPOLIO
for all scouring and house-cleaning.

The democratic Melbourne Age always
speaks of “Capital and Labour” —puts the cart
before the horse—the dispensable before the indis-
pensable.
Two men, who assisted Constable Bevan
to recover the body of Edward Loch, drowned
while trying to cross the flooded Mary River,
annexed and divided four shillings which fell from
the corpse’s pockets, claiming the same as
legitimate payment for “lost time.”
James Blackley claims to be the “ ringer”
of the Bourke district wool-sheds this year. On
the Dunlop station, where 200,000 sheep were
shorn, Blackley sheared 212 jumbucks during a
working day of eight hours. In forty hours he
sheared 338, being at the rate of 2g minutes for
each sheep.
The new Mayor of Brisbane is “Blue-
Ribbon” Clark, a snip by trade and a “social
reformer ” by “special mission.” Toast-and-water
banquets will now be the Aldermanic recreation,
and when Clark gives “a nop” the refreshments
will be limited to coffee unroyal and pop without
a stick in it.
Mrs. O’Shea is described as having the
thin lips and bulging brow we see on the Cleopatra
medals, and as being as cold-blooded ordinarily as
Cleopatra was known to be. She is not a very tall
or big-boned woman, but is ruddy and full-
blooded ; her hair is blonde and profuse, and her
complexion is fair.
An agitation is begun again to introduce
cadets into the Victorian Police instead of finding
officers from the ranks of the force. The plan is
too thin—the idea being that the police are too
much in sympathy with the proletariat, viewing
the contingency of strike riots, wherein citizen
soldiers declare they will not take up arms against
the People. -
Yates, in the World, denies that Sir J. E.
Millais, artist, was ever in Australia, and he ought
to know. The current statement is that Millais
was working on the Bendigo goldfield somewhere
about the time that Premier Salisbury visited it,
this latter fact being well enough certified. Salis-
bury was in Melbourne and Bendigo about 1852,
and, now Childers and Lowe have retired, no
other English forward statesman has a like ex-
perience.
When the Gordon Gumming slander cases
come on for trial we suppose that “ Wales ” will
be permitted to asseverate his say-so “on my
honour as a gentleman,” instead of being required
to “ kiss the book ” after the manner of common
mortality. He has gone through this farce before,
when he was placed in a position in which every
“real gentleman” would brazenly tell a lie in
order to save some of the reputation of a lady.
Editor Bulletin, —We do not think
the health-giving properties of the banana are
appreciated by the fruit-eating public, who merely
look upon it as a cheap edible when other fruits
are scarce or out of season. Cut in slices, the
banana makes an excellent salad served with
mayonnaise dressing; curried, it is far more palat-
able than either fish or meat; boiled as a veget-
able, while green, in the skin, for three hours, and
peeled before sending it to table, it will be found
excellent. It also makes an extremely delicious
pudding for the nursery, served in a pie-dish,
covered with sweet milk and some sugar and
baked for an hour in a moderately quick oven.
Many children in Fiji can with truth assert in
regard to the banana—“His is wot I growed on.”
A most agreeable breakfast-dish is to be made by
frying slices of bacon, and then slices of banana in
the hot bacon fat, with plenty of pepper, and
serving together as one does a dish of bacon and
eggs. These are a few -of the many ways of
utilising this most accessible fruit for both rich
and poor.—Yours faithfully, O’Kelly & Brown,
banana-growers, Nanua River, Fiji, 16—1—’91,
FREETRADE OR PROTECTION.
Some vote Protection, some Freetrade,
As best for this young country’s weal.
But ask the youth or blushing maid,
And each will answer— Pemell’s Meal
As best, because it has been tried,
There’s nothing half so good beside.
His Golden Meal is what I mean,
'Tis good and pure and cheap, I wean.
Gladstone’s R.O, Disability Bill, to enable
Catholics to be appointed to the Lord Lieutenancy
and the Lord Chancellorship of Ireland, has been
defeated in the Commons by a majority of 33.
And yet there have been R.O. Viceroys of India.
Sir T. Cockburn-Campbell, one of the
W.A. delegates to London in connection with the
recent legislation granting a constitution to that
province, has been appointed President of the
nominated Legislative Council, conditionally on
his relinquishing his connection with the local
press. This condition has been complied with,
and Mr. Hackett now edits the West Austra-
lian.
Canada, The Bulletin predicts, will not
be permitted to purchase Alaska : the sentiment
of the Republic would be outraged by the sale of
a single inch of territory.
Says Sydney Echo : —
Ona of the most satisfactory signs of the present time,
however, is that the colonies are slowly and gradually,
perhaps, but surely, uniting, without waiting for the
appearance of the outside foe; and, as a proof that this
is a spontaneous and genuine movement on the part of
the people, we find that the anniversary of the first
settlement is being celebrated in Melbourne with Cale-
donian sports and other demonstrations.
Editor Stronach’s entertaining non sequitur is
based on the deplorable fact that he is himself a
pure-bred Scotchman.
Says Froude, in his monograph on Bea-
consfield:—
Disraeli failed, as he deserved to fail. He thought that
he was reviving patriotic enthusiasm, and all that he did
was to create jingoism. ... No public man in Eng-
land ever rose so higli and acquired power so great, so
little of whose work has survived him. . . . Possibly
if among his other gifts lie had inherited an English
character, he might have devoted himself more completely
to great national questions ; he might even haveinseribed
his name in the great roll of English worthies. But he
was English only by adoption, and he never completely
identified himself with the country which he ruled. At
heart lie was a Hebrew to the end.
Dora Femi’s history was peculiar. She
was one of our pioneeresses and Cockatoo peeresses,
such as they are. About a century ago in London
she was famous as a ladies’ nurse and lying-in-
ordinary woman amid the Peerage, but her little
game was to plunder right and left, Until at last
she got at the gold watch, diamonded in 29 holes,
of a Marquis of Carabas, besides a magnificently-
jewelled order of his, worth £600. She was
wheedled out of the latter, on a promise that there
was to be no prosecution, after which she went to
what Squeers spells as Bot-tin-ey, and married A-
convict who became a very rich squatter.
Fronde’s “Disraeli” glozes over Dizzy’s
worst mistake, when he stole holus-bolus. General
Foy’s eulogium of General Gourion St. Cyr for
his epithalamium, in the House of Commons, on
the Duke of Wellington. This was unpardonable,
an index to the essential hollowness of the man,
his charlatanry, never to be effaced by all his
ability. But the speech was undeniably the best
of the occasion, and Dizzy swallowed all the com-
pliments —just as did a now-deceased N.S.W.
politician who had the cheek to pronounce in
public an original panegyric on Garibaldi, stolen
word for word from the Masonic burial-service.
Attorney-General Clarke (Tasmania) has
drafted an Australian Federal Constitution on
the Yankee model. Clarke proposes a British
Governor-General (say a Royal Prince, to speedily
disgust us into a Republic), a Senate and House
of Representatives, then provincial legislatures
which shall elect their own provincial or Lieut.-
Governors. Federal, as distinguished from
provincial powers are defined, but it would be
premature to discuss their details. The world
now waits for Premier Griffith, of Queensland, to
expose his plans and specifications, which may or
may not be partially foreshadowed by his already
unfolded local decentralization antidote to tri-
partite territorial separation. Mr. Clarke, while
preferring the United States as a model for Aus-
tralian unity, thinks that Canada is “too
advanced for the colonies !”
There are two sides to the question now in
dispute at Sydney School of Arts, as to whether
women shall or shall not be admitted to full
membership at half rates. On the one hand,
women in all vocations of life are only paid half
the wages demanded by men, and are, therefore,
only able to pay a half-share of the cost of leisured
luxuries. This is due doubtlessly to their own
helplessness, but all the same the men who use
female labour loaf on the women they employ to
the extent of half the current rate of male wages.
When, as The Bulletin advocates, the work
done by every competent female employe is paid
for at the same rate as that of a similarly com-
petent male, lovely woman may be fairly asked to
put up her fair share of general expenses for
general privileges. On the other side of the ledger
is the undoubted fact that the average lady
member of the School of Arts gives twice as much
trouble as the average male, and, in the light of
services rendered, ought to be charged double.
We leave the subject at that, with the prophecy
that the women will “get there all the same.”

To H. F. Kugelmann, Esq.
Dear Sir -This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but I became worse every day, wasting away' until
* oecame a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you. and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in “March last. At the
end of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vormted about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaming in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Peter Smythe, J.p.
